The First Doubt: God's Generosity Implies Eternal World
First of three arguments for the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument for the eternity of the world based on God's essential generosity and its implications.

Core Idea
If God is generous by essence, His generosity is eternal, so the world must be eternal.

This lesson presents the first doubt attributed to Proclus: that God's generosity, being essential and eternal, necessitates the world's eternity. The argument relies on the assumption that the cause of the world is God's generosity, which cannot change or be impeded.

Guided Path
1
Read the first doubt passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how each premise is stated.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the three premises
The argument has three key claims: (1) God is generous by essence, (2) generosity is the cause of the world, (3) generosity is eternal. Trace how each is supported.
The third premise is supported by denying any impediment.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the premises, the conclusion is that the world must be eternal. Ask yourself why each premise is questionable from a theological perspective.
The author will later challenge the meaning of 'generosity'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة من شبه برقلس، قال الباري سبحانه جواد بذاته، وعلة العالم جوده، وجوده قديم لم يزل، فيلزم أن يكون وجود العالم قديما لم يزل. قال: ولا يجوز أن يكون مرة جوادًا ومرة غير جواد، فإنه يوجب التغير في ذاته. قال: ولا مانع من فيض جوده، إذ لو كان مانع لما كان من جوده؛ إذ المانع الذاتي مانع أبدا، وقد تحقق الجود بإيجاد الموجود فهو خلف، ولو كان المانع من غيره كان الغير هو الحامل لواجب الوجود، وواجب الوجود لا يحمل على شيء ولا يمنع من شيء.

شبهة أخرى تقارب هذه الشبهة، قال: ليس يخلو الصانع من أن يكون لم يزل صانعا بالفعل، أو لم يزل صانعا بالقوة، فإن كان الأول فالمصنوع معلول لم يزل، وإن كان الثاني فما بالقوة لا يخرج إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، ومخرج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل غير ذات الشيء، فيجب أن تتغير ذات الصانع لمغير وذلك باطل.

شبهة أخرى قال: كل علة لا يجوز عليها التحرك والاستحالة، فإنما تكون من جهة ذاتها، لا من جهة الانتقال من غيرها، وكل علة من جهة ذاتها فمعلولها من جهة ذاتها، وإذا كانت ذاتا لم تزل فمعلولها لم يزل.

والجواب: قلنا: ما المعنى بقولك: الباري تعالى جواد بذاته؟ وما معنى الجود؟ فإن عندنا وعندكم الجودة ليس صفة ذاتية زايدة على الذات، بل هو صفة فعلية، والصفات عندكم إما أن تكون سلوبًا كالقديم والغنى، فإن معنى القديم نفي الأولية، ومعنى الغنى نفي الحاجة، وإما أن تكون إضافات كالخالق والرازق على اصطلاحنا والمبدئ، والعلة الأولى على اصطلاحكم وليس الله صفة وراء هذين المعنيين، فالجود من قسم الإضافة لا من قسم السلب، فلا فرق إذا بين معنى المبدئ وبين معنى الجود، ومعناهما الفاعل الصانع، فكأنكم قلتم: صانع بذاته، وهو محل النزاع ومصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن الخصم يقول: ليس فاعلا لذاته، وفعله ليس قديما لم يزل، وفيه وقع الخلاف غيرت العبارة من الفعل إلى الجود وجعلته دليل المسألة، وإذا كان معنى الجود راجع إلى الفعل والإيجاد فقوله: مرة هو جواد ومرة
A doubt from the doubts of Proclus: He said: The Creator, exalted, is generous by His essence, and the cause of the world is His generosity, and His generosity is eternal never ceasing, so it follows that the existence of the world is eternal never ceasing. He said: It is not permissible that He be sometimes generous and sometimes not generous, for that would necessitate change in His essence. He said: And there is no impediment to the emanation of His generosity, for if there were an impediment it would not be from His generosity; for the essential impediment is an impediment forever, and generosity has been realized by bringing the existent into existence, so that is a contradiction. And if the impediment were from another, then that other would be the bearer for the Necessary Existent, and the Necessary Existent is not borne by anything nor impeded by anything.

Another doubt approximating this doubt: He said: The Maker is not free from either being always an actual maker or always a potential maker. If the first, then the made is an effect that has never ceased. If the second, then what is potential does not emerge into actuality except by an extraneous cause, and the extraneous cause of a thing from potentiality to actuality is other than the essence of the thing, so it would be necessary that the essence of the Maker change due to a changer, and that is false.

Another doubt: He said: Every cause that does not admit of motion and alteration exists only from the aspect of its essence, not from the aspect of transition from something else. And every cause that is from the aspect of its essence has its effect from the aspect of its essence. And if it is an essence that has never ceased, then its effect has never ceased.

And the answer: We say: What do you mean by your statement: the Creator, exalted, is generous by His essence? And what is the meaning of generosity? For according to us and according to you, generosity is not an essential attribute superadded to the essence, but rather it is an attribute of action. And attributes according to you are either negations, like the Ancient and the Self-Sufficient—for the meaning of 'Ancient' is negation of priority, and the meaning of 'Self-Sufficient' is negation of need—or relations, like the Creator and the Provider according to our terminology, and the Originator and the First Cause according to your terminology. And there is no attribute of God beyond these two meanings. So generosity belongs to the category of relation, not negation. Thus there is no difference between the meaning of 'Originator' and the meaning of 'generosity'; both mean the efficient, the maker. So it is as if you said: 'maker by His essence,' and that is the very point of dispute and a petition of the first principle. For the adversary says: He is not an agent by His essence, and His act is not eternal. In that lies the disagreement. You changed the expression from 'act' to 'generosity' and made it the proof of the matter. And if the meaning of 'generosity' goes back to act and bringing into existence, then his saying: 'He is sometimes generous and sometimes not generous...'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (shubahāt) for the eternity of the world, attributed to Proclus and similar thinkers, followed by the beginning of the author's response. The first argument: God is essentially generous (jawād bi-dhātih), His generosity is the cause of the world, and since His generosity is eternal, the world must be eternal. God cannot be sometimes generous and sometimes not, as that would imply change in His essence. There is no impediment to the outpouring of His generosity; an essential impediment would be permanent (contradicting His generosity), and an external impediment would require something to bear or constrain God, which is impossible. The second argument: The Maker is either eternally actual or eternal potential. If actual, the effect co-exists eternally; if potential, a cause external to His essence is needed to actualize the potential, which would entail change in His essence. The third argument: A cause that is immutable is a cause by essence, so its effect is also by essence and thus eternal. The answer begins by questioning the meaning of 'generous by essence.' The author argues that generosity is not an essential attribute but an attribute of action; attributes are either negations (like qadīm, ghani) or relations (like khāliq, rāziq). Generosity is a relational attribute, synonymous with 'Originator' or 'First Cause', meaning simply 'agent' or 'maker'. Thus the objection merely restates the point in dispute (that God is agent by essence) without proof—a begging the question. The answer is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms
جواد بذاته
جود
علة
فيض
مانع ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's generosity being essential means it is an attribute like knowledge, and thus the world must be eternal.
Correction: The author will later argue that 'generosity' is not an essential attribute but a relational one referring to action, so the argument begs the question.

Checkpoint
What does the first doubt conclude about the existence of the world?
A) The world is created in time
B) The world is eternal
C) The world is contingent
D) The world is an illusion

Key Takeaway
The first doubt argues that God's essential generosity necessitates an eternal world, but the author will challenge the meaning of 'generosity'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own life, have you ever assumed that God's generosity means He should give you what you want immediately? How does Proclus' argument—that God's generosity must lead to eternal creation—mirror your own impatience with God's timing, and what does that reveal about your understanding of God's actual generosity?
?
Proclus says that if God were sometimes generous and sometimes not, it would imply change in His essence. Consider your own generosity: are you consistent in giving? What does your inconsistency say about your character, and how does God's unchanging generosity expose your need for His grace to become more like Him?
?
The doubt claims no impediment can stop God's generosity. Do you truly believe that God's generosity is actively flowing toward you right now, or do you feel blocked? If you sense a barrier, what is it—and could it be that the real impediment is not external but your own unbelief or ingratitude? What concrete step can you take today to remove that barrier?

The Second Doubt: Actual vs. Potential Maker
Second of three arguments for the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the eternity of the world based on the dilemma of the Maker being actual or potential.

Core Idea
If God is always actual, the world is eternal; if potential, change is implied, which is impossible.

This lesson presents the second doubt: whether the Maker has always been actual or potential. Both alternatives lead to conclusions that conflict with the Islamic view of creation in time. The argument forces a choice that seems to leave no room for a temporal beginning of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the second doubt passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two alternatives presented.
The argument is a dilemma (two horns).
2
Analyze each horn
First horn: if always actual, the effect (world) is eternal. Second horn: if potential, a cause is needed to actualize, leading to change in God. Both seem problematic.
Consider what assumptions the argument makes about God's nature.
3
Identify the conclusion
The argument claims that the only way to avoid change is to accept that God is always actual, hence the world is eternal.
This is a direct challenge to creation ex nihilo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة من شبه برقلس، قال الباري سبحانه جواد بذاته، وعلة العالم جوده، وجوده قديم لم يزل، فيلزم أن يكون وجود العالم قديما لم يزل. قال: ولا يجوز أن يكون مرة جوادًا ومرة غير جواد، فإنه يوجب التغير في ذاته. قال: ولا مانع من فيض جوده، إذ لو كان مانع لما كان من جوده؛ إذ المانع الذاتي مانع أبدا، وقد تحقق الجود بإيجاد الموجود فهو خلف، ولو كان المانع من غيره كان الغير هو الحامل لواجب الوجود، وواجب الوجود لا يحمل على شيء ولا يمنع من شيء.

شبهة أخرى تقارب هذه الشبهة، قال: ليس يخلو الصانع من أن يكون لم يزل صانعا بالفعل، أو لم يزل صانعا بالقوة، فإن كان الأول فالمصنوع معلول لم يزل، وإن كان الثاني فما بالقوة لا يخرج إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، ومخرج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل غير ذات الشيء، فيجب أن تتغير ذات الصانع لمغير وذلك باطل.

شبهة أخرى قال: كل علة لا يجوز عليها التحرك والاستحالة، فإنما تكون من جهة ذاتها، لا من جهة الانتقال من غيرها، وكل علة من جهة ذاتها فمعلولها من جهة ذاتها، وإذا كانت ذاتا لم تزل فمعلولها لم يزل.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could change from potential to actual without an external cause.
Correction: The argument assumes that any change from potentiality to actuality requires an external cause, which would be impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the second doubt, what is the consequence if the Maker is always an actual maker?
A) The world is created in time
B) The world is eternal
C) The Maker must change
D) The Maker is potential

Key Takeaway
The second doubt forces a choice: either the world is eternal or God changes—both unacceptable to the adversary, but the eternalist uses it to support pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is always actual and perfect, why do you sometimes act as if God is not fully present or active in your circumstances? What specific area of your life reveals a hidden assumption that God's power is potential rather than actual? Take a moment to confess any doubt that God's action in your life is incomplete.
?
Consider the phrase 'ما بالقوة لا يخرج إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج' (what is potential does not emerge into actuality except by an extraneous cause). In your spiritual life, are you waiting for some external circumstance to 'activate' your faith or obedience, as if God's grace is not already fully available? What change in your heart is needed to stop treating God as a potential helper and start trusting Him as already actual?
?
The argument says that if God were potential, His essence would have to change. Do you ever subconsciously imagine that God needs to adjust or 'update' His plans based on your actions? Repent of any belief that God is waiting for you to move first, and commit to resting in the reality that God's will for you is already perfect and active.

The Third Doubt: Causation by Essence
Third of three arguments for the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the eternity of the world based on the nature of an immutable cause.

Core Idea
An immutable cause by essence produces its effect eternally.

This lesson covers the third doubt: any cause that is immutable (cannot change) must be a cause by its essence, not by any external factor. And if it is a cause by essence, its effect must also be essential—meaning eternal. This argument directly links God's immutability to the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the third doubt passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument is succinct but powerful.
It uses the concept of 'cause by essence' versus 'cause by accident'.
2
Understand the key premise
God is immutable (no motion or change). Therefore, He is a cause by His essence, not by any transition or external factor.
This is based on the idea that if a cause changes, its effect might be temporary.
3
Draw the implication
If God is a cause by essence, then the effect (the world) is also by essence—hence inseparable and eternal.
This challenges the doctrine of temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة من شبه برقلس، قال الباري سبحانه جواد بذاته، وعلة العالم جوده، وجوده قديم لم يزل، فيلزم أن يكون وجود العالم قديما لم يزل. قال: ولا يجوز أن يكون مرة جوادًا ومرة غير جواد، فإنه يوجب التغير في ذاته. قال: ولا مانع من فيض جوده، إذ لو كان مانع لما كان من جوده؛ إذ المانع الذاتي مانع أبدا، وقد تحقق الجود بإيجاد الموجود فهو خلف، ولو كان المانع من غيره كان الغير هو الحامل لواجب الوجود، وواجب الوجود لا يحمل على شيء ولا يمنع من شيء.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can be a cause without producing an eternal effect if He chooses to create in time.
Correction: The argument assumes that if the cause is essential, the effect must be inseparable and co-eternal.

Checkpoint
What does the third doubt conclude about the world?
A) The world is created from nothing
B) The world is eternal
C) The world is contingent
D) The world is an illusion

Key Takeaway
The third doubt contends that an immutable, essential cause must have an eternal effect, directly opposing temporal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Answer: Redefining Generosity as a Relational Attribute
Partial answer refuting the first doubt by analyzing the concept of generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the first doubt by analyzing the meaning of 'generosity' and classifying it as a relational attribute.

Core Idea
Generosity is not an essential attribute but a relational one, synonymous with 'maker', so the argument begs the question.

The author begins his answer by questioning the definition of 'generosity' (jūd). He argues that according to both parties, attributes are either negations (like 'Ancient') or relations (like 'Creator'). Generosity falls under relations, not essential attributes. Thus saying 'God is generous by essence' is equivalent to saying 'God is maker by essence'—which is exactly the point in dispute. The argument thus becomes a circular reasoning (petitio principii).

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author deconstructs the term 'generosity'.
This is a crucial methodological move in kalam.
2
Understand the classification of attributes
The author divides attributes into two categories: negations (salabiyya) and relations (idāfiyya). Give examples from the text.
For example, 'Ancient' negates priority, 'Creator' denotes a relation to creation.
3
See how 'generosity' is categorized
The author argues that 'generosity' is a relational attribute, meaning the same as 'originator' or 'maker'.
Therefore, the phrase 'generous by essence' reduces to 'maker by essence'.
4
Identify the fallacy
The original argument uses 'generosity' to prove the world's eternity, but it simply assumes that God is an eternal maker. That is the point of contention.
This is called 'begging the question' (muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة من شبه برقلس، قال الباري سبحانه جواد بذاته، وعلة العالم جوده، وجوده قديم لم يزل، فيلزم أن يكون وجود العالم قديما لم يزل. قال: ولا يجوز أن يكون مرة جوادًا ومرة غير جواد، فإنه يوجب التغير في ذاته. قال: ولا مانع من فيض جوده، إذ لو كان مانع لما كان من جوده؛ إذ المانع الذاتي مانع أبدا، وقد تحقق الجود بإيجاد الموجود فهو خلف، ولو كان المانع من غيره كان الغير هو الحامل لواجب الوجود، وواجب الوجود لا يحمل على شيء ولا يمنع من شيء.

شبهة أخرى تقارب هذه الشبهة، قال: ليس يخلو الصانع من أن يكون لم يزل صانعا بالفعل، أو لم يزل صانعا بالقوة، فإن كان الأول فالمصنوع معلول لم يزل، وإن كان الثاني فما بالقوة لا يخرج إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، ومخرج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل غير ذات الشيء، فيجب أن تتغير ذات الصانع لمغير وذلك باطل.

شبهة أخرى قال: كل علة لا يجوز عليها التحرك والاستحالة، فإنما تكون من جهة ذاتها، لا من جهة الانتقال من غيرها، وكل علة من جهة ذاتها فمعلولها من جهة ذاتها، وإذا كانت ذاتا لم تزل فمعلولها لم يزل.

والجواب: قلنا: ما المعنى بقولك: الباري تعالى جواد بذاته؟ وما معنى الجود؟ فإن عندنا وعندكم الجودة ليس صفة ذاتية زايدة على الذات، بل هو صفة فعلية، والصفات عندكم إما أن تكون سلوبًا كالقديم والغنى، فإن معنى القديم نفي الأولية، ومعنى الغنى نفي الحاجة، وإما أن تكون إضافات كالخالق والرازق على اصطلاحنا والمبدئ، والعلة الأولى على اصطلاحكم وليس الله صفة وراء هذين المعنيين، فالجود من قسم الإضافة لا من قسم السلب، فلا فرق إذا بين معنى المبدئ وبين معنى الجود، ومعناهما الفاعل الصانع، فكأنكم قلتم: صانع بذاته، وهو محل النزاع ومصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن الخصم يقول: ليس فاعلا لذاته، وفعله ليس قديما لم يزل، وفيه وقع الخلاف غيرت العبارة من الفعل إلى الجود وجعلته دليل المسألة، وإذا كان معنى الجود راجع إلى الفعل والإيجاد فقوله: مرة هو جواد ومرة
A doubt from the doubts of Proclus: He said: The Creator, exalted, is generous by His essence, and the cause of the world is His generosity, and His generosity is eternal never ceasing, so it follows that the existence of the world is eternal never ceasing. He said: It is not permissible that He be sometimes generous and sometimes not generous, for that would necessitate change in His essence. He said: And there is no impediment to the emanation of His generosity, for if there were an impediment it would not be from His generosity; for the essential impediment is an impediment forever, and generosity has been realized by bringing the existent into existence, so that is a contradiction. And if the impediment were from another, then that other would be the bearer for the Necessary Existent, and the Necessary Existent is not borne by anything nor impeded by anything.

Another doubt approximating this doubt: He said: The Maker is not free from either being always an actual maker or always a potential maker. If the first, then the made is an effect that has never ceased. If the second, then what is potential does not emerge into actuality except by an extraneous cause, and the extraneous cause of a thing from potentiality to actuality is other than the essence of the thing, so it would be necessary that the essence of the Maker change due to a changer, and that is false.

Another doubt: He said: Every cause that does not admit of motion and alteration exists only from the aspect of its essence, not from the aspect of transition from something else. And every cause that is from the aspect of its essence has its effect from the aspect of its essence. And if it is an essence that has never ceased, then its effect has never ceased.

And the answer: We say: What do you mean by your statement: the Creator, exalted, is generous by His essence? And what is the meaning of generosity? For according to us and according to you, generosity is not an essential attribute superadded to the essence, but rather it is an attribute of action. And attributes according to you are either negations, like the Ancient and the Self-Sufficient—for the meaning of 'Ancient' is negation of priority, and the meaning of 'Self-Sufficient' is negation of need—or relations, like the Creator and the Provider according to our terminology, and the Originator and the First Cause according to your terminology. And there is no attribute of God beyond these two meanings. So generosity belongs to the category of relation, not negation. Thus there is no difference between the meaning of 'Originator' and the meaning of 'generosity'; both mean the efficient, the maker. So it is as if you said: 'maker by His essence,' and that is the very point of dispute and a petition of the first principle. For the adversary says: He is not an agent by His essence, and His act is not eternal. In that lies the disagreement. You changed the expression from 'act' to 'generosity' and made it the proof of the matter. And if the meaning of 'generosity' goes back to act and bringing into existence, then his saying: 'He is sometimes generous and sometimes not generous...'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (shubahāt) for the eternity of the world, attributed to Proclus and similar thinkers, followed by the beginning of the author's response. The first argument: God is essentially generous (jawād bi-dhātih), His generosity is the cause of the world, and since His generosity is eternal, the world must be eternal. God cannot be sometimes generous and sometimes not, as that would imply change in His essence. There is no impediment to the outpouring of His generosity; an essential impediment would be permanent (contradicting His generosity), and an external impediment would require something to bear or constrain God, which is impossible. The second argument: The Maker is either eternally actual or eternal potential. If actual, the effect co-exists eternally; if potential, a cause external to His essence is needed to actualize the potential, which would entail change in His essence. The third argument: A cause that is immutable is a cause by essence, so its effect is also by essence and thus eternal. The answer begins by questioning the meaning of 'generous by essence.' The author argues that generosity is not an essential attribute but an attribute of action; attributes are either negations (like qadīm, ghani) or relations (like khāliq, rāziq). Generosity is a relational attribute, synonymous with 'Originator' or 'First Cause', meaning simply 'agent' or 'maker'. Thus the objection merely restates the point in dispute (that God is agent by essence) without proof—a begging the question. The answer is cut off at the end of the page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'generosity' is an essential attribute like knowledge, directly implying eternal giving.
Correction: The author shows that 'generosity' is a relational attribute about action, not essence, so it cannot prove eternal creation without begging the question.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the first doubt by showing that 'generosity' is a relational attribute meaning 'maker', so the argument just assumes God is an eternal maker without proof.

?
The author insists on precise categories for God's attributes. Are you content with vague notions about God, or will you commit to deeper study of His names and attributes to avoid misattributing to Him what is not worthy?
?
If generosity is truly about action, not essence, then your own acts of generosity reflect your character. Are your generous deeds consistent and deliberate, or are they sporadic and thoughtless? How will you train yourself to be genuinely generous as a steady habit?

First Shubhah: Is the Maker Eternally Actual or Potential?
The author presents a shubhah about the Maker's eternal activity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the objection about the Maker's eternal activity: that it seems to force either an eternal world or a change in the Maker.

Core Idea
The Maker's eternal activity appears to demand either an eternal cosmos or an impossible change in the Maker.

The objector presents a dilemma: either the Maker has always been actually creating, or He has always been potentially able to create. Both alternatives lead to conclusions that the mutakallimun (theologians) find unacceptable. This passage lays out the objection clearly before the author proceeds to refute it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The objector begins by asserting that the Maker must be either eternally actual or eternally potential. Recognize this as a forced choice designed to create a problem.
Pay attention to the logical structure of the two alternatives.
2
Trace the first alternative
If the Maker is eternally actual, then His effect (the artifact) must also be eternal, implying the world is eternal. This contradicts the belief in temporal creation.
The opponent likely denies an eternal world.
3
Trace the second alternative
If the Maker is eternally potential, then something external must bring Him from potentiality to actuality, causing a change in His essence. The objector deems this impossible.
This alternative leads to an unacceptable change in the Divine essence.
4
Reflect on the problem
Consider how this objection challenges the claim that the world is temporally originated while preserving divine immutability. The author will later refute it.
This sets up the need for a sophisticated theological response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى تقارب هذه الشبهة، قال: ليس يخلو الصانع من أن يكون لم يزل صانعا بالفعل، أو لم يزل صانعا بالقوة، فإن كان الأول فالمصنوع معلول لم يزل، وإن كان الثاني فما بالقوة لا يخرج إلى الفعل إلا بمخرج، ومخرج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل غير ذات الشيء، فيجب أن تتغير ذات الصانع لمغير وذلك باطل.
Another objection similar to this objection: he said: The Maker is not free from either having been eternally an actual maker, or having been eternally a potential maker. If the first, then the artifact is an effect that never ceases. If the second, then what is in potentiality does not emerge into actuality except by an extraneous factor, and the extraneous factor of a thing from potentiality to actuality is other than the essence of the thing, so it is necessary that the essence of the Maker change due to a changer, and that is false.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection (shubhah) about the eternal activity of the Maker. The objector argues that the Maker must be either eternally acting actually or eternally acting potentially. If He is eternally actually acting, then His effect (the world) must be eternal, which the opponent (likely the mutakallimun) denies. If He is eternally potential, then He needs something external to bring Him from potentiality to actuality, which would imply change in His essence, and that is impossible. Thus, the objector concludes that the belief in a temporal world (huduth) is problematic. The author is presenting this objection before refuting it.

Generosity as a Relational Attribute
The author defines generosity as a relational attribute of action and exposes a logical error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why generosity (al-jūd) is classified as a relational attribute of action rather than an essential attribute, and why the objection commits a petition of principle.

Core Idea
Generosity is not an essence – it's an action.

In this passage, the author begins his answer to the two previous shubhahs by questioning the very meaning of 'generosity by essence.' He argues that generosity is not an attribute of God's essence but rather an attribute of His action, falling under the category of relation. This distinction is crucial, because the opponent's argument mistakenly treats generosity as an essential attribute, leading to a petition of principle. Let us examine the author's logical dissection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of divine attributes
The author states that attributes are either negations (like eternal and self-sufficient) or relations (like Creator and Provider). Generosity belongs to relations.
This classification is drawn from the philosophers' own framework.
2
See how 'generosity' reduces to 'Maker'
The author equates 'generous by essence' with 'Maker by essence,' which is exactly the point of dispute.
The opponent says God is not an agent by essence.
3
Identify the petition of principle
The objector merely changed the wording from 'action' to 'generosity' without proving anything, thus committing a logical fallacy.
This is a key refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: قلنا: ما المعنى بقولك: الباري تعالى جواد بذاته؟ وما معنى الجود؟ فإن عندنا وعندكم الجودة ليس صفة ذاتية زايدة على الذات، بل هو صفة فعلية، والصفات عندكم إما أن تكون سلوبا كالقديم والغنى، فإن معنى القديم نفي الأولية، ومعنى الغنى نفي الحاجة، وإما أن تكون إضافات كالخالق والرازق على اصطلاحنا والمبدئ، والعلة الأولى على اصطلاحكم وليس الله صفة وراء هذين المعنيين، فالجود من قسم الإضافة لا من قسم السلب، فلا فرق إذا بين معنى المبدئ وبين معنى الجود، ومعناهما الفاعل الصانع، فكأنكم قلتم: صانع بذاته، وهو محل النزاع ومصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن الخصم يقول: ليس فاعلا لذاته، وفعله ليس قديما لم يزل، وفيه وقع الخلاف غيرت العبارة من الفعل إلى الجود وجعلته دليل المسألة، وإذا كان معنى الجود راجع إلى الفعل والإيجاد فقوله: مرة هو جواد ومرة غير جواد كقوله: مرة فاعل ومرة غير فاعل، وذلك أيضا محل النزاع فلم يجب أن يقال: إنه يوجب التغير.
وإنما حل الشبهة فيه من وجهين:
أحدهما: أن الفعل إنما امتنع في الأزل لا لمعنى يرجع إلى الفاعل، بل لمعنى راجع إلى نفس الفعل حيث لم يتصور وجوده، فإن الفعل ما له أول، والأزل ما لا أول له، واجتماع ما لا أول له مع ما له أول محال، فهو تعالى جواد حيث يتصور الجود ولا يستحيل الموجود، أليس لو عين الشخص في زماننا فيقال: يجب أن يوجد أزلا؛ لأن الباري سبحانه جواد لذاته، كان السؤال محالا؛ لأن الموجود المعين استحال وجوده فيما لم يزل، فاستحالة وجود الموجود هو المانع لفيض الوجود لا منعا يكون ذلك حجرا أو زجرا، بل هو ممتنع في ذاته، وهكذا لو أوجد ما أوجده أولاً آخراً، أو أوجد جميع الموجودات معا من غير ترتيب واحد على واحد كل ذلك غير جايز عند الخصم، ولم يقدح في كونه جوادا، هذا كما يقدره المتكلم أن الباري سبحانه يوصف بالقدرة على ما يجوز وجوده، وأما ما يستحيل وجوده فلا يقال: الباري تعالى ليس قادرا عليه، بل يقال: المستحيل في ذاته غير مقدور فلا يتصور وجوده.
And the answer: We say: What do you mean by 'the Exalted Creator is generous by His essence'? And what is the meaning of generosity? For according to us and according to you, generosity is not an essential attribute superadded to the essence, but rather it is an attribute of action. And attributes according to you are either negations like eternal (al-qadīm) and self-sufficient (al-ghanī), for the meaning of eternal is negation of beginning, and the meaning of self-sufficient is negation of need; or relational like the Creator (al-khāliq) and the Provider (al-rāziq) according to our terminology, and the Originator (al-mubdi') and the First Cause (al-ʿilla al-ūlā) according to your terminology. And there is no attribute of God beyond these two meanings. So generosity belongs to the category of relation, not the category of negation. Therefore, there is no difference between the meaning of Originator and the meaning of generosity. Their meaning is the acting Maker (al-fāʿil al-ṣāniʿ). So it is as if you said: 'Maker by His essence,' and that is the point of dispute and a petition of principle (muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb al-awwal). For the opponent says: He is not an agent by His essence, and His action is not eternal. And therein lies the disagreement. You have merely changed the expression from 'action' to 'generosity' and made it the proof of the issue. And if the meaning of generosity reduces to action and bringing into existence, then saying: sometimes He is generous and sometimes not generous is like saying: sometimes He is an agent and sometimes not an agent. That too is the point of dispute, so it is not correct to say that it necessitates change.

And the resolution of the objection (shubhah) therein is from two aspects:

One of them: That action was only prevented in pre-eternity (al-azal) not due to a meaning that goes back to the agent, but due to a meaning that goes back to the action itself, since its existence was not conceivable. For action has a beginning, and pre-eternity has no beginning, and the conjunction of what has no beginning with what has a beginning is impossible. So the Exalted is generous where generosity is conceivable and existence is not impossible. Consider: if you specify a person in our time and say: it must exist eternally because the Exalted Creator is generous by His essence, this question would be impossible; because the existence of that specific existent in what never ceases is impossible. So the impossibility of the existence of the existent is the preventer of the outpouring of existence, not a prevention that is a prohibition or deterrence, but it is impossible in itself. And likewise, if He brought into existence what He brought into existence first and last, or brought into existence all existents together without ordering one after another, all of that is not permissible according to the opponent, and it does not impair His being generous. This is like what the mutakallim considers: that the Exalted Creator is described as having power over what is possible of existence. But as for what is impossible of existence, it is not said: the Exalted Creator is not powerful over it, but rather it is said: the impossible in itself is not an object of power, so its existence is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is the author's detailed answer to the two previous shubhahs. He begins by questioning the meaning of 'generosity by essence' (جواد بذاته). He argues that generosity (الجود) is not an essential attribute but an attribute of action. According to the philosophers (whom he is addressing), attributes are either negations (like eternal and self-sufficient) or relations (like Creator and Provider). Generosity falls under relation, not negation. So saying 'generous by essence' is equivalent to saying 'Maker by essence,' which is exactly the point of dispute. The opponent claims God is not an agent by essence and His act is not eternal. The objector has merely changed the wording without proof, committing a petition of principle. Then the author gives two resolutions: 

1. The first resolution: The impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (al-azal) is not due to the agent but due to the act itself. The act has a beginning, while pre-eternity has no beginning, so conjoining them is conceptually impossible. God's generosity is manifest when and where existence is possible; it is not undermined by the impossibility of a specific thing existing eternally. The example: If a specific person exists now, we cannot demand that he should have existed eternally because of God's generosity, because his specific existence in eternity is inherently impossible. This impossibility is not a withholding from God but a logical impossibility in the object itself. So God remains generous even though He does not create everything at once or eternally.

2. The second resolution (implied in the analogy): God's power (qudra) is only over what is possible; the impossible is not an object of power. So the fact that God does not create the world eternally does not impugn His generosity or power. 

Thus, the author refutes the objections by clarifying the nature of generosity and by showing that the impossibility of eternal creation lies in the object, not the agent.

Key Terms
الجود
صفة فعلية
إضافة
سلب
الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'generosity' is an essential attribute of God like omniscience.
Correction: The author clarifies that generosity is an attribute of action, not of essence, because it involves relation to creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'generosity' considered a relational attribute?
A) Because it is a negation of need.
B) Because it implies a relation to an object of generosity.
C) Because it is superadded to the essence.
D) Because it is synonymous with 'eternal'.

Key Takeaway
Generosity is a relational attribute of action, and using it to argue for eternal creation commits a petition of principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'generous,' do you think of it as a permanent fact about His essence or as something He actively does for you? How does that distinction change how you rely on His generosity in your daily struggles?
?
The author says that calling God 'generous by essence' is circular reasoning. Examine your own arguments about God: are there ways you assume something about God without realizing it's the very thing you're trying to prove? What does that reveal about your intellectual honesty before Him?
?
If generosity is a relational attribute tied to action, then your experience of God's generosity depends on His acting toward you. Are you living in expectation of that action, or have you reduced His generosity to a comfortable abstraction? Ask yourself: when was the last time you truly thanked God for a specific act of generosity, not just a general feeling?

5 min
Two Resolutions on Generosity and Pre-eternity
The author offers two resolutions to the objection that God's generosity would require changing from non-generous to generous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity does not contradict God's generosity, through two logical resolutions.

Core Idea
Impossibility lies in the act, not the agent.

Having clarified that generosity is a relational attribute, the author now directly answers the objection that God would have to change from non-generous to generous. He presents two resolutions: first, the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity is due to the nature of the act itself, not a deficiency in God; second, God's power only applies to what is possible. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the first resolution: conceptual impossibility
Action has a beginning, pre-eternity has no beginning; their conjunction is logically impossible.
This shifts the focus from God to the nature of the act.
2
Apply the analogy of specific existents
If a specific person exists now, we cannot demand they exist eternally; their existence is inherently impossible.
God's generosity is not diminished by such impossibility.
3
Understand the second resolution: power and possibility
God's power only covers possible things; impossible things are not objects of power.
This parallels the discussion on divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: قلنا: ما المعنى بقولك: الباري تعالى جواد بذاته؟ وما معنى الجود؟ فإن عندنا وعندكم الجودة ليس صفة ذاتية زايدة على الذات، بل هو صفة فعلية، والصفات عندكم إما أن تكون سلوبا كالقديم والغنى، فإن معنى القديم نفي الأولية، ومعنى الغنى نفي الحاجة، وإما أن تكون إضافات كالخالق والرازق على اصطلاحنا والمبدئ، والعلة الأولى على اصطلاحكم وليس الله صفة وراء هذين المعنيين، فالجود من قسم الإضافة لا من قسم السلب، فلا فرق إذا بين معنى المبدئ وبين معنى الجود، ومعناهما الفاعل الصانع، فكأنكم قلتم: صانع بذاته، وهو محل النزاع ومصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن الخصم يقول: ليس فاعلا لذاته، وفعله ليس قديما لم يزل، وفيه وقع الخلاف غيرت العبارة من الفعل إلى الجود وجعلته دليل المسألة، وإذا كان معنى الجود راجع إلى الفعل والإيجاد فقوله: مرة هو جواد ومرة غير جواد كقوله: مرة فاعل ومرة غير فاعل، وذلك أيضا محل النزاع فلم يجب أن يقال: إنه يوجب التغير.
وإنما حل الشبهة فيه من وجهين:
أحدهما: أن الفعل إنما امتنع في الأزل لا لمعنى يرجع إلى الفاعل، بل لمعنى راجع إلى نفس الفعل حيث لم يتصور وجوده، فإن الفعل ما له أول، والأزل ما لا أول له، واجتماع ما لا أول له مع ما له أول محال، فهو تعالى جواد حيث يتصور الجود ولا يستحيل الموجود، أليس لو عين الشخص في زماننا فيقال: يجب أن يوجد أزلا؛ لأن الباري سبحانه جواد لذاته، كان السؤال محالا؛ لأن الموجود المعين استحال وجوده فيما لم يزل، فاستحالة وجود الموجود هو المانع لفيض الوجود لا منعا يكون ذلك حجرا أو زجرا، بل هو ممتنع في ذاته، وهكذا لو أوجد ما أوجده أولاً آخراً، أو أوجد جميع الموجودات معا من غير ترتيب واحد على واحد كل ذلك غير جايز عند الخصم، ولم يقدح في كونه جوادا، هذا كما يقدره المتكلم أن الباري سبحانه يوصف بالقدرة على ما يجوز وجوده، وأما ما يستحيل وجوده فلا يقال: الباري تعالى ليس قادرا عليه، بل يقال: المستحيل في ذاته غير مقدور فلا يتصور وجوده.
And the answer: We say: What do you mean by 'the Exalted Creator is generous by His essence'? And what is the meaning of generosity? For according to us and according to you, generosity is not an essential attribute superadded to the essence, but rather it is an attribute of action. And attributes according to you are either negations like eternal (al-qadīm) and self-sufficient (al-ghanī), for the meaning of eternal is negation of beginning, and the meaning of self-sufficient is negation of need; or relational like the Creator (al-khāliq) and the Provider (al-rāziq) according to our terminology, and the Originator (al-mubdi') and the First Cause (al-ʿilla al-ūlā) according to your terminology. And there is no attribute of God beyond these two meanings. So generosity belongs to the category of relation, not the category of negation. Therefore, there is no difference between the meaning of Originator and the meaning of generosity. Their meaning is the acting Maker (al-fāʿil al-ṣāniʿ). So it is as if you said: 'Maker by His essence,' and that is the point of dispute and a petition of principle (muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb al-awwal). For the opponent says: He is not an agent by His essence, and His action is not eternal. And therein lies the disagreement. You have merely changed the expression from 'action' to 'generosity' and made it the proof of the issue. And if the meaning of generosity reduces to action and bringing into existence, then saying: sometimes He is generous and sometimes not generous is like saying: sometimes He is an agent and sometimes not an agent. That too is the point of dispute, so it is not correct to say that it necessitates change.

And the resolution of the objection (shubhah) therein is from two aspects:

One of them: That action was only prevented in pre-eternity (al-azal) not due to a meaning that goes back to the agent, but due to a meaning that goes back to the action itself, since its existence was not conceivable. For action has a beginning, and pre-eternity has no beginning, and the conjunction of what has no beginning with what has a beginning is impossible. So the Exalted is generous where generosity is conceivable and existence is not impossible. Consider: if you specify a person in our time and say: it must exist eternally because the Exalted Creator is generous by His essence, this question would be impossible; because the existence of that specific existent in what never ceases is impossible. So the impossibility of the existence of the existent is the preventer of the outpouring of existence, not a prevention that is a prohibition or deterrence, but it is impossible in itself. And likewise, if He brought into existence what He brought into existence first and last, or brought into existence all existents together without ordering one after another, all of that is not permissible according to the opponent, and it does not impair His being generous. This is like what the mutakallim considers: that the Exalted Creator is described as having power over what is possible of existence. But as for what is impossible of existence, it is not said: the Exalted Creator is not powerful over it, but rather it is said: the impossible in itself is not an object of power, so its existence is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is the author's detailed answer to the two previous shubhahs. He begins by questioning the meaning of 'generosity by essence' (جواد بذاته). He argues that generosity (الجود) is not an essential attribute but an attribute of action. According to the philosophers (whom he is addressing), attributes are either negations (like eternal and self-sufficient) or relations (like Creator and Provider). Generosity falls under relation, not negation. So saying 'generous by essence' is equivalent to saying 'Maker by essence,' which is exactly the point of dispute. The opponent claims God is not an agent by essence and His act is not eternal. The objector has merely changed the wording without proof, committing a petition of principle. Then the author gives two resolutions: 

1. The first resolution: The impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (al-azal) is not due to the agent but due to the act itself. The act has a beginning, while pre-eternity has no beginning, so conjoining them is conceptually impossible. God's generosity is manifest when and where existence is possible; it is not undermined by the impossibility of a specific thing existing eternally. The example: If a specific person exists now, we cannot demand that he should have existed eternally because of God's generosity, because his specific existence in eternity is inherently impossible. This impossibility is not a withholding from God but a logical impossibility in the object itself. So God remains generous even though He does not create everything at once or eternally.

2. The second resolution (implied in the analogy): God's power (qudra) is only over what is possible; the impossible is not an object of power. So the fact that God does not create the world eternally does not impugn His generosity or power. 

Thus, the author refutes the objections by clarifying the nature of generosity and by showing that the impossibility of eternal creation lies in the object, not the agent.

Key Terms
الأزل
الممتنع
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is generous, He must create the world in pre-eternity, and not doing so shows stinginess.
Correction: The author explains that the impossibility lies in the act itself, not in God. Eternal creation is conceptually impossible, so God's generosity is not affected.

Checkpoint
According to the first resolution, why was creation impossible in pre-eternity?
A) Because God was not generous at that time.
B) Because the act itself has a beginning, contradicting pre-eternity's lack of beginning.
C) Because the opponent decided it was impossible.
D) Because God lacked power over it.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of creation in pre-eternity is not a flaw in God's generosity or power, but a logical impossibility in the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a time when you accused God of not being generous because He didn't give you something you wanted? How does this lesson's distinction between impossibility in the act and the agent change your view? Will you repent from that accusation and trust His wisdom?
?
You have likely demanded from God things that are logically impossible—like wanting a consequence without a cause. This lesson says power relates only to the possible. What specific impossible demand are you holding onto? Are you willing to let it go and see His generosity in what He has made possible for you?
?
The source says impossibility is in the object, not the agent. Think of a trial in your life that you believe God could have prevented. Could it be that the prevention itself was impossible because it would contradict His wisdom or justice? Will you now submit to His decree as a reflection of His perfection, not His lack?

Answering the Objection That God's Generosity Is Not Negated by Temporal Creation
The author resolves the first shubha regarding God's generosity and temporal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends God's generosity against the objection that He did not create in eternity, by distinguishing between the possibility of the act and the capability of the agent.

Core Idea
Generosity is about giving what is possible, not about giving at all times.

The idea that a generous being must always be giving is intuitive, but the author challenges this assumption. He argues that true generosity is about giving existence when it is possible, and that the impossibility of an eternal act does not reflect on God's character. This passage explains how the act's temporal nature is due to its own limits, not any deficiency in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two-part answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the objection and the author's response. Notice the structure: the objection is stated, then the answer is given in two aspects (وجهين).
2
Focus on the first aspect
The first aspect argues that the act was impossible in eternity not because of God, but because the act itself has a beginning and eternity has no beginning. Understand why this combination is impossible.
This shows that the impossibility is in the act itself, not in the agent.
3
Examine the second aspect with examples
The author gives examples: if a specific temporal being were demanded to exist eternally, it would be impossible because that being's existence in eternity is inherently impossible. Similarly, creating all things at once is not required for generosity.
4
Connect to the principle about divine power
The passage concludes with a general principle: God is described as powerful over what is possible. Impossible things are simply not objects of power, so saying 'God is not generous' is a category mistake.
This reinforces that God's generosity is not diminished by the impossibility of certain acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير جواد كقوله : مرة فاعل ومرة غير فاعل، وذلك أيضا محل النزاع فلم يجب أن يقال : إنه يوجب التغير. وإنما حل الشبهة فيه من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الفعل إنما امتنع في الأزل لا لمعنى يرجع إلى الفاعل، بل لمعنى راجع إلى نفس الفعل حيث لم يتصور وجوده ، فإن الفعل ما له أول، والأزل ما لا أول له ، واجتماع ما لا أول له مع ما له أول محال، فهو تعالى جواد حيث يتصور الجود ولا يستحيل الموجود، أليس لو عين الشخص في زماننا فيقال: يجب أن يوجد أزلا ؛ لأن الباري سبحانه جواد لذاته، كان السؤال محالا ؛ لأن الموجود المعين استحال وجوده فيما لم يزل، فاستحالة وجود الموجود هو المانع لفيض الوجود لا منعًا يكون ذلك حملا أو زجرا ، بل هو ممتنع في ذاته، وهكذا لو أوجده ما أوجده أولا وآخرا، أو أوجد جميع الموجودات معا من غير ترتيب واحد على واحد كل ذلك غير جايز عند الخصم ، ولم يقدح في كونه جوادًا ، هذا كما يقدره المتكلم أن الباري سبحانه يوصف بالقدرة على ما يجوز وجوده، وأما ما يستحيل وجوده فلا يُقال : الباري تعالى ليس قادرًا عليه، بل يقال : المستحيل في ذاته غير مقدور فلا يتصور وجوده.
Not generous, as one says: sometimes an agent and sometimes not an agent, and that too is the point of dispute, so it should not be said: it necessitates change. The resolution of the doubt is from two aspects: One of them: The act was prohibited in eternity not due to a meaning returning to the agent, but due to a meaning returning to the act itself, since its existence was not conceivable. For the act has a beginning, and eternity has no beginning, and the combination of what has no beginning with what has a beginning is impossible. Thus He, exalted, is generous where generosity is conceivable and the existing is not impossible. If a particular person in our time were specified and it were said: it must exist eternally because the Creator is generous by essence, the question would be impossible, because the existence of that particular being in what has not ceased is impossible. So the impossibility of the existence of the existing is the preventer of the outpouring of existence, not a prevention that is a restraint or prohibition, but it is impossible in itself. Similarly, if He brought into existence what He brought into existence first and last, or brought all existents together without one following another, all that is not permissible according to the opponent, and it does not detract from His being generous. This is as the mutakallim holds: the Creator is described as having power over what is possible of existence. As for what is impossible of existence, it is not said: the Creator is not capable of it, but it is said: the impossible in itself is not an object of power, so its existence is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab). The objection claims that if God was not an agent in eternity and then became one in time, He would be subject to change, which would negate His generosity. The author gives a two-part answer. First, the act was impossible in eternity not because of any deficiency in God, but because the act itself has a beginning, and eternity has no beginning—combining them is impossible. Therefore, God is truly generous because He gives existence when it is possible. Second, the author illustrates with examples: if someone demanded that a specific temporal being must exist eternally because God is generous, the demand is absurd because that being's existence in eternity is inherently impossible. The impossibility lies in the thing itself, not in a lack of generosity. He then extends this to other scenarios like creating all things simultaneously, which the opponent also rejects without accusing God of stinginess. The conclusion is that God is described as powerful over what is possible; impossible things are simply not objects of power.

جواد
أزل
شبهة
مستحيل
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's delayed creation shows He is not generous.
Correction: God's generosity is not measured by the timing of creation; the act was impossible in eternity due to its own nature, not due to any deficiency in God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was the act of creation impossible in eternity?
A) Because God was not yet generous
B) Because the act has a beginning and eternity has no beginning
C) Because God was weak in eternity
D) Because the act was not needed

Key Takeaway
God's generosity is not compromised by the timing of creation; rather, what is impossible in itself cannot be an object of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Think of a desire you have that seems delayed. Could it be that the timing of its fulfillment is impossible in itself? How does understanding God's generosity as giving 'when possible' affect your patience and trust? Are you willing to let go of your timeline?
?
If God's non-creation in eternity is not due to stinginess but due to the act's nature, how does that reshape your understanding of His wisdom? Will you now surrender your complaints and instead ask: 'What is truly possible according to His knowledge?' Make a commitment to do that today.

Answering the Objection That God Became an Agent
The author applies the same reasoning to the shubha about God becoming an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's not being an agent in eternity does not imply a change in His essence, but rather stems from the impossibility of the act itself.

Core Idea
God didn't change; the act was impossible in eternity by its very nature.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: if God was not an agent in eternity and then became one, does that mean He changed from potential to actual? The author's answer is a gem of kalam reasoning, distinguishing between two types of impossibility. Follow the logic carefully to see how divine transcendence is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the objection: 'If He was not an agent and then became an agent...' and the author's response.
The objection is about potentiality and actuality.
2
Understand intrinsic vs. extrinsic impossibility
The key distinction: some things are impossible in themselves (like combining eternity and beginning), while others are impossible due to an external condition. The act is impossible in itself in eternity.
Intrinsic impossibility means it cannot exist at all in that context.
3
See why eternity and beginning cannot combine
The act has a beginning, but eternity has no beginning. These are mutually exclusive, so the act is impossible in eternity. When eternity is negated, the possibility appears.
This is a logical, not temporal, point.
4
Recognize that God's essence doesn't change
The objection claims God changed from potential to actual. The author shows that the impossibility is in the object, not in God. God's power remains constant; it is the object of power that changes status.
Thus God remains transcendent and unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الجواب أيضا عن قولهم : لو لم يكن صانعا فصار صانعا كان صانعا بالقوة فصار صانعا بالفعل وتغيرت ذاته. فإنا نقول : إنما لم يكن صانعا لم يزل ؛ لأن الصنع فيما لم يزل مستحيل الوجود، وإذا استحال وجود الشيء في نفسه ولذاته لم يكن مقدورا، فلا يكون مصنوعا قط، وإذا استحال وجود الشيء لمعنى آخر كان جايزا في ذاته ، فإذا زال ذلك المعنى تحقق الجواز فصار مقدورا فيكون مصنوعا والصنع لم يزل، إنما استحال لنفي أولية الأزل وإثبات أولية الصنع، فكان الجمع بينهما محالا ، فإذا نفيت الأزلية تعينت الأولية، فتحقق الجواز، فصح المقدور، فوجد المصنوع.
And this is also the answer to their statement: 'If He was not an agent and then became an agent, He would have been an agent in potentiality and then became an agent in actuality, and His essence would have changed.' So we say: He was not an agent in eternity only because the act in what has not ceased is impossible of existence. When the existence of a thing is impossible in itself and by its essence, it is not an object of power, so it is never made. And when the existence of a thing is impossible due to another meaning, it is possible in itself, so when that meaning ceases, the possibility is realized, and it becomes an object of power and thus is made. And the act has not ceased [to be impossible], it was only impossible due to the negation of the beginninglessness of eternity and the affirmation of the beginning of the act. So the combination of the two was impossible. When eternity is negated, beginning becomes determined, possibility is realized, the object of power is valid, and the made thing exists.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is the answer to a second objection (shubha). The objection states that if God was not an agent in eternity and then became one in time, it would imply a change in His essence from potentiality to actuality. The author replies by distinguishing between two types of impossibility: intrinsic and extrinsic. The act (صنع) is intrinsically impossible in eternity because eternity has no beginning and the act has a beginning—they cannot coexist. Thus, God was not an agent not due to a change in Him, but because the act itself was impossible. When the obstacle (the beginninglessness of eternity) is removed, the possibility emerges and the act can occur. The author emphasizes that the impossibility is due to the logical contradiction of combining a beginningless state with a beginningful act, not due to any deficiency in God's power or essence.

Key Terms
صانع
صنع
مقدور
جواز
أولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's becoming an agent after not being one implies a change in His essence from potentiality to actuality.
Correction: The change is not in God but in the object of power: the act was impossible in eternity due to a logical contradiction, so God's not being an agent was due to the impossibility of the act, not any deficiency in Him.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was God not an agent in eternity?
A) Because He lacked power
B) Because the act was impossible in itself due to the nature of eternity
C) Because He was waiting for the right time
D) Because He was not yet generous

Key Takeaway
God's not being an agent in eternity is due to the intrinsic impossibility of the act, not a change in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted Allah's power because He did not act according to your desired timeline? How does the author's explanation that the act itself was 'intrinsically impossible' in eternity reshape your understanding of divine wisdom and patience? What can you do this week to trust Allah's timing even when it contradicts your expectations?
?
When you encounter a situation that seems 'impossible' to resolve, do you attribute that impossibility to Allah's inability or to the nature of the situation itself? Based on this lesson, how can you distinguish between what is truly impossible for Allah and what is only temporarily impossible due to wisdom? Commit to reciting the name 'Al-Qadir' (The All-Powerful) with this understanding in your next prayer.
?
The passage teaches that God does not change; rather, the conditions for the act change. In your own life, do you ever expect Allah to change His essence to accommodate your desires? How can you instead change your own perspective to align with His eternal wisdom? Write down one specific area where you need to adjust your expectations, and surrender it to Allah today.

Refuting the Notion That God Is a Cause of Himself
The author refutes the shubha that God is a cause of Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God is a cause of Himself is impossible and how the author distinguishes cause as originator of something else.

Core Idea
God is the originator of the world, not a cause of Himself.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle doubt: if God is a cause, could He be a cause of His own essence? By clarifying the meaning of 'cause' and the logical implications of simultaneity, the author shows that self-causation is absurd. This reinforces the transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the key terms like علة, معلول, معية.
The passage is dense but short; read it carefully.
2
Understand the definition of cause
The author clarifies that being a cause means being an originator (مبدئ) of something else, not a cause of one's own essence. This is the crucial distinction that resolves the doubt.
Notice that the word 'cause' is used differently here: it implies bringing something into existence.
3
Analyze the two impossibilities
The author explains that it is impossible for the effect to exist simultaneously with the cause—either in essence or in time. Simultaneity in essence would negate causality; in time would make the cause temporal and changeable.
This leads to the conclusion that the cause must precede the effect in existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أنه علة لذاته، فإن معنى كونه علة أي مبدئ لوجود شيء، وإنما الممتنع وجود المعلول العلة معية بالذات، فإن ذلك يرفع العلية والمعلولية، وكذلك نقول: يستحيل وجود المعلول العلية معية بالزمان، فإن ذلك يوجب كون العلة زمانية قابلة للتغير، وذلك محال أيضا، فيجب أن يتقدم في الوجود ؛ لأن وجود الباري سبحانه وجود لذاته غير مستفاد من غيره، ووجود العالم مستفاد منه ، والمفيد أبدا يتقدم في الوجود.
And likewise the answer to the third doubt, that He is a cause of His essence. For the meaning of His being a cause is that He is an originator (mubdi') of the existence of something. The only impossible thing is the existence of the effect together with the cause in terms of essence, for that would negate causality and effect-hood. And similarly we say: It is impossible for the effect to exist together with the cause in terms of time, for that would necessitate that the cause be temporal and subject to change, and that is also impossible. So the cause must precede in existence, because the existence of the Creator, exalted, is an existence by itself, not derived from another, and the existence of the world is derived from Him. And the giver (mufid) always precedes in existence.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is the answer to a third objection (shubha). The objection claims that if God is a cause ('illa) of His own essence, that would be impossible. The author clarifies the meaning of 'cause': it means an originator (mubdi') of something else, not a cause of itself. The impossibility is that the effect exists simultaneously with the cause either in essence (ma'iyya bi al-dhat) or in time (ma'iyya bi al-zaman). Simultaneity in essence would negate causality entirely. Simultaneity in time would make the cause temporal and changeable, which is impossible for God. Therefore, the cause must precede the effect in existence. The author then gives the reason: God's existence is self-subsistent and not derived from anything, while the world's existence is derived from Him. The giver always precedes the receiver in existence.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مبدئ
معية
مفيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is a cause of Himself.
Correction: God is the originator of the world, not a cause of His own essence. Being a cause means bringing something else into existence, not self-causation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the cause must precede the effect in existence?
A) Because the cause is temporal
B) Because the cause's existence is self-subsistent and the effect's is derived
C) Because the effect is impossible
D) Because God changes

Key Takeaway
The cause must precede the effect in existence because God's existence is self-subsistent while the world's is derived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are the effect; God is the cause. Your existence is derived from Him, moment by moment. But do you live as if your existence is your own? Have you ever felt that you are the originator of your own life—your career, your achievements, your relationships? How does the truth that you are always a receiver, never a giver in relation to God, change the way you view your successes and failures? Will you return to a posture of complete dependency, acknowledging that your very breath is from Him?
?
The passage teaches that simultaneity in time makes the cause temporal and changeable, which is impossible for God. You live in time, bound by seconds and years, subject to change. How often do you project onto God your own experience of time—expecting Him to act when you want, as if He shares your schedule? Are you willing to submit to a God who transcends time, trusting that His precedence is not measured by your clock but by the reality that He is always first? Will you stop trying to bring God into your timeline and instead accept that you are in His?

God's Creation Does Not Imply Change
Author answers objection about divine creation implying change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that God's becoming a creator would entail a change in His essence, by distinguishing between impossibility in itself and impossibility due to an external condition.

Core Idea
God's non-creation before time is due to the object's impossibility, not a change in His power.

This passage addresses a classic objection: if God was not a creator in eternity and then became a creator, does that imply a change in His essence? The author provides a nuanced answer by analyzing the concept of impossibility. This lesson will walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'صانع' (creator) and 'مستحيل' (impossible).
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if God was not a creator and then became one, He changed from potentiality to actuality, implying change in His essence.
The objection is stated at the beginning: 'لو لم يكن صانعا فصار صانعا...'
3
Understand the two types of impossibility
The author distinguishes between impossibility in itself (مستحيل لذاته) and impossibility due to an external condition (مستحيل لمعنى آخر). The former makes something not within power; the latter can be resolved.
This distinction is key to the refutation.
4
Follow the resolution
Creation in eternity is impossible in itself, so God's not creating is due to the object's impossibility, not a lack of power. When time begins, the condition of eternity is removed, possibility is realized, and God creates.
Thus no change in God's essence occurs; only the condition for the object's possibility changes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that creation has not ceased (لم يزل) and no change in God's essence is implied.
This defends the doctrine of God's immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الجواب أيضًا عن قولهم: لو لم يكن صانعا فصار صانعا كان صانعا بالقوة فصار صانعا بالفعل وتغيرت ذاته. فإنا نقول: إنما لم يكن صانعا لم يزل؛ لأن الصنع فيما لم يزل مستحيل الوجود، وإذا استحال وجود الشيء في نفسه ولذاته لم يكن مقدوراً، فلا يكون مصنوعا قط، وإذا استحال وجود الشيء لمعنى آخر كان جايزا في ذاته، فإذا زال ذلك المعنى تحقق الجواز فصار مقدوراً فيكون مصنوعا والصنع لم يزل، إنما استحال لنفي أولية الأزل وإثبات أولية الصنع، فكان الجمع بينهما محالا، فإذا نفيت الأزلية تعينت الأولية، فتحقق الجواز، فصح المقدور، فوجد المصنوع.
This is also the answer to their statement: If He was not a creator and then became a creator, He was a creator potentially and became a creator actually, and His essence changed. So we say: He was not a creator only because creation in what has not ceased [in eternity] is impossible of existence. When the existence of a thing is impossible in itself and by its essence, it is not within power, so it is never made. And when the existence of a thing is impossible for another meaning, it is possible in itself; when that meaning ceases, the possibility is realized, so it becomes within power, and thus it is made. And creation has not ceased; it was impossible only because of denying the priority of eternity and affirming the priority of creation, so combining them is impossible. When eternality is negated, priority is determined, possibility is realized, the possible becomes sound, and the made thing exists.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The opponents argue that if God was not a creator and then became a creator, His essence would change from potentiality to actuality, implying change in God. The author refutes this by distinguishing two kinds of impossibility: 1) Impossibility in itself (the thing's existence is inherently impossible), and 2) Impossibility due to an external condition (the thing is possible in itself but prevented by something else). Creation in eternity is impossible in itself because eternal creation is contradictory (it would require both eternity and temporal priority). So God's non-creation in eternity is not due to a lack of power but due to the impossibility of the object. When the condition of eternity is removed (i.e., when time begins), the thing becomes possible, and God creates it. Thus no change occurs in God's essence; only the condition for the object's possibility changes.

Key Terms
صانع
بالقوة
بالفعل
أزل
أولية
مستحيل
مقدور
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's non-creation before time implies a change in His attributes or essence.
Correction: The author argues that creation in eternity is impossible in itself (the object cannot exist), so God's not creating is due to the object's impossibility, not any change in God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was God not a creator in eternity?
A) Because He lacked the power to create.
B) Because creation in eternity is impossible in itself.
C) Because He chose not to create until a specific time.
D) Because creation would have caused change in His essence.

Key Takeaway
God's non-creation in eternity is due to the inherent impossibility of eternal creation, not any change in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's creating the universe after not creating, do you ever entertain doubts about His perfection? How does this lesson's distinction between impossibility in itself and impossibility by condition secure your certainty in God's eternal power? Make a firm intention to dispel such doubts using this logical tool.
?
Have you ever felt that your own delayed response to a command of Allah was due to a change in your resolve? In what area of your life are you still 'not creating' obedience? What 'impossibility' in yourself do you need to remove so that action becomes possible?
?
The passage teaches that when a condition changes, what was impossible becomes possible. What spiritual condition in your heart—like pride, laziness, or doubt—needs to be negated so that you can produce a good deed that you've been delaying? Write down one concrete step to remove that condition this week.
