The Second Approach: Questioning the Premise that a Creator is More Perfect
Author challenges the criterion of perfection used to argue that God must create.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that God must create because a creator is more perfect, by questioning the criterion of perfection.

Core Idea
Perfection is intrinsic to God, not dependent on His actions.

The second approach targets the very foundation of the doubt: the assumption that a being that acts is more perfect than one that does not. The author challenges this premise, asking how we know it is true, especially for a self-sufficient deity. This lesson explores that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure between the author and the opponents.
The passage is a dialectical argument.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents claim that a being that acts is more perfect than one that does not, so a creator God is more perfect. Locate this claim in the text.
The key phrase is 'موجود ما يفعل أكمل من موجود لا يفعل'.
3
Understand the author's counter-question
The author asks: if the opposite were true (non-action is more perfect), what would you say? This shows the premise is not self-evident.
The phrase 'لو عكس الأمر عليكم' indicates the reversal.
4
Grasp the distinction between intrinsic and acquired perfection
The author explains that for a being whose perfection is by itself, external action adds nothing. If a being needs action to be perfect, it is deficient in itself and cannot be the Necessary Existent.
Compare 'كماله بذاته' vs 'كماله من غيره'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that the opponents' premise does not apply to God, because His perfection is intrinsic. Thus, the doubt that God must create is unfounded.
This lesson establishes a key principle about divine self-sufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الوجه الثاني في حل الشبهة أن نطالبهم أولا فنقول: بم عرفتم أنه سبحانه يجب أن يكون جوادا لذاته؟ قالوا: لأن موجودا ما يفعل أكمل من موجود لا يفعل، وأن تصدر عنه موجودات أشرف من أن لا تصدر. قيل له: لو عكس الأمر عليكم أن الموجود الذي لا يفعل أكمل فما جوابكم عنه؟ إذ ليس هذا من القضايا الضرورية خصوصا في موجود يكون كماله بذاته لا بغيره، وإنما يصدر عنه فعل لو صدر لا لغرض، ولا ليكتسب به حمدا ولا أمرا ما يتعلق بالتناقل. لعمري لو كان موجودان أحدهما كماله بذاته، والآخر كماله من غيره، شهد العقل بالضرورة أن الذي كماله بالذات أشرف من الذي كماله بغيره، والموجود الذي لو لم يفعل فعلا كان ناقصا فليس بكامل الذات، بل هو ناقص مستكمل من غيره، فلم يصح أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته.
As for the second aspect in solving the doubt: we first demand of them and say: How do you know that He, exalted, must be generous by essence? They said: Because a being that acts is more perfect than a being that does not act, and that beings emanate from Him is nobler than that they do not emanate. It was said to him: If the matter were reversed for you, that the being which does not act is more perfect, what would be your answer? For this is not among necessary propositions, especially regarding a being whose perfection is by itself, not by another. And an act would proceed from Him only if it proceeds without purpose, and not to acquire praise or any matter related to transmission. By my life, if there were two beings, one whose perfection is by itself and the other whose perfection is from another, reason necessarily testifies that the one whose perfection is by itself is nobler than the one whose perfection is from another. And a being which, if it did not perform an act would be deficient, is not perfect in essence; rather it is deficient, seeking perfection from another, so it would not be correct for it to be necessary of existence by itself.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the second approach to solving the doubt about why God must create. The author challenges the premise that a being that acts is necessarily more perfect than one that does not act. The opponents claim that a creator God is more perfect. But the author asks: How do you know? If the opposite were true (that non-action is more perfect), what would you say? The criterion of perfection is not self-evident (ضروري) in this case. The key point: For a being whose perfection is in its own essence (كماله بذاته), external action does not add perfection. If a being would be deficient without acting, that shows its perfection depends on something else, making it not self-sufficient. The author argues that God's perfection is intrinsic; He does not need to act to be perfect. Therefore, the argument that a creator is more perfect is invalid when applied to a self-sufficient being like God.

Key Terms
جواد
كمال
بالذات
بغيره
ناقص
مستكمل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine perfection requires external actions.
Correction: The author shows that perfection is intrinsic to God, and external actions do not add to His perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the opponents' argument that a creator is more perfect?
A) The argument uses non-necessary premises.
B) The argument depends on doubt.
C) The argument assumes God is temporal.
D) The argument denies divine unity.

Key Takeaway
God's perfection is intrinsic; He does not need to create to be perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You often tie your self-worth to your achievements and actions. How does the truth that God's perfection is intrinsic, needing no acts, challenge your assumption that your value depends on what you do? What would it mean for you to find your worth in God's sufficiency rather than your productivity?

Answering the Objection of Change in God's Essence
Author answers the second objection by clarifying the sense of 'became a creator'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection that God's becoming a creator implies a change in His essence by distinguishing between change in God and change in creation.

Core Idea
God's creating does not change Him; it changes the world.

The second objection claims that if God was not a Creator and then became one, something new must have occurred. The objector assumes this new thing happens in God's essence. In this passage, the author offers a critical distinction: either the new thing occurs in God's essence or in something else. By denying the first and affirming the second, he clarifies the real point of dispute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's assumption
The objector assumes that 'becoming a creator' requires a new attribute or change in God's essence. Read the objection carefully to see this implicit assumption.
2
Notice the author's disjunction
The author responds by asking: does the new thing occur in God's essence or in something else? This disjunction breaks the objector's assumption into two clear possibilities.
3
Identify the two possibilities
The first possibility is that something new occurs in God's essence (the objector's implied claim). The second is that it occurs in what is other than His essence (i.e., in creation).
4
See which is granted and which is denied
The author clearly denies the first possibility ('the first is not granted') and grants the second ('the second is granted'). This is the key move: the only newness is in creation.
5
Recognize the point of dispute
The author concludes that the second possibility is precisely the point of dispute (محل النزاع). The objector mistakenly thought the dispute was about change in God, but it is actually about change in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حل الشبهة الثانية نقول: ما المعنى بقولكم: إذا لم يكن صانعا فصار صانعا فقد تجدد أمر؟ فنقول: تجدد في ذات الصانع أمر أم تجدد أمر في غير ذاته، والأول غير مسلّم، والثاني مسلّم، وهو محل النزاع.
And as for solving the second doubt, we say: What is the meaning of your statement: 'If He was not a creator and then became a creator, then something new has occurred'? So we say: Did something new occur in the essence of the Creator or did something new occur in what is other than His essence? The first is not granted, and the second is granted, and it is the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection. The objectors claim that if God was not a creator and then became a creator, something new must have occurred. The author responds by distinguishing two possibilities: either the new thing occurs in God's essence, or it occurs in something else (i.e., in the created world). The author denies the first possibility (that anything new occurs in God's essence) and affirms the second (that the newness is in the creation). This is precisely the point of contention (محل النزاع): the objectors assume that the change must be in God, but the author argues that the only change is the coming into existence of the world, not any change in God.

Key Terms
تجدد
صانع
ذات
محل النزاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's becoming a creator implies a change in His essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that the newness is only in the created thing, not in God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when God 'becomes a creator', where does the new thing occur?
A) In God's essence.
B) In the created thing.
C) In both God and the creation.
D) In the void.

Key Takeaway
The newness of creation does not entail any newness in God's essence; the change is only in what is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power to create, do you ever unconsciously imagine that He must 'change' from a state of not-creating to creating, as if He were a created being? How does this lesson correct that assumption, and what must you do to root out such anthropomorphic thoughts from your heart?
?
Your understanding of God's perfection hinges on His absolute lack of change. How will you actively replace those notions with the truth that God's essence is eternally the same?
?
If you truly believed that God's essence never changes regardless of creation, how would it affect your reliance on Him in times of crisis? Would you trust Him more if you knew He is not affected by the turmoil around you? Write down one specific situation this week where you will remind yourself of this truth and act upon it.

Resolving the Doubt about Divine Attribute Change
Author resolves a doubt by making two distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves a doubt about divine attribute change by distinguishing between internal and external change and clarifying the meaning of potentiality.

Core Idea
Change in God's attributes is external, not in His essence.

This passage tackles a key objection: if God was not a maker and then became a maker, doesn't that imply change in God? The author shows that the change occurs in creation, not in God's essence, and he clarifies the term 'potentiality' to avoid misunderstanding. Let's see his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the Two Distinctions
The passage makes two distinctions: one about the location of change (internal vs. external) and one about the meaning of 'potentiality' (receptivity vs. capability).
3
Understand the First Distinction
The author asks whether the new matter occurs in God's essence or outside it. He rejects the first and concedes the second, making it the point of dispute.
This preserves divine immutability.
4
Analyze the Second Distinction
The term 'potentiality' is ambiguous. Pure receptivity implies imperfection and is rejected; capability is eternal and conceded, requiring no actualization in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حل الشبهة الثانية، نقول: ما المعنى بقولكم: إذا لم يكن صانعا فصار صانعا فقد تجدد أمر؟ فنقول: تجدد في ذات الصانع أمر أم تجدد أمر في غير ذاته، والأول غير مسلّم، والثاني مسلّم، وهو محل النزاع. وقولهم: كان صانعا بالقوة أيضاً مشترك، فإن القوة يراد بها الاستعداد المجرد ويراد بها القدرة، والأول غير مسلّم، والثاني مسلّم، وهو محل النزاع ولا يحتاج إلى مخرج إلى الفعل.
As for resolving the second doubt, we say: What do you mean by your statement: 'If He was not a maker and then became a maker, then a matter has newly occurred'? We say: Did something new occur in the essence of the Maker, or did something new occur in something other than His essence? The first is not conceded, and the second is conceded, and it is the point of dispute. And their statement: 'He was a maker in potentiality' is also ambiguous, for 'potentiality' may mean pure receptivity or may mean capability. The first is not conceded, and the second is conceded, and it is the point of dispute, and it does not need an emergence into actuality.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage resolves the second doubt (shubha) concerning the divine attribute of being a maker. The objectors claim that if God was not a maker and then became a maker, something new must have occurred. The author first asks them to clarify what they mean by 'something new occurring.' He then makes a crucial distinction: either something new occurs in God's own essence, or it occurs in something other than His essence. The first is not conceded (for that would imply change in God, which is impossible), while the second is conceded—and this is precisely the point of dispute. Next, the author addresses the objection that God was a maker 'in potentiality.' He notes the term 'potentiality' (quwwa) is ambiguous: it can mean pure receptivity (isti'dād mujarrad) or capability (qudra). The first meaning is not conceded because that would imply imperfection, but the second meaning (capability) is conceded, and that is the point of dispute. The author concludes that the concession of capability does not require any emergence into actuality in God's essence, as the capability is eternal.

Key Terms
شبهة
تجدد
قوة
استعداد مجرد
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God becomes a creator after not being one, something new must have occurred in God's essence.
Correction: The change is external, in creation, not in God's essence. God's capability is eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the point of dispute in the second doubt?
A) Whether something new occurs in God's essence
B) Whether something new occurs in something other than God's essence
C) Whether God is a maker in potentiality
D) Whether capability is different from receptivity

Key Takeaway
When a doubt about God's attributes arises, careful distinction between internal and external change, and precise definition of terms, can resolve the apparent contradiction without compromising divine immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you willing to let go of any subtle assumptions that God might be deficient like His creation? What will change today in how you think about God's attributes?
?
The passage warns against ambiguous terms like 'potentiality' that can be misunderstood. Are there words or concepts in your own understanding of God that you hold loosely without examining their precise meaning? What concrete step will you take to clarify them using the Quran and the scholars' explanations?

Refuting the Third Doubt: Changeless Causes
Refutation of the claim that every changeless cause is a cause by its essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that every unchanging cause is a cause by its essence is false, using the example of the First Intellect and separate intellects.

Core Idea
Changelessness does not make a cause self-sufficient; even unchanging beings depend on Allah.

In this passage, the author refutes a key objection raised by philosophers who argue that if a cause never changes, it must be a cause by its own essence. He shows that this principle collapses when applied to their own First Intellect – an unchanging entity that is nevertheless not a cause by itself. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
The doubt claims that every cause which cannot undergo motion or change is necessarily a cause by virtue of its own essence. Recognize why this matters for divine attributes.
This is a philosophical principle that would affect the relationship between God and creation.
2
Identify the counterexample
The author uses the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) as a counterexample. It is unchanging but not a cause by itself; it depends on the Necessary Existent.
The First Intellect is a key concept in the philosophers' own system.
3
Generalise the principle
The same reasoning applies to the separate intellects; they are unchanging yet not causes by their own essences. Thus the claim is false.
This shows the refutation is not ad hoc but systematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حل الشبهة الثالثة، قال: كل علة لا يجوز عليها التحرك والاستحالة، فإنما تكون علة من جهة ذاتها غير مسلّم، بل هو باطل على أصله بالعقل الأول، فإنه لا يجوز عليه التحرك والاستحالة، وليس علة من جهة ذاته، بل هو معلول الواجب الوجود وعلة من جهة كونه واجبًا به لا بذاته، وكذلك العقول المفارقة لا تقبل الحركة والاستحالة وليست علل من جهة ذواتها.
As for resolving the third doubt, he said: Every cause which cannot undergo motion and change is only a cause by virtue of its own essence? This is not conceded; rather it is false according to his own principle regarding the First Intellect, for it cannot undergo motion and change, yet it is not a cause by virtue of its own essence, but rather it is an effect of the Necessary Existent, and a cause by virtue of being necessary through Him, not through its own essence. And likewise the separate intellects do not accept motion and change, yet they are not causes by virtue of their own essences.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage resolves the third doubt (shubha). The objectors claim that every cause which does not admit motion or change is necessarily a cause by virtue of its own essence (min jihati dhātihā). The author states that this claim is not conceded; rather it is false based on the objector's own principle regarding the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal). The First Intellect is a being that does not admit motion or change, but it is not a cause by its own essence—it is an effect (maʿlūl) of the Necessary Existent, and it causes only because it is necessary through Him (wājib bihi), not through itself. Similarly, the separate intellects (al-ʿuqūl al-mufāraqa) are unchanging but are not causes by their own essences. Thus the general principle is invalid; being unchanging does not entail being a cause by essence.

Key Terms

علة
ذات
العقل الأول
واجب الوجود
العقول المفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Changelessness implies self-sufficiency and being a cause by essence.
Correction: An entity can be unchanging yet dependent on another for its causality, as shown by the First Intellect.

Checkpoint
What is the author's counterexample to the claim that every unchanging cause is a cause by its essence?
A) The human soul
B) The Necessary Existent
C) The First Intellect
D) The four elements

Key Takeaway
The nature of a cause is determined not by its unchangingness but by its dependence on or independence from the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

In what specific area of your life are you acting as if you are a 'cause by essence' rather than a 'cause by another'?
?
The passage shows that being changeless does not mean being independent. What area of your life do you treat as permanent or fixed? Could that very stability be hiding your reliance on Allah? What would change if you truly believed that every constant in your life is sustained by Allah moment by moment?

The First Sunni Proof: Precise Acts Indicate a Knowing Creator
First Ash'arī proof from precise acts, extended to all humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'arī proof that precise actions indicate a Creator with comprehensive knowledge, and why this proof applies to all human agents alike.

Core Idea
Precise design points to a Designer who knows every detail.

The Mu'tazila argued that humans create their own actions. The Ahl al-Sunna countered with a proof based on the obvious precision in the world. Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari formulated this proof using the example of an inattentive person, but the author of Nihāyat al-Aqdām shows that the proof actually applies to everyone. Let's examine how precise acts lead us to a knowing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the logic of the proof unfold naturally.
The passage contains a complete argument, so read it in full.
2
Identify the core reasoning
The proof is: precise actions (itqān) are effects of knowledge. Human knowledge does not encompass all details of an action. Therefore the precision must come from another Agent who has complete knowledge.
Notice the terms 'itqān' and 'ihkām' – precision and soundness.
3
Understand the extension to all humans
The author argues that al-Ash'ari's example of the inattentive person (ghāfil) is not a limitation. Even a learned person lacks total detailed knowledge of every aspect of their own action. So the proof is universal.
This corrects a potential misunderstanding that the proof only works for unconscious actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام على المعتزلة فنذكر أولا طريق أهل السنة في إسناد الكائنات إلى الله سبحانه خلقا وإبداعا. ولهم طريقان في ذلك ؛ أحدهما قولهم : الأفعال المحكمة دالة على علم مخترعها، إذ الإتقان والإحكام من آثار العلم لا محالة، وإذا كان الفعل صادرًا من فاعل متقن فيجب أن يكون من آثار علم ذلك الفاعل، ومن المعلوم أن علم العبد لا يتعلق قط بما يفعله من كل وجه، بل لو علمه علمه من وجه دون وجه علم جملة لا علم تفصيل، فوجوه الإحكام في الفعل لم تدل على علمه، وليست من آثار علمه، فيتعين أن الفاعل غيره، وهو الذي أحاط به علما من كل وجه، وهذه الطريقة هي التي اعتمد عليها الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه وأوردها في كتبه، وفرضها في الغافل إذا صدر عنه فعل. والحق أن الدليل ليس بمختص بالغافل، فإن الغافل كما لم يحط علما بالفعل من كل وجه، كذلك العالم لم يحط به علما من كل وجه، والفاعل الخالق يجب أن يكون محيطا بالفعل من كل وجه؛ إذ الإحكام إنما يثبت فيه من آثار علمه لا آثار علم غيره، ويدل على علمه لا على علم غيره، فكما يستحيل إيجاد الفعل
As for the discussion of the Mu'tazila, we first mention the method of Ahl al-Sunna in attributing existent things to Allah, exalted, as creation and origination. They have two methods in this: one of them is their statement: Precise actions indicate the knowledge of their inventor, for precision and soundness are from the effects of knowledge without doubt. And if an action proceeds from a precise agent, it must be from the effects of that agent's knowledge. It is known that the knowledge of the servant does not pertain at all to what he does from every aspect; rather, if he knows it, he knows it from one aspect without another, knowledge of the whole not detailed knowledge. So the aspects of soundness in the action do not indicate his knowledge, nor are they from the effects of his knowledge. Thus it is determined that the agent is other than him, and He is the One who encompasses it with knowledge from every aspect. This method is the one relied upon by Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him, and he presented it in his books, and he hypothesized it in the case of the inattentive person when an action issues from him. The truth is that the proof is not specific to the inattentive person, for the inattentive person just as he does not encompass the action with knowledge from every aspect, likewise the learned person does not encompass it with knowledge from every aspect. And the Creator agent must be encompassing of the action from every aspect, for soundness is only established in it from the effects of His knowledge, not from the effects of the knowledge of another, and it indicates His knowledge, not the knowledge of another. For just as it is impossible to bring about the action...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two Sunni methods for proving that all created things are acts of God alone, in contrast to the Mu'tazila who attribute some acts to human agency. The argument is based on the observation of precision and soundness (itqān, ihkām) in created things. Such precision is an effect of knowledge. Human beings, whether ignorant or learned, do not have complete detailed knowledge of all aspects of their own actions; they know the general intention but not every detail. Therefore, the precise design in an action cannot be attributed to the human agent. Instead, it must be attributed to an agent who encompasses the action with full knowledge in every aspect—that agent is God. The author notes that Imam al-Ash'ari used this proof, framing it in terms of a person who is inattentive (ghāfil) when an action occurs from him unexpectedly. However, the correct position, the author argues, is that the proof is not restricted to the inattentive person. Even a learned, fully aware human being does not have exhaustive knowledge of every detail of his action. Hence, the proof applies universally. The last sentence begins a further point (the impossibility of a created thing bringing about the action), but it is cut off. Nonetheless, the main refutation of limiting the proof to the ghāfil is complete.

إتقان
إحكام
علم العبد
غافل
فاعل خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only works when a person is inattentive (ghāfil) and does not know their action.
Correction: The author clarifies that even a learned person does not have exhaustive detailed knowledge of every aspect of their action, so the proof applies to all humans equally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the proof from precise actions apply to both the inattentive and the learned person?
A) Because both have no knowledge at all.
B) Because even the learned person does not have complete detailed knowledge of every aspect of his action.
C) Because al-Ash'ari only formulated the proof for the inattentive person.
D) Because the Mu'tazila rejected the proof for the learned.

Key Takeaway
The precision we see in actions cannot come from limited human knowledge, so it must point to a Creator who knows every detail—this proof holds for all human acts, not just unconscious ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

After reading this lesson, can you honestly say that you had comprehensive knowledge of every detail that led to that outcome? How does this truth change the way you will thank Allah for your successes from now on?

This lesson shows that even your errors are under Allah's comprehensive knowledge and that all precise aspects come from Him. How does this shift your understanding of personal responsibility and reliance on Allah? What would it mean for you to live each day acknowledging that every outcome—good or bad—is ultimately from the All-Knowing Creator?
?
The proof argues that no human, no matter how learned or attentive, can claim full authorship of any action's precision. Does this humble your pride in your own abilities? Are you willing to honestly examine your heart and root out the assumption that your successes are your own doing? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself that every precise moment of your life is a direct gift from Allah?

God's Knowledge Is Passive and Follows the Known
Conclusion that divine knowledge is passive and follows the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge is passive and follows the known object, contrary to practical knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge conforms to reality; it does not create it.

This passage concludes an earlier discussion by clarifying a fundamental distinction: God's knowledge is not practical like human know-how. Rather, it is passive, meaning it simply mirrors what is known. Theologians unanimously agreed that knowledge follows the known, attaching to it without altering it. Let us examine the source text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once with its English translation. Notice the key terms: 'ilm fi'li (practical knowledge) and 'ilm infi'ali (passive knowledge).
The word 'Sirr' at the end may be a section marker.
2
Identify the distinction
Focus on the phrase 'lan yutasarra an yakuna 'ilman fi'liyyan' — His knowledge cannot be conceived as practical. Contrast this with 'ulūmuhu kulluhā infi'āliyya' — all His knowledges are passive. Ask: What does it mean for knowledge to be passive versus practical?
Practical knowledge involves bringing something into existence; passive knowledge just perceives what exists.
3
Understand the consensus
Read the statement that all mutakallimun agreed that knowledge follows the known (al-'ilm yatba' al-ma'lum). Note that this means knowledge does not bestow a quality on the object nor acquire one from it. Reflect on why this is important for Islamic theology.
This principle prevents attributing change or limitation to God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإرادة والدواعي والآلات والأدوات ؛ لأن علمه لن يتصور أن يكون علما فعليا بل علومه كلها انفعالية، ولذلك اتفق المتكلمون بأسرهم على أن العلم يتبع المعلوم فيتعلق به على ما هو به ولا يكسبه صفةً، ولا يكتسب عنه صفةً، وهذا هو هذه الطريقة ونهايتها. سر
And will, motives, instruments, and tools; because His knowledge cannot be conceived to be practical knowledge, but all His knowledges are passive, and therefore all the mutakallimun agreed that knowledge follows the known, so it attaches to it as it is, and does not bestow upon it a quality, nor does it acquire a quality from it, and this is this method and its end. Sirr.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark from an earlier discussion. The author states that attributes such as will (al-irada), motives (ad-dawa'i), instruments (al-alat), and tools (al-adawat) are not applicable to God in a direct sense because His knowledge cannot be practical ('ilman fi'liyyan) but rather all His knowledge is passive (infi'aliyya). This is a key distinction: human knowledge can be practical (e.g., knowing how to make something) but God's knowledge is not like that; it is simply awareness without being a cause. The author then notes that all the mutakallimun (theologians) agree that knowledge follows the known (al-'ilm yatba' al-ma'lum). This means knowledge conforms to its object; it does not change the object or acquire a new quality from it. The sentence 'This is this method and its end' (wahuwa hadhihi al-tariqa wa nihayatuha) signals that the author has completed a certain line of argument. The word 'Sirr' at the end may indicate a section break or a point of subtlety.

Key Terms
علم فعلي
علم انفعالى
يتبع المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's knowledge is active like human practical knowledge, creating or altering the known.
Correction: This passage explicitly denies that God's knowledge can be practical; it is entirely passive, conforming to the known without bestowing or acquiring qualities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is passive and follows the known, a point of consensus among theologians.

Al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani on the Effect of Created Power
Positions of al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani on the effect of created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views of al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani on whether created power has any effect, and why both avoid the charge of arbitrary specification.

Core Idea
Created power has no effect on existence itself, according to the Ash'ari school.

This lesson contrasts the positions of two major Ash'ari thinkers. While both reject the idea that created power directly affects existence, they differ on whether it affects anything at all. Understanding this nuance is key to later arguments about the scope of human ability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, then al-Ash'ari's position, then al-Baqillani's.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The passage begins with the opponent asserting that it is impossible to restrict the capacity of power to some existents. Recognize why this would be a problem for the Ash'ari view.
3
Analyze al-Ash'ari's position
Al-Ash'ari denies any capacity (ṣalāḥiyya) to created power, neither for existence nor any attribute. This completely avoids the charge of specification.
4
Analyze al-Baqillani's position
Al-Baqillani affirms an effect for created power, but exempts the aspect of existence (jiha al-wujūd) from that effect. Thus he also avoids the charge of generalization.
5
Compare and contrast
Reflect on how both positions are compatible with Ash'ari theology despite their difference. Both maintain that existence itself is solely from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل موجود واستحال على أصلكم حصر صلاحية القدرة في بعض الموجودات دون البعض. ولم يثبت شيخنا أبو الحسن رحمه الله للقدرة الحادثة صلاحية. صلاحية أصلا لا لجهة الوجود ولا لصفة من صفات الوجود، فلم يلزمه التعميم والتخصيص، وأما القاضي أبو بكر فقد أثبت لها أثرًا - كما سنذكره - ولكنه يبرئ جهة الوجود عن التأثير فيه، فلم يلزمه التعميم.
Every existent, and it is impossible according to your principle to restrict the capacity of power to some existents without others. Our master Abu al-Hasan (may God have mercy on him) did not prove for the temporally originated power any capacity at all, neither for the aspect of existence nor for any attribute of existence, so he was not forced into generalization and specification. As for al-Qadi Abu Bakr, he proved for it an effect — as we will mention — but he exempts the aspect of existence from having an effect on it, so he was not forced into generalization.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrinal positions of the two Ash'ari authorities, al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani, regarding the effect of created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). It begins by stating the opponent's claim that it is impossible to restrict power's capacity to some existents—a point the author has been arguing against. Then it distinguishes: al-Ash'ari denied any effect for created power, neither on the aspect of existence (jiha al-wujūd) nor on any attribute of existence, so he is not forced into the charge of arbitrary specification (takhṣīṣ). Al-Baqillani, by contrast, affirmed an effect, but he exempts the aspect of existence from being affected, so he also avoids the charge of generalization (ta'mīm). This sets the stage for al-Baqillani's proof that follows.

B) Al-Ash'ari denies any effect; al-Baqillani affirms an effect but not on existence.

Both al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani reject that created power affects existence itself, though they differ on whether it affects anything else.

Al-Baqillani's Defense of the Unknown State
Al-Baqillani defends his unknown state by analogy to rational aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani defends the intelligibility of a state (ḥāl) that lacks a name by using rational proof and analogy to other accepted attributes in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A state is known by proof and analogy, not by its name.

Al-Baqillani's students object that his posited state is unknown because it has no name. In response, he argues that rational proof and accepted analogies from other attributes make it intelligible. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how he turns his opponents' own acceptance of similar states against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection that the state is 'unknown' because it lacks a name and meaning.
2
Identify al-Baqillani's three-part defense
Look for his three moves: (1) the state is known by proof despite lacking a name, (2) analogy to rational aspects of a single act, (3) appeal to opponents' own acceptance of similar attributes.
These three parts form the core of his argument.
3
Examine the analogies
Note the examples: occurrence (from power), specification (from will), perfection (from knowledge). Then the opponents' acceptance of moral and essential attributes as states additional to existence.
This shows the analogy is consistent with kalam methodology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this defense reinforces the kalam concept of aḥwāl (states) as rationally intelligible even without independent names.
This lesson establishes a key methodological principle in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فألزمه أصحابه أنك أثبت حالا مجهولة لا ندري ما هي؛ إذ لا اسم لها ولا معنى. قال : بل هي معلومة بالدليل والتقسيم الذي أرشدنا إليه كما بينا، فإن لم تتيسر لي عبارة عنها باسم خاص لم يضر ذلك، ألسنا أثبتنا وجوها واعتبارات عقلية للفعل الواحد، وأضفنا كل وجه إلى صفة أثرت فيه مثل الوقوع فإنه من آثار القدرة، والتخصيص ببعض الجايزات فإنه من آثار الإرادة والإحكام فإنه من دلائل العلم، وعند الخصم كون الفعل واجبًا أو ندبًا أو حلالا أو حراما أو حسنًا أو قبيحاً صفات زائدة على وجوده، بعضها ذاتية للفعل وبعضها من آثار الإرادة، وكذلك الصفات التابعة للحدوث مثل كون الجوهر متحيزا وقابلا للعرض، فإذا جاز عنده إثبات صفات هي أحوال واعتبارات زائدة على الوجود لا تتعلق بها القادرية وهي معقولة ومفهومة فكيف يستبعد مني إثبات أثر للقدرة الحادثة معقولا ومفهومًا.
Then his followers compelled him [by saying]: 'You have posited an unknown state that we do not know what it is, for it has neither a name nor a meaning.' He said: 'Rather, it is known by the proof and the division to which we have guided, as we have shown. If I do not have an expression for it by a specific name, that does no harm. Have we not posited rational aspects and considerations for a single act, and attributed each aspect to an attribute that affects it, such as occurrence being from the effects of power, specification with some possibilities being from the effects of will, and perfection being from the indications of knowledge? And according to the opponent, the act being obligatory, recommendable, permissible, forbidden, good, or evil are attributes additional to its existence, some of them essential to the act and some from the effects of will. Likewise, the attributes dependent on temporality, such as the substance being spatially extended and capable of receiving accidents. So if he allows the positing of attributes that are states and considerations additional to existence that the power does not attach to, and they are intelligible and understood, then how can he consider it far-fetched for me to posit an effect for the temporally originated power that is intelligible and understood?'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents an objection raised by al-Baqillani's own followers and his response. The followers object that he has posited an unknown state (ḥāl majhūla) with no name or meaning, implying it is unintelligible. Al-Baqillani responds in three parts. First, he asserts that the state is known by the rational proof and division he provided, even if it lacks a specific name; the absence of a name is not a problem. Second, he draws an analogy: we routinely posit multiple rational aspects (wujūh) for a single act and attribute each to a different faculty, e.g., occurrence to power, specification to will, perfection to knowledge. These are accepted even though they are not named separately. Third, he points to the opponent's own acceptance of attributes over and above existence, such as moral values (obligation, prohibition, etc.) and essential attributes like substance being extended and receptive to accidents. If the opponent accepts such 'states and considerations' (aḥwāl wa iʿtibārāt) that are intelligible yet not tied to power, then it is inconsistent to reject al-Baqillani's positing of an effect for created power that is equally intelligible. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
الحال المجهولة
الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية
آثار القدرة
آثار الإرادة
دلائل العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A state that has no name is completely unknowable and cannot be rationally discussed.
Correction: Al-Baqillani argues that a state can be known by rational proof and analogy, even without a specific name. Many accepted attributes in kalam are likewise unnamed but intelligible.

Checkpoint
How does al-Baqillani respond to the objection that the state he posits is unknown?
A) He admits it is unknown and abandons his position.
B) He says it is known by rational proof and analogy to other recognized attributes.
C) He claims it is known by divine revelation.
D) He redefines it as an attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani proves that a logically inferred state is known even without a specific name, using rational proof and accepted analogies in kalam.

In light of al-Baqillani's argument, what unnamed reality might you be dismissing from your relationship with Allah today? Will you commit to investigating it through proof and reflection rather than relying on terms you already know?
?

Defending the 'State' (Hal): Known by Proof, Not by Name
Author defends the concept of hal against the charge of being unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defends the concept of a 'state' (ḥāl) as a rationally derived attribute of an action, even without a specific name.

Core Idea
A state need not have a name, as long as it is known by proof.

In this passage, the author responds to the objection that the 'state' (ḥāl) attributed to the servant's power is an unknown entity with no name or meaning. He argues that rational analysis of acts yields intelligible aspects (wujūh) that are well-understood, even if not given a specific label. This lesson walks through the objection and the author's three-part defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's initial claim that God's power only works in existence, and the challenge from his companions that the Ash'ari has affirmed an unknown state with no name or meaning.
The opponent tries to force a dilemma: only one state (origination) remains, and it cannot be known.
2
Identify the author's response
Notice that the author does not retreat but rather asserts that the state is known by proof and rational division, not by arbitrary naming.
The proof is based on distinguishing multiple aspects (wujūh) of a single act.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives three examples: occurrence (from power), specification (from will), and precision (from knowledge). These aspects are intelligible and attributed to different divine attributes.
Each aspect is a different 'state' of the act.
4
Analyze the analogy
The author points out that the opponent himself admits attributes like moral categories (obligatory, good, etc.) and spatial attributes (substance being spatial) as additional to existence.
If the opponent accepts those, he should accept the Ash'ari's state for the servant's power.
5
Conclude the consistency
The author's argument shows that the opponent's objection is inconsistent: he accepts similar rational attributes but rejects the state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخصم قادرية الباري سبحانه لم تؤثر إلا في حال هو الوجود ؛ لأنه أثبت في العدم ساير صفات الأجناس ؛ من الشيئية والجوهرية والعرضية والكونية واللونية إلى أخص الصفات؛ من الحركات والسكون والسوادية والبياضية، فلم يبق سوى حالة واحدة هي الحدوث، فليأخذ الحدوث، فليأخذ مني في قدرة العبد مثله. فألزمه أصحابه أنك أثبت حالا مجهولة لا ندري ما هي ؛ إذ لا اسم لها ولا معنى.
قال : بل هي معلومة بالدليل والتقسيم الذي أرشدنا إليه كما بينا، فإن لم تتيسر لي عبارة عنها باسم خاص لم يضر ذلك، ألسنا أثبتنا وجوها واعتبارات عقلية للفعل الواحد، وأضفنا كل وجه إلى صفة أثرت فيه مثل الوقوع فإنه من آثار القدرة، والتخصيص ببعض الجايزات فإنه من آثار الإرادة والإحكام فإنه من دلائل العلم ، وعند الخصم كون الفعل واجبًا أو ندبًا أو حلالا أو حراما أو حسنًا أو قبيحاً صفات زائدة على وجوده، بعضها ذاتية للفعل وبعضها من آثار الإرادة ، وكذلك الصفات التابعة للحدوث مثل كون الجوهر متحيزا وقابلا للعرض، فإذا جاز عنده إثبات صفات هي أحوال واعتبارات زائدة على الوجود لا تتعلق بها القادرية وهي معقولة ومفهومة فكيف يستبعد مني إثبات أثر للقدرة الحادثة معقولا ومفهومًا.
The opponent [claims]: The power of the Exalted God was not effective except in the state of existence; because he has affirmed in nonexistence the rest of the attributes of genera: from thingness, substantiality, accidentality, spatiality, color-ness, to the most specific attributes, from motions, rest, blackness, whiteness. So there remains only one state, which is origination (al-huduth). So let him take origination, and let him take from me in the power of the slave its like. So his companions obliged him [saying]: You have affirmed an unknown state which we do not know what it is, for it has no name and no meaning.
He said: Rather it is known by the proof and the division to which we guided, as we explained. If I do not have a ready expression for it by a specific name, that does not harm. Have we not affirmed aspects (wujuh) and rational considerations for the single act, and attributed each aspect to a property (sifa) that produced it, such as occurrence (al-wuqu‘) for it is from the effects of power, specification (al-takhsis) by some possibilities for it is from the effects of will, and precision (al-ihkam) for it is from the indications of knowledge. And according to the opponent, the fact that an act is obligatory, recommended, permissible, forbidden, good, or evil are attributes additional to its existence, some essential to the act and some from the effects of will. And likewise the attributes subsidiary to origination, such as that a substance (jawhar) is spatial (mutahayyiz) and receptive of accidents. So if it is permissible for him to affirm attributes that are states (ahwal) and considerations additional to existence which power does not relate to, and they are intelligible and understood, then how does he consider it far-fetched for me to affirm an effect (athar) of originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) that is intelligible and understood?
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent (al-khasm) begins by arguing that God's power only produces effects in the state of existence, not in nonexistence. The opponent then lists various attributes that God has in nonexistence (like thingness, substantiality, etc.) and claims that only one state remains: origination (al-huduth). The opponent challenges the Ash‘ari to take this state as the effect of the servant's power. The Ash‘ari's companions then object: 'You have affirmed an unknown state (hal) with no name or meaning.' The author replies in three steps. First, the state is known by proof and rational division, not by arbitrary naming. Second, he gives examples: for any single act, we can distinguish different aspects (wujuh) like occurrence (from power), specification (from will), and precision (from knowledge). Third, he compares this to the opponent's own admission of attributes like moral categories (obligatory, good, etc.) which are additional to existence. If the opponent allows such rational attributes, he should also allow the Ash‘ari's state for the servant's power. The conclusion is that the opponent's objection is inconsistent.

قدرة (qudra)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

اعتبارات (iʿtibārāt)

Correction: The passage shows it is derived from rational division and analogy to accepted attributes.

B) A proof and rational division using examples of aspects of acts
C) The opponent's admission that all attributes are purely nominal
D) The consensus of early Muslims

Key Takeaway
The state (ḥāl) is not an arbitrary invention but is rationally deduced from the various aspects (wujūh) of an action, which are intelligible even without a specific name.

Distinguishing Creation from Acquisition via Aspects
First explanation of the distinction between divine creation and human acquisition using mental aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari concept of mental aspects distinguishes divine creation from human acquisition.

Core Idea
Actions have general aspects from Allah and specific aspects from humans.

In this passage, the author clarifies the Ash'ari position on human acts by analyzing the mental aspects of an action. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction between divine omnipotence and human responsibility. You are about to read a technical exposition that uses the terms 'general aspects' and 'specific aspects' to separate creation from acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms used.
2
Identify the two types of aspects
Look for general aspects (like existence) and specific aspects (like being writing). Understand how they differ.
Aspects are mental considerations, not physical parts.
3
Attribute each aspect to its agent
See how general aspects come from the Creator and specific aspects come from the human agent. This is the key distinction.
4
Note the implications for the agents
The Creator's essence does not change, but the human's does. Also, the Creator knows all aspects, while the human need not.
This highlights the difference between divine creation and human acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوضح طريقة القاضي ويبين أنه ما خالف الأصحاب تلك المخالفة البعيدة أن الفعل ذو جهات عقلية واعتبارات ذهنية عامة وخاصة؛ كالوجود والحدوث والعرضية واللونية وكونه حركة أو سكونًا، وكون الحركة كتابة أو قولًا، وليس الفعل بذاته شيئًا من هذه الوجوه، بل هي كلها مستفادة له من الفاعل، والذي له بذاته هو الإمكان فقط، وأما وجوده فمستفاد من موجده على الوجه الذي هو به وهو أعم الوجوه، وأما كونه كتابةً أو قولًا فمستفاد من كاتبه أو قائله وهو أخص الوجوه، فيتميز الوجهان تميزًا عقليًا لا حسيا وتغاير المتعلقان أحدهما إيجادًا وإبداعًا، وهو نسبة أعم الوجوه إلى صفة لها عموم التعلق، وسمي الثاني كسبًا وفعلاً، وهو نسبة أخص الوجوه إلى صفة لها خصوص التعلق، فهو من حيث وجوده يحتاج إلى موجد، ومن حيث الكتابة والقول يحتاج إلى كاتب وقائل، والموجد لا تتغير ذاته أو صفته لوجود الموجد، ويشترط كونه عالمًا بجميع جهات الفعل، والمكتسب تتغير ذاته وصفته لحصول الكسب، ولا يشترط كونه عالمًا بجميع جهات الفعل.
And from what clarifies the method of al-Qadi and shows that he did not contradict the companions in that distant disagreement is that the act has rational aspects and mental considerations, general and specific, such as existence, origination, accidentalness, color-ness, and its being motion or rest, and the motion’s being writing or speech. The act in itself is not any of these aspects; rather, they are all derived from the agent. That which belongs to it in itself is only possibility. As for its existence, it is derived from its bringer-into-existence in the manner in which it is, and that is the most general of aspects. As for its being writing or speech, it is derived from its writer or speaker, and that is the most specific of aspects. So the two aspects are distinguished rationally, not sensibly, and the two objects of relation are different: one is bringing-into-existence and originating, and it is the relation of the most general aspect to an attribute that has generality of relation; the second is called acquisition and act, and it is the relation of the most specific aspect to an attribute that has specificity of relation. So it, in terms of its existence, needs a bringer-into-existence, and in terms of writing and speech, it needs a writer and speaker. The bringer-into-existence’s essence or attribute does not change due to the existence of the brought-into-existence, and it is required that he be knowing of all aspects of the act. The acquirer’s essence and attribute change due to the occurrence of acquisition, and it is not required that he be knowing of all aspects of the act.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the distinction between divine creation (khalq) and human acquisition (kasb) based on the concept of aspects (wujuh) of an action. The author explains that every action has multiple rational and mental aspects, some general (such as existence, origination, accidentalness) and some specific (such as being writing or speech). The action in itself is not identical to any of these aspects; it only has the property of possibility (imkan) in itself. The general aspects (like existence) are derived from the Creator (mujid), while the specific aspects (like being writing) are derived from the human agent (katib or qa'il). Thus, the two aspects are distinguished rationally, not sensibly. The author then correlates: the first relation (the general aspect) is called creation (ijad) and is attributed to an attribute that has general scope (the divine power); the second relation (the specific aspect) is called acquisition (kasb) and is attributed to an attribute that has specific scope (the human power). Consequently, the act needs a Creator for its existence, and a human agent for its specific qualities. The Creator's essence and attributes do not change when He creates, and He must know all aspects of the act. In contrast, the human acquirer changes through the act, and need not know all aspects.

جهات عقلية
وجود
إمكان
إيجاد
كسب
موجد
مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both existence and specific qualities of an act come from the same agent.
Correction: The passage shows existence is from Allah, while specific qualities like writing are from the human.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the aspect that belongs to the act in itself?
A) Existence
B) Possibility (imkan)
C) Motion
D) Writing

Key Takeaway
The mental aspects of an action allow for both divine creation and human acquisition to coexist without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage teaches that your essence and attributes change through every act you acquire (كسب). Think of one habit you have—good or bad—that is shaping who you are. What concrete step will you take today to acquire a better specific aspect in that area?
?
Since you are not required to know all aspects of your actions, but Allah does, how can you trust Him with outcomes beyond your knowledge? What is one worry you can hand over to Him right now, knowing He handles the general aspects?

The Asymmetry of Divine and Human Power
Argument from scope: divine vs. human power capabilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scope of divine power is universal and human power is specific, and why these two cannot be interchanged.

Core Idea
God's power is uniform and universal; human power is varied and restricted.

The author presents a second argument to distinguish creation from acquisition. By contrasting the capabilities of eternal and temporal power, he establishes a reciprocal asymmetry: what is specific cannot be attributed to God, and what is universal cannot be attributed to humans. This sets the stage for the official definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the passage once to understand the flow. Note the key terms: eternal power (قدرة أزلية) and temporal power (قدرة حادثة).
The author uses a logical contrast to drive the point.
2
Identify the two powers and their capabilities
Find how the author describes the capability (صلاحية) of each power. Eternal power cannot be restricted; temporal power cannot be universal.
The temporal power is limited to 'specific motions'.
3
Understand the asymmetry principle
The author argues that attributing specific acts (like writing) to God would imply deficiency, and attributing universal acts (like creating) to humans would imply perfection. Hence, such attributions are invalid.
This is the core of the distinction: what belongs to the Creator cannot belong to the creature, and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان آخر نقول: جلت القدرة الأزلية عن أن يكون لها صلاحية مخصوصة مقصورة على وجوه من الفعل مخصوصة، وقصرت القدرة الحادثة عن أن يكون لها صلاحية عامة شاملة لجميع وجوه الفعل، وذلك أن صلاحية القدرة الحادثة لم تشمل جميع الموجودات بالاتفاق، فلا تصلح لإيجاد الجوهر وكل عرض، بل هي مقصورة على حركات مخصوصة، والقدرة الحادثة فيها مختلفة الصلاحية حتى يمكن أن يقدر نوعية مخصوصة في القدرة الحادثة لتنوع الصلاحية، فلذلك اقتصرت على بعض الموجودات دون البعض بخلاف قدرة الباري سبحانه فإن صلاحيتها واحدة لا تختلف فيجب أن يكون متعلقها واحدا لا يختلف، وذلك هو الوجود. فإذا لم يجز أن يضاف أخص الأوصاف إلى الباري سبحانه؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى قصوره في الصلاحية، كذلك لا يجوز أن يضاف أعم الأوصاف إلى القدرة الحادثة؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى كمال في الصلاحية، فلا ذاك الكمال مسلوب عن القدرة الإلهية، ولا هذا الكمال ثابت للقدرة الحادثة، فينعم النظر فيه إذ فيه خلاص، فلا يجوز أن يضاف إلى الموجد ما يضاف إلى المكتسب حتى يقال: هو الكاتب القائل القاعد القائم، ولا يجوز أن يضاف إلى المكتسب ما يضاف إلى الموجد حتى يقال: هو الموحد المبدع الخالق الرازق. هذا كان الجواب المرتضى عند التمييز بين الخلق والكسب، أن الخلق هو الموجود بإيجاد الموجد ويلزمه حكم وشرط. أما الحكم فأن لا يتغير الموجد بالإيجاد فيكسبه صفة ولا يكتسب عنه صفة. وأما الشرط أن يكون عالما به من كل وجه، والكسب هو المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، ويلزمه حكم وشرط. أما الحكم فأن يتغير المكتسب بالكسب فيكسبه صفة ويكتسب عنه صفة. وأما الشرط فأن لا يشترط كونه عالما به من كل وجه.
And another exposition: we say: The eternal power is too exalted to have a specific capability restricted to specific aspects of the act, and the temporal power is too limited to have a general capability encompassing all aspects of the act. This is because the capability of the temporal power does not encompass all existents by agreement, so it is not fit for bringing into existence the substance and every accident, but rather it is restricted to specific motions. And the temporal power has varying capability such that a specific type can be posited in it due to the variation of capability. Therefore it is restricted to some existents rather than others, unlike the power of the Exalted Creator, for its capability is one and does not vary, so its object must be one and not vary, and that is existence. So if it is not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes to the Exalted Creator, because it leads to deficiency in His capability, likewise it is not permissible to attribute the most general attributes to the temporal power, because it leads to perfection in capability. Thus neither is that perfection absent from the divine power, nor is this perfection established for the temporal power. So reflect deeply on this, for therein is deliverance. So it is not permissible to attribute to the Creator what is attributed to the acquirer, such that it is said: He is the writer, speaker, sitter, stander. Nor is it permissible to attribute to the acquirer what is attributed to the Creator, such that it is said: He is the unifier, innovator, creator, provider. This was the approved answer concerning the distinction between creation and acquisition: that creation is the existent by the bringing-into-existence of the Creator, and it entails a ruling and a condition. As for the ruling: that the Creator does not change by the bringing-into-existence, so that He acquires nor is He acquired from a quality. As for the condition: that He be knowing of it in every aspect. And acquisition is that which is empowered by the temporal power, and it entails a ruling and a condition. As for the ruling: that the acquirer changes by the acquisition, so that he acquires a quality and acquires from it a quality. As for the condition: that he is not required to be knowing of it in every aspect.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a second argument distinguishing divine creation (khalq) from human acquisition (kasb), followed by a concise statement of the approved answer. The author starts by contrasting the eternal power (al-qudra al-azaliyya) and the temporal power (al-qudra al-haditha). The eternal power cannot have a specific capability limited to particular aspects, while the temporal power cannot have a universal capability covering all aspects. The temporal power is restricted to specific motions and varies in its capacity, so it can only affect some things, unlike God's power which is uniform and has existence as its single object. Therefore, it is not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes (like writing, speaking) to God, as that would imply deficiency in His power. Similarly, it is not permissible to attribute the most general attributes (like existence) to the temporal power, as that would imply perfection in it. This establishes a clear boundary: what belongs to the Creator (the general aspects) cannot be attributed to the creature, and vice versa. The author then presents the approved answer: creation (khalq) is the product of the Creator's bringing-into-existence, with the ruling that the Creator does not change by the act (He acquires no new attribute) and the condition that He knows it from every aspect. Acquisition (kasb) is the product of the temporal power, with the ruling that the acquirer changes (he acquires an attribute) and the condition that he need not know the act from every aspect.

Key Terms
قدرة أزلية
قدرة حادثة
صلاحية
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power and human power have the same scope and that the same acts can be attributed to both.
Correction: The passage shows that God's power is universal and uniform, while human power is specific and varied. Thus, specific acts like writing cannot be attributed to God, and universal acts like creating cannot be attributed to humans.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes (e.g., writing) to God?
A) Because it would imply that God's power is limited
B) Because God does not write
C) Because writing is a specifically human action

Key Takeaway
Divine power is universal and uniform; human power is specific and varied; they cannot be interchanged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Creation and Acquisition
Approved definitions of creation and acquisition with rulings and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal definitions of creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb), along with their respective rulings and conditions.

Core Idea
Creation: the Creator remains unchanged; acquisition: the acquirer changes.

After the argument from scope, the author now presents the approved answer. He defines creation as the product of divine power and acquisition as the product of human power, each with a distinct ruling and condition. This provides a precise theological vocabulary for distinguishing divine and human agency in acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the part that defines creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb). Notice that each is followed by a ruling (حكم) and a condition (شرط).
The definitions are concise and symmetrical.
2
Compare the rulings
The ruling for creation states that the Creator does not change by the act; He acquires no new attribute. The ruling for acquisition states that the acquirer changes and acquires a quality.
This highlights the difference in the agent's relation to the act.
3
Compare the conditions
The condition for creation requires that the Creator know the act from every aspect. The condition for acquisition does not require such comprehensive knowledge.
Divine knowledge is complete; human knowledge is limited.
4
Appreciate the distinction
Reflect on how these definitions complement the earlier argument. Creation is inherently transcendent, while acquisition involves change in the human agent.
This completes the theological framework of khalq and kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان آخر نقول: جلت القدرة الأزلية عن أن يكون لها صلاحية مخصوصة مقصورة على وجوه من الفعل مخصوصة، وقصرت القدرة الحادثة عن أن يكون لها صلاحية عامة شاملة لجميع وجوه الفعل، وذلك أن صلاحية القدرة الحادثة لم تشمل جميع الموجودات بالاتفاق، فلا تصلح لإيجاد الجوهر وكل عرض، بل هي مقصورة على حركات مخصوصة، والقدرة الحادثة فيها مختلفة الصلاحية حتى يمكن أن يقدر نوعية مخصوصة في القدرة الحادثة لتنوع الصلاحية، فلذلك اقتصرت على بعض الموجودات دون البعض بخلاف قدرة الباري سبحانه فإن صلاحيتها واحدة لا تختلف فيجب أن يكون متعلقها واحدا لا يختلف، وذلك هو الوجود. فإذا لم يجز أن يضاف أخص الأوصاف إلى الباري سبحانه؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى قصوره في الصلاحية، كذلك لا يجوز أن يضاف أعم الأوصاف إلى القدرة الحادثة؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى كمال في الصلاحية، فلا ذاك الكمال مسلوب عن القدرة الإلهية، ولا هذا الكمال ثابت للقدرة الحادثة، فينعم النظر فيه إذ فيه خلاص، فلا يجوز أن يضاف إلى الموجد ما يضاف إلى المكتسب حتى يقال: هو الكاتب القائل القاعد القائم، ولا يجوز أن يضاف إلى المكتسب ما يضاف إلى الموجد حتى يقال: هو الموحد المبدع الخالق الرازق. هذا كان الجواب المرتضى عند التمييز بين الخلق والكسب، أن الخلق هو الموجود بإيجاد الموجد ويلزمه حكم وشرط. أما الحكم فأن لا يتغير الموجد بالإيجاد فيكسبه صفة ولا يكتسب عنه صفة. وأما الشرط أن يكون عالما به من كل وجه، والكسب هو المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، ويلزمه حكم وشرط. أما الحكم فأن يتغير المكتسب بالكسب فيكسبه صفة ويكتسب عنه صفة. وأما الشرط فأن لا يشترط كونه عالما به من كل وجه.
And another exposition: we say: The eternal power is too exalted to have a specific capability restricted to specific aspects of the act, and the temporal power is too limited to have a general capability encompassing all aspects of the act. This is because the capability of the temporal power does not encompass all existents by agreement, so it is not fit for bringing into existence the substance and every accident, but rather it is restricted to specific motions. And the temporal power has varying capability such that a specific type can be posited in it due to the variation of capability. Therefore it is restricted to some existents rather than others, unlike the power of the Exalted Creator, for its capability is one and does not vary, so its object must be one and not vary, and that is existence. So if it is not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes to the Exalted Creator, because it leads to deficiency in His capability, likewise it is not permissible to attribute the most general attributes to the temporal power, because it leads to perfection in capability. Thus neither is that perfection absent from the divine power, nor is this perfection established for the temporal power. So reflect deeply on this, for therein is deliverance. So it is not permissible to attribute to the Creator what is attributed to the acquirer, such that it is said: He is the writer, speaker, sitter, stander. Nor is it permissible to attribute to the acquirer what is attributed to the Creator, such that it is said: He is the unifier, innovator, creator, provider. This was the approved answer concerning the distinction between creation and acquisition: that creation is the existent by the bringing-into-existence of the Creator, and it entails a ruling and a condition. As for the ruling: that the Creator does not change by the bringing-into-existence, so that He acquires nor is He acquired from a quality. As for the condition: that He be knowing of it in every aspect. And acquisition is that which is empowered by the temporal power, and it entails a ruling and a condition. As for the ruling: that the acquirer changes by the acquisition, so that he acquires a quality and acquires from it a quality. As for the condition: that he is not required to be knowing of it in every aspect.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a second argument distinguishing divine creation (khalq) from human acquisition (kasb), followed by a concise statement of the approved answer. The author starts by contrasting the eternal power (al-qudra al-azaliyya) and the temporal power (al-qudra al-haditha). The eternal power cannot have a specific capability limited to particular aspects, while the temporal power cannot have a universal capability covering all aspects. The temporal power is restricted to specific motions and varies in its capacity, so it can only affect some things, unlike God's power which is uniform and has existence as its single object. Therefore, it is not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes (like writing, speaking) to God, as that would imply deficiency in His power. Similarly, it is not permissible to attribute the most general attributes (like existence) to the temporal power, as that would imply perfection in it. This establishes a clear boundary: what belongs to the Creator (the general aspects) cannot be attributed to the creature, and vice versa. The author then presents the approved answer: creation (khalq) is the product of the Creator's bringing-into-existence, with the ruling that the Creator does not change by the act (He acquires no new attribute) and the condition that He knows it from every aspect. Acquisition (kasb) is the product of the temporal power, with the ruling that the acquirer changes (he acquires an attribute) and the condition that he need not know the act from every aspect.

Key Terms
خلق
كسب
حكم
شرط
إيجاد
مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both creation and acquisition involve change in the agent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that creation involves no change in the Creator, while acquisition involves change in the human agent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling (حكم) for acquisition (كسب)?
A) The acquirer does not change
B) The acquirer changes and acquires a quality
C) The acquirer must know the act from every aspect
D) The acquirer does not need to perform the act

Key Takeaway
Creation: Creator unchanged, knows all; acquisition: acquirer changes, knows limited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you reflect that it changes you – either for good or ill – and that you are not fully aware of its every consequence? How does this realization affect your daily intentions and efforts?
?
If creation requires God's perfect knowledge of every detail, how does that transform your trust in His decree, especially in times of hardship or confusion?
?
Since you are not required to know everything about your actions, but only to act with the knowledge He has granted, are you content with your limited understanding and still striving within that? Or do you find yourself paralyzed by what you don't know?

The Distinction Between Specific and General Attributes
Argument against mixing divine and human attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specific human attributes (like writing) cannot be attributed to God, and why general divine attributes (like creating) cannot be attributed to humans, based on the nature of divine and temporal power.

Core Idea
Divine and human attributes are distinct; mixing them leads to theological error.

This passage from al-Juwayni's Nihayat al-Aqdam presents a key argument about attribute attribution. It uses the concepts of 'most specific' and 'most general' attributes to show that neither can be transferred between God and creation. Understanding this distinction preserves divine transcendence and avoids anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the contrast between divine power (قدرة الباري) and temporal power (القدرة الحادثة).
Pay attention to the logical flow: it moves from a general principle to specific prohibitions.
2
Understand the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: divine power is uniform → its object must be existence alone → thus specific attributes imply deficiency in divine power → and general attributes imply perfection in temporal power → so they cannot be exchanged.
3
Apply the distinction to examples
Reflect on the examples: why we cannot say God 'writes' in a human sense, and why humans cannot be called 'creators' in the divine sense.
Think about how this rule protects both divine transcendence and human limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض الموجودات دون البعض بخلاف قدرة الباري سبحانه فإن صلاحيتها واحدة لا تختلف فيجب أن يكون متعلقها واحدا لا يختلف، وذلك هو الوجود. فإذا لم يجز أن يضاف أخص الأوصاف إلى الباري سبحانه لأنه يؤدي إلى قصوره في الصلاحية، كذلك لا يجوز أن يضاف أعم الأوصاف إلى القدرة الحادثة لأنه يؤدي إلى كمال في الصلاحية، فلا ذاك الكمال مسلوب عن القدرة الإلهية، ولا هذا الكمال ثابت للقدرة الحادثة، فينعم النظر فيه الأن فيه خلاص، فلا يجوز أن يضاف إلى الموجد ما يضاف إلى المكتسب حتى يقال: هو الكاتب القائل القاعد القايم، ولا يجوز أن يضاف إلى المكتسب ما يضاف إلى الموجد حتى يقال: هو الموحد المبدع الخالق الرازق.
Some existents are more specific than others, unlike the power of the Exalted Creator, for its suitability is one and does not differ, so its object must be one and does not differ, and that is existence. So if it is not permissible to attribute the most specific attributes to the Exalted Creator, because it leads to deficiency in suitability, likewise it is not permissible to attribute the most general attributes to the temporal power, because it leads to perfection in suitability. So that perfection is removed from divine power, and this perfection is not established for temporal power. So consider this, for in it is deliverance. So it is not permissible to attribute to the creator what is attributed to the acquirer, so that it is said: He is the writer, the speaker, the sitter, the stander; nor is it permissible to attribute to the acquirer what is attributed to the creator, so that it is said: He is the unifier, the innovator, the creator, the provider.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument about the proper attribution of attributes to God and to human beings. It begins by distinguishing created powers, which differ in their suitability for specific objects, from divine power, which is uniform and has existence as its only proper object. It then argues that if most specific attributes (like writing, speaking, sitting) cannot be attributed to God because they imply deficiency (as they are limited to specific acts), then conversely, most general attributes (like being creator, innovator) cannot be attributed to human power because they imply perfection in suitability (humans cannot create ex nihilo). Therefore, one must keep the two categories distinct: divine attributes belong to God, and human actions are acquired. The conclusion prohibits saying of God that He writes, speaks, sits, or stands in a human sense, and prohibits saying of humans that they are creators, innovators, etc.

الموجد
المكتسب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since God is the ultimate cause, human actions can be called 'creating' in a derivative sense.
Correction: This passage guards against that by distinguishing the ranges of attributes: what is true of the Creator cannot be applied to the creature, and vice versa.

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible to attribute the most general attributes (like creator) to temporal power?
A) Because it would imply deficiency in divine power.
B) Because it would imply perfection in suitability for temporal power.
C) Because temporal power cannot produce existence.
D) Because temporal power is limited to specific objects.

Key Takeaway
Divine and human attributes belong to entirely separate categories; mixing them undermines divine transcendence or falsely elevates human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Which attribute of God do you find easiest to imagine in human terms? How would retraining that imagination change the way you relate to Him?
?
The passage ends by saying 'consider this, for in it is deliverance.' From what specific confusion or spiritual danger does this truth deliver you personally?
