Seeking Help Proves Human Dependence
The author argues that the command to seek Allah's help in acts shows the servant is not independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command to seek Allah's help demonstrates that human actions are dependent on Allah, refuting the idea of human independence.

Core Idea
The command to seek help proves we are not independent.

This passage uses two Quranic verses to argue that if humans were independent, they would not need to ask Allah for guidance. The command to seek help implies a need for divine assistance, and the concept of grace (manna) further shows that guidance is a favor from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses cited.
2
Identify the two Quranic proofs
Find the two verses: 'Guide us to the straight path' and 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate…'. Notice how they are used as evidence.
3
Understand the logical argument
The author states that if the servant were independent, he would not need to ask for help. The command to seek help shows dependence.
This is a basic principle: need for help implies lack of independence.
4
Consider the opponent's view and the response
The opponent (Mu'tazila) interprets grace as creation of neutral power, which would nullify the meaning of being gracious for guidance. The author cites a verse to refute this.
The verse 'But Allah bestows grace upon you that He guided you to faith' emphasizes that guidance itself is a favor.
5
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this argument establishes total dependence on Allah for actions, countering any claim of human independence.
Reflect on how this relates to the broader Islamic creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوضح الجواب غاية الإيضاح أن التكليف بافعل ولا تفعل ورد بالاستعانة بالله سبحانه وتعالى في نفس المكلف به كقوله تعالى: (اهْدِنَا الصِّرَاطَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ) [الفاتحة: 5]، وكقوله تعالى: (رَبَّنَا لَا تُزِغْ قُلُوبَنَا بَعْدَ إِذْ هَدَيْتَنَا) [آل عمران: 8]، وسواء كانت الهداية نفسها هي المسئولة بالدعاء أو الثبات عليها هو المسئول، ولا شك أن العبد لو كان مستقلا بإنشائها بقدرته مستبدا بالثبات عليها كان مستغنيا عن هذه الاستعانة، ثم الله سبحانه يمن على من يشاء من عباده بأن هداهم للإيمان، وعند الخصم هو محمول على خلق القدرة وهي صالحة للضدين جميعًا على السواء، وذلك يبطل قضية الامتنان بالهداية، قال الله سبحانه وتعالى: (بَلِ اللَّهُ يَمُنُّ عَلَيْكُمْ أَنْ هَدَنَكُمْ لِلإِيمَنِ) [الحجرات: 17].
And among what clarifies the answer with utmost clarity is that the obligation with 'do' and 'do not do' came with seeking help from Allah the Most High in the very act of obligation, as His saying: 'Guide us to the straight path' [al-Fatiha: 5], and as His saying: 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us' [Al 'Imran: 8], whether the guidance itself is what is asked for in supplication or the steadfastness upon it is what is asked for. There is no doubt that if the servant were independent in creating it by his power and self-sufficient in steadfastness upon it, he would be needless of this seeking help. Then Allah the Most High bestows grace upon whom He wills of His servants by guiding them to faith, while according to the opponent it is interpreted as the creation of power, and it is equally suitable for both opposites, and that nullifies the principle of being gracious for guidance. Allah the Most High says: 'But Allah bestows grace upon you that He guided you to faith' [al-Hujurat: 17].
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Qur'anic command to seek help from Allah (istighātha) in performing the very acts that are commanded. The author argues that if the servant were independent (mustaqill) in creating his own acts and in persisting in them, he would not need to ask Allah for guidance. Yet Allah commands us to ask for guidance (hidāya), implying that He is the one who grants it. Moreover, Allah bestows grace (yumanu) upon whom He wills for guiding them to faith. According to the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila), this grace is understood as creating power that is neutral for both opposites, which would undermine the meaning of being gracious for guidance (imtinān bi-l-hidāya). The verse 'But Allah bestows grace upon you that He guided you to faith' is cited to emphasize that guidance is a favor from Allah, not from the servant's own power.

Key Terms

تكليف
هداية
منة
قدرة
استقلال
استبداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The command to ask for help implies that the servant has some independent ability.
Correction: Actually, it shows total dependence on Allah's grace; independence would make seeking help unnecessary.

Checkpoint
What does the command to seek guidance from Allah (e.g., in surat al-Fatiha) imply about human power?
A) That humans are independent
B) That humans are totally helpless
C) That humans are in need of Allah's help in their actions
D) That guidance is created by humans

Key Takeaway
The command to seek Allah's help in the Quran proves that human actions are entirely dependent on Allah's guidance and grace, not on independent power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'اهْدِنَا الصِّرَاطَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ' in every rak'a, do you truly feel that without Allah's guidance you would be utterly lost? How does that awareness shape your daily decisions, or have you become numb to its meaning?
?
The verse 'But Allah bestows grace upon you that He guided you to faith' (49:17) tells you that your very faith is a gift, not your achievement. If you fully believed that, would you still boast about your spiritual efforts or look down on others? What would change in your relationship with Allah and with people?
?
The author argues that if you were independent, you wouldn't need to ask Allah for help in your actions. Think of one area of your life where you still feel self-sufficient—where you rely on your own ability. Are you willing to turn to Allah in that area today, acknowledging that even that act depends on His grace? What specific 'do' or 'do not do' will you ask His help for right now?

The Argument from Absolute Independence (Istiġnā) for Divine Oneness
Shahrastānī expands the argument from independence to prove the impossibility of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of absolute independence (istiġnā muṭlaq) logically requires that there can be only one God.

Core Idea
Absolute independence means nothing is independent of you; two absolutes would cancel each other.

Shahrastānī deepens the argument for divine oneness by shifting from mutual hindrance in action to mutual hindrance in independence. This lesson unpacks the logical steps that show why two absolutely independent beings are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: tamānuʿ (تمانع), istiġnā bi-dhāt (استغناء بالذات), and istiġnā muṭlaq (استغناء مطلق).
The passage builds on the earlier tamānuʿ argument but says this one is more decisive.
2
Understand the shift from act to essence
The author claims that mutual hindrance in independence is more indicative than mutual hindrance in action. Reflect on why independence goes to the essence of divinity.
Independence by essence is a deeper attribute than action.
3
Follow the logical entailment
If two gods, each independent of the other, then each is the object of the other's independence. But absolute independence means nothing is independent of you. So each falls short, becoming needy.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
The verse 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (47:38) is cited as an allusion. Consider how it reinforces the concept that only Allah is absolutely independent.
This verse contrasts divine self-sufficiency with human neediness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنزد هذا بيانا وشرحًا فإن التمانع في الفعل إن أوجب استحالة الإلهين، والتمانع في الاستغناء بالذات أدل على استحالة الإلهين. فنقول : لو قدرنا إلهين استغنى كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه من كل وجه خرج المستغني عنه عن كمال الاستغناء، فإن المستغنى على الإطلاق من يستغنى به ولا يستغنى عنه، فيكون كل واحد منهما مستغنى عنه، فيكون مفتقرا قاصرًا عن درجة الاستغناء المطلق، فلن يتصور إذا إلهان مستغنيان على الإطلاق ( الْغَنِيُّ وَأَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَآءُ ) إشارة إلى هذا المعنى.
And let us increase this in clarification and explanation. For mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) in act, if it necessitates the impossibility of two gods, then mutual hindrance in independence (istiġnā) by essence is more indicative of the impossibility of two gods. So we say: If we suppose two gods, each being independent of the other in every respect, then the one from whom independence is exercised would fall short of complete independence. For the absolutely independent is one through whom independence is achieved and from whom independence is not exercised. Thus each of them would be one from whom independence is exercised, and thus would be needy, falling short of the degree of absolute independence. Therefore it is impossible to conceive two gods absolutely independent. 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (Quran 47:38) points to this meaning.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and clarification of the argument from mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ). The author, Shahrastānī, states that while the argument from mutual hindrance in action (al-fiʿl) already proves the impossibility of two gods, the argument from mutual hindrance in independence (istiġnā) by essence is even more decisive. He then explains: if we suppose two gods, each being completely independent of the other, then each would be the object of the other's independence, meaning the other is independent from him. But true absolute independence means that one is the source of independence for all others and is not himself the object of anyone's independence. Therefore, each of the supposed gods would fall short of absolute independence because he is dependent on the other not to be needed. Hence both are needy, and absolute independence cannot apply to two. The verse from the Quran (47:38) is cited as an allusion to this meaning.

Key Terms
تمانع
استغناء بالذات
استغناء مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that independence simply means not needing others.
Correction: True absolute independence also implies that others cannot be independent of you. If two gods were independent of each other, each would be the object of the other's independence, thus falling short of absolute independence.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument from independence (istiġnā) prove that there cannot be two gods?
A) Because each would be independent of the other, which is impossible.
B) Because each would be dependent on the other for existence.
C) Because absolute independence requires that nothing is independent of you, so two absolutes are impossible.
D) Because the Quran says so.

Key Takeaway
Absolute independence (istiġnā muṭlaq) cannot apply to two beings because each would then be the object of the other's independence, thereby falling short of absoluteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is absolutely independent, what does that say about your constant worry over things that are not in your control? How does your anxiety reveal a hidden belief that something other than Allah can grant you independence?
?
The argument shows that any being you rely on besides Allah is actually needy itself. In your life, what person, possession, or achievement do you find yourself depending on for security? What would change if you truly saw them as needy and Allah alone as independent?
?
Absolute independence means not only that Allah does not need you, but also that you cannot be independent of Him. Are there areas of your life where you act as if you are independent of His will—where you make decisions without seeking His guidance or acknowledging His control?

The Argument from Similarity and Difference for Divine Oneness
Shahrastani argues that two gods would be either different or similar, both impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shahrastani's logical argument for God's oneness by considering the impossibility of two gods being either different or similar in essential attributes.

Core Idea
Two gods are impossible: difference excludes one, similarity requires contingency.

Shahrastani presents a crisp logical proof for tawḥīd (divine oneness). He asks us to suppose two gods and then examines the only two possibilities: they are either different or similar. Both lead to contradictions that safeguard the uniqueness of the Divine Essence. This passage shows how rational argumentation reinforces the Quranic declaration that 'there is nothing like unto Him.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a dichotomy.
The author uses a very concise style; the translation helps clarify the logical steps.
2
Understand the dichotomy
The argument begins with a supposition (two gods) and splits into two exclusive possibilities: difference or similarity. This is a classic kalam method.
Dichotomy is also called 'division'—it exhausts all possibilities.
3
Analyze the 'different' case
If they differ in an essential attribute, then only one has the attribute that defines divinity, so the other cannot be a god.
Essential attributes are those without which divinity is inconceivable.
4
Analyze the 'similar' case
If they are similar in all essential attributes, they would be indistinguishable in essence, so they would need external factors (time, place) to tell them apart. But such factors are contingent and cannot apply to God.
The examples of blackness and substance illustrate this principle.
5
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The author ends by quoting 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11), showing that the rational conclusion aligns with revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : لو قدرنا إلهين فإما أن يكونا مختلفين في الصفات الذاتية أو متماثلين، والمختلفان يستحيل أن يكونا إلهين؛ لأن الصفة الذاتية التي بها تقدس الإله عن غيره إذا كانا مختلفين فيها كان الذي اتصف بها هو الإله والمخالف ليس بإله، وإما أن يكونا متماثلين في الصفات الذاتية من كل وجه فالمتماثلان ليس يتميز أحدهما عن الثاني بالحقيقة والخاصية، فإن حقيقتهما واحدة بل بلوازم زائدة على الحقيقة مثل المحل والمكان والزمان وكل ذلك ينافي الإلهية، أليس لما كان السوادان متماثلين لم يتميز أحدهما بحقيقته السوادية، بل باختلاف المحل أو باختلاف الزمان وكذلك الجوهران فدل ذلك على أن التماثل في الإلهية لن يتصور بوجه ( لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ) إشارة إلى هذا المعنى.
And we say: If we suppose two gods, then either they are different in essential attributes or similar. If they are different, it is impossible for them to be gods; because the essential attribute by which the god is sanctified from others—if they differ in it, then the one who possesses that attribute is the god, and the one differing is not a god. If they are similar in essential attributes in every respect, then the two similar things are not distinguished from each other by essence and specific property; for their essence is one, but rather by additional concomitants such as location, place, and time—all of which are incompatible with divinity. For just as two blacknesses, when similar, are not distinguished by their blackness-essence, but by difference of location or difference of time, and likewise two substances—this indicates that similarity in divinity is inconceivable in any way. 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11) points to this meaning.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's oneness by examining the logical possibilities for two gods. The author presents a dichotomy: if two gods exist, they must be either different or similar in their essential attributes. If they are different in an essential attribute, then only the one possessing that attribute is truly divine, while the other lacks it and thus is not God. If they are similar in all essential attributes, then they would be indistinguishable in essence, requiring something external like location or time to tell them apart—but such external distinctions are attributes of contingent beings and contradict the nature of divinity. The example of two black objects illustrates that similarity alone does not individuate; they are distinguished by location or time. Hence similarity in divinity is inconceivable. The Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) is cited as an allusion.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية
تماثل
اختلاف
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could be distinguished by their attributes or by external factors like time and place without compromising divinity.
Correction: Shahrastani shows that any distinction in essential attributes means one lacks the attribute of divinity, and any external distinction introduces contingency, which is incompatible with the necessary nature of God.

Checkpoint
According to Shahrastani, why is it impossible for two gods to be similar in all essential attributes?
A) Because similarity implies they are the same being.
B) Because they would require external distinctions like time or place, which contradict divinity.
C) Because similarity means they cannot be separate.
D) Because the Quran says there is nothing like God.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of God is rationally necessary because any plurality leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's oneness is rationally necessary, why do you still find yourself acting as if you can compartmentalize your life into sacred and secular, as if there were multiple sources of meaning? What would change if you truly internalized that nothing is like Him?
?
The argument shows that any distinction between two gods would either deny one's divinity or introduce contingency. How does this challenge your own attempts to define God according to your preferences or cultural assumptions? Are you willing to submit to the God who is beyond all comparison?

Objection and Answer: Will, Knowledge, and Divine Attributes
Shahrastānī presents an objection to the tamānuʿ argument and gives his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the tamānuʿ argument regarding different actions and how the answer clarifies the nature of divine will as an eternal attribute independent of knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine will does not follow knowledge; it is an eternal attribute of God.

Shahrastānī now addresses a subtle objection: if two gods have different wills for the same object, would that be impossible because will follows knowledge? The objector argues that for a single locus, knowledge is one, so wills cannot differ. Shahrastānī's answer reveals a deeper point about divine attributes: will is not necessitated by knowledge but freely relates to possibilities. Let's examine this exchange carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objector's argument: they claim that will follows knowledge, and knowledge follows the known object. Since the locus is one, knowledge is one, so two different wills cannot attach to it. This would undermine the tamānuʿ argument if it relies on different actions.
Notice the logical chain: unity of locus → unity of knowledge → impossibility of differing wills.
2
Analyze the answer
Now read Shahrastānī's response: will does not follow knowledge; rather, it is an eternal attribute that relates to all possible things in accordance with knowledge. It can relate to opposites if the causes differ, though the objection here concerns a single locus.
The key move is denying that will is determined by knowledge.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this defense preserves the tamānuʿ argument: by establishing that divine will is not constrained by knowledge in a way that would force unity of will for a single object, the possibility of multiple gods with conflicting wills remains open.
This reveals an important aspect of Ashʿarī theology: attributes like will are independent and eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال على دليل التمانع في فعلين مختلفين : فإن قيل : الاختلاف الذي قدرتموه في إرادة التحريك من أحدهما وإرادة التسكين من الآخر غير متصور فإن الإرادة تتبع العلم والعلم يتبع المعلوم، فإذا كان المحل واحدا فالعلم واحد فلا يصح تعلق إرادتين مختلفتين بمحل واحد، فكيف يتصور اختلاف الإرادة حتى يكون أحدهما مريدا للتحريك والآخر مريدا للتسكين؟ والجواب : الإرادة لا تتبع العلم، بل هي صفة قديمة متعلقة بكل الممكنات على وفق العلم بها، وإذا كانت الإرادة متعلقة بالممكنات على وفق العلم صح تعلقها بالضدين عند السببين المختلفين، وهاهنا لم يسألوا عن هذا بل عن محال واحد.
Objection on the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) in two different actions: For it might be said: The difference which you supposed in the will of one to move and the will of the other to still is inconceivable, because will follows knowledge, and knowledge follows the known object. So if the locus is one, knowledge is one, so it is not valid for two different wills to attach to a single locus. How then can difference in will be conceived such that one intends to move and the other intends to still? The answer: Will does not follow knowledge; rather it is an eternal attribute related to all possibilities in accordance with knowledge of them. And if will is related to possibilities in accordance with knowledge, it is valid for it to relate to two opposites in the case of two different causes. But here they are not asking about this; rather about a single locus.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair concerning the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) when the two actions are different (e.g., one moves, the other stills). The objector argues that such difference in will is inconceivable because will follows knowledge, and knowledge follows the known object. If the locus (the object in question) is one, then the knowledge of that object is one, and thus there cannot be two different wills attached to it—so the assumption of two conflicting wills is impossible. Therefore, the argument of mutual hindrance cannot be based on different actions. The answer is that will does not follow knowledge in the sense of being determined by it; rather, will is an eternal attribute that relates to all possible things in accordance with knowledge. Since will relates to possibilities as known, it is possible for it to relate to two opposites if the causes are different. However, the objector's point is about a single locus, which is a different matter. Thus, the objection is deflected by clarifying the nature of divine will: it is not necessitated by knowledge, and it can encompass opposites under different conditions, but the scenario of a single locus is not the one relevant to the argument.

Key Terms
تمانع
إرادة تتبع العلم
صفة قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will is determined by knowledge, so that if the object of knowledge is one, the will must be one.
Correction: The answer clarifies that divine will is an eternal attribute that does not follow knowledge; it can relate to opposites under different causes, preserving the possibility of conflicting divine wills.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main point against the tamānuʿ argument for two distinct actions?
A) That two gods cannot have different wills because will follows knowledge, which is one for a single object.
B) That two gods would always agree.
C) That the actions are not different.
D) That the argument only works for identical actions.

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine wills cannot differ for a single object fails because divine will is not constrained by knowledge; it is an eternal attribute that freely relates to possibilities.

This lesson shows that divine will is not constrained even by God's own knowledge. What would change in your life if you truly believed that God's will is not limited by what you think is possible or reasonable?

The Objection: Are Different Wills Inconceivable?
An objection against the supposition of two different wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objection raised against the proof of mutual opposition (tamānu'), namely that different wills are inconceivable because will follows knowledge.

Core Idea
The objector argues that will follows knowledge, so different wills are impossible.

The proof of mutual opposition relies on the possibility of two gods willing opposite acts. But what if such a difference in will is itself inconceivable? This objection challenges the very foundation of the proof. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own reasoning come through.
Notice the logical chain: will follows knowledge, knowledge follows known.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the steps: known → knowledge → will. The objector argues that same known implies same knowledge, hence same will. So difference is inconceivable.
3
Understand the impact on the proof
The objector concludes that since the proof requires difference (actual or supposed) and difference is impossible, the proof is invalid.
This is a direct challenge to the proof's foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال على دليل التمانع في فعلين مختلفين : فإن قيل : الاختلاف الذي قدرتموه في إرادة التحريك من أحدهما وإرادة التسكين من الآخر غير متصور فإن الإرادة تتبع العلم والعلم يتبع المعلوم، فإذا كان المعلوم هو الحركة، فمن ضرورته أن يكون المراد هو الحركة، وتقدير الاختلاف في العلم غير متصور، فتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة أيضا غير متصور، ومبنى دلالة التمانع على تحقيق الاختلاف أو تقديره وذلك غير جائز ; فبطل التمسك بها. قال الأصحاب الحركة والسكون من جملة الجايزات جبلة ; إذ ليس في وجود كل واحد منهما استحالة وإذا كانت القدرة صالحة وتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة متصور عقلا، فنحن نجعل المقدر كالمحقق وإن ما يلزم من التحقيق يلزم من التقدير ; كتقدير قيام لون أو عرض آخر بذاته تعالى نازل منزلة التحقيق في الاستحالة، والعلم ليس يخرج الجايز عن قضية الجواز، فإن خلاف المعلوم جايز الوجود جنساً، وأقول : إذا علم أحدهما تحريكه بإرادته وقدرته أفيعلم الثاني تحريكه بإرادة نفسه وقدرته أم يعلم تحريكه بإرادة غيره وقدرته، فإن علم ذلك موجودًا بإرادته وقدرته ، فيكون العلم الثاني جهلاً لا علماً ، وإن علمه موجودًا بإرادة غيره وقدرته فيكون الغير إلها عالما مريدا قادرًا، ويكون الثاني متعطلاً ولم يوجد له إلا علم متعلق بفعل غيره فقط، وأما إرادته لفعل غيره وقدرته على فعل غيره فتقديرهما مستحيل الوجود أو ناقص الوجود، فإن أخص وصف الإرادة ما يتأتى به التخصيص، وأخص وصف القدرة ما يتأتى بها الإيجاد والإبداع، وهما إذا تعلقا بفعل الغير خرجا عن أخص حقيقتهما، ففي تعطيلهما عن الفعل بعد جواز الفعل إبطال حقيقتهما.
An objection on the proof of mutual opposition concerning two different actions: If it is said: The difference that you supposed in the will of one to cause motion and the will of the other to cause stillness is inconceivable, because will follows knowledge and knowledge follows the known. So when the known is motion, it is necessary that the willed is motion, and the supposition of difference in knowledge is inconceivable, so the supposition of difference in will is also inconceivable. And the basis of the proof of mutual opposition is on actualizing difference or supposing it, and that is not permissible, so recourse to it is invalid. The Companions said: Motion and stillness are among contingent things by nature, for there is no impossibility in the existence of each of them. And if power is sound and the supposition of difference in will is rationally conceivable, then we treat the supposed as actual, and whatever follows from actuality follows from supposition. Like the supposition of a color or another accident subsisting in His essence, which is treated as actual in impossibility. And knowledge does not remove the contingent from the judgment of contingency, for the contrary of the known is possible in genus. And I say: If one of them knows that he causes motion by his will and power, does the second know that he causes motion by his own will and power, or does he know that it is caused by the will and power of another? If he knows it as existing by his own will and power, then the second knowledge is ignorance, not knowledge. And if he knows it as existing by the will and power of another, then the other is a god, knowing, willing, powerful, and the second is inactive, having only knowledge related to the act of another. As for his will for another's act and his power over another's act, their supposition is impossible or deficient in existence. For the most specific description of will is that by which specification is attained, and the most specific description of power is that by which origination and creation are attained. If they are attached to another's act, they depart from their most specific reality. So their being rendered inactive from act after the possibility of the act is a nullification of their reality.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof from mutual opposition (tamānu') in the case of two different actions (e.g., one wills motion, the other stillness). The objector argues that will follows knowledge, and knowledge follows the known; thus if the known is motion, the willed must be motion, and difference in knowledge is inconceivable, so difference in will is also inconceivable. Therefore, the proof's basis on establishing or supposing difference is invalid. The response (by 'the Companions'=asḥāb) counters: motion and stillness are contingent by nature, and the supposition of difference in will is rationally conceivable. They treat the supposed as equivalent to the actual in terms of implications. Knowledge does not remove contingency; the contrary of the known is possible in genus. Then a further argument: if one self knows his own act, the second self's knowledge must either be of his own act (contradiction) or of the other's act (making the other a god, rendering the second inactive). Thus, the will and power of the second become attached to another's act, which contradicts their essential definitions (specification and origination). Hence, the assumption of two gods leads to absurdity, preserving the proof.

Key Terms
التمانع
الإرادة
العلم
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual opposition only works if two gods actually exist and actually will opposite acts.
Correction: The objector argues that even the supposition of different wills is inconceivable, which would invalidate the proof even if the gods were hypothetical.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is the supposition of two different wills inconceivable?
A) Because power is limited to one act
B) Because will follows knowledge and knowledge follows the known, so same known implies same will
C) Because motion and stillness are the same
D) Because the proof does not require difference

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that the proof of mutual opposition fails because the required difference in wills is inconceivable due to the dependence of will on knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector claims that will must follow knowledge. In your own life, is your will consistently aligned with what you know to be true? Where do you find a disconnect, and what does that reveal about your sincerity?
?
If you believe in the proof of mutual opposition, how do you respond to this objection without dismissing it too quickly? What does your response say about your commitment to seeking truth even when it challenges your assumptions?
?
The objector's argument forces us to think deeply about the relationship between God's attributes. How does reflecting on this relationship change the way you understand God's unity? Will you take time today to study the attributes of Allah more carefully, not just accept them superficially?

The Scholarly Response: Why Supposition Suffices
The scholars' response defending the proof of mutual opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scholars (asḥāb) refute the objection by asserting the rational conceivability of different wills and treating supposition as equivalent to actuality.

Core Idea
The scholars treat rational supposition of difference as sufficient for the proof, and further argue that assuming two gods leads to absurdity.

The objection claimed that different wills are inconceivable. The scholars respond by affirming that motion and stillness are contingent, and the supposition of different wills is rationally conceivable. Moreover, they show that assuming two gods each willing the same act leads to contradiction, and if one wills another's act, that negates their attributes. Let's follow their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the scholars' own reasoning come through.
Notice the multiple arguments: contingency, supposition as actual, knowledge, dilemma.
2
Understand the first part: contingency and supposition
The scholars assert that motion and stillness are contingent, so different wills are rationally conceivable. They treat the supposition as equivalent to actuality for implications.
Analogous to supposing a color in God, which is treated as impossible.
3
Follow the second argument: the dilemma
If one god knows his own act, then the second god's knowledge is either of his own act (false) or of the first god's act (making the first god the true deity).
This leads to either ignorance or acknowledgment of the other's divinity.
4
Grasp the conclusion about attributes
Will and power attached to another's act contradict their essential definitions (specification and origination), so the second god's attributes would be nullified.
Thus the assumption of two gods is absurd, preserving the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال على دليل التمانع في فعلين مختلفين : فإن قيل : الاختلاف الذي قدرتموه في إرادة التحريك من أحدهما وإرادة التسكين من الآخر غير متصور فإن الإرادة تتبع العلم والعلم يتبع المعلوم، فإذا كان المعلوم هو الحركة، فمن ضرورته أن يكون المراد هو الحركة، وتقدير الاختلاف في العلم غير متصور، فتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة أيضا غير متصور، ومبنى دلالة التمانع على تحقيق الاختلاف أو تقديره وذلك غير جائز ; فبطل التمسك بها. قال الأصحاب الحركة والسكون من جملة الجايزات جبلة ; إذ ليس في وجود كل واحد منهما استحالة وإذا كانت القدرة صالحة وتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة متصور عقلا، فنحن نجعل المقدر كالمحقق وإن ما يلزم من التحقيق يلزم من التقدير ; كتقدير قيام لون أو عرض آخر بذاته تعالى نازل منزلة التحقيق في الاستحالة، والعلم ليس يخرج الجايز عن قضية الجواز، فإن خلاف المعلوم جايز الوجود جنساً، وأقول : إذا علم أحدهما تحريكه بإرادته وقدرته أفيعلم الثاني تحريكه بإرادة نفسه وقدرته أم يعلم تحريكه بإرادة غيره وقدرته، فإن علم ذلك موجودًا بإرادته وقدرته ، فيكون العلم الثاني جهلاً لا علماً ، وإن علمه موجودًا بإرادة غيره وقدرته فيكون الغير إلها عالما مريدا قادرًا، ويكون الثاني متعطلاً ولم يوجد له إلا علم متعلق بفعل غيره فقط، وأما إرادته لفعل غيره وقدرته على فعل غيره فتقديرهما مستحيل الوجود أو ناقص الوجود، فإن أخص وصف الإرادة ما يتأتى به التخصيص، وأخص وصف القدرة ما يتأتى بها الإيجاد والإبداع، وهما إذا تعلقا بفعل الغير خرجا عن أخص حقيقتهما، ففي تعطيلهما عن الفعل بعد جواز الفعل إبطال حقيقتهما.

D) The proof fails

Key Takeaway
The scholars refute the objection by affirming the rational conceivability of different wills and showing that assuming two gods leads to logical absurdity, thus preserving the proof of mutual opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a rational supposition of another god leads to absurdity. Have you ever entertained even a fleeting thought that something else—your own plans, a person, or a system—could have ultimate control over your life alongside Allah? How does this proof strip away that illusion? What would it take for you to uproot that thought completely today?
?
The scholars treat the supposed as actual. Consider a doubt that sometimes whispers in your heart: 'What if there is no divine plan, or what if God is not just?' That doubt is a supposition. According to this argument, its mere supposition already leads to logical and spiritual absurdity. Will you continue to entertain that supposition, or will you treat it as impossible and dismiss it? What concrete step can you take to replace that doubt with certainty?
?
The proof hinges on the specific nature of divine attributes: will is for specification, power for origination. When you attribute power or will to anything other than Allah—your own ability, a job, a relationship—you effectively attach divine attributes to a created being. This is shirk in its essence. In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to feel that your success or failure depends on something other than Allah alone? What would change if you truly believed that only Allah's will and power bring things into existence?

Defining Divine Agency: Knowledge, Will, and Power
Definition of divine agency using three attributes and a denial of change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine agency (fā'ilīya) in terms of God's eternal knowledge, will, and power, and why it excludes any change in Him.

Core Idea
God's act of creation involves no change in His essence or attributes.

In this passage, the author defines what it means for God to act. Rather than describing God's action as a temporal event, he roots it in His eternal attributes: knowledge, will, and power. The key is that God's creating something at a specific time does not imply that He Himself changes. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: the definition is built on a conditional (when He knew...), followed by willing and bringing into existence, with a crucial negative condition (without any change).
The text cuts off at the end; do not be distracted by the incomplete sentence.
2
Identify the three key elements
Find the three attributes mentioned: knowledge (al-ʿilm), will (al-irāda), and power (al-qudra). Note how they are linked: knowledge determines the time, will aligns with knowledge, and power executes.
These three are the basis of divine agency.
3
Understand the negative condition
The passage explicitly states that God's essence and attributes do not change. Ask yourself why this is important: it preserves God's transcendence and distinguishes Islamic theology from views that God changes or acquires new attributes.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The text ends with 'resembles' (yushbihu). Consider what the author might be comparing—perhaps God's action to a necessary emanation. Use the commentary to think about possible completions.
This incomplete phrase does not affect the core definition provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قلنا : إن معنى فاعلية الباري سبحانه وتعالى أنه لما علم وجود شيء في وقت مخصوص أو تقدير وقت أراد ذلك على مقتضى علمه، وأوجده بقدرته على مقتضى إرادته من غير أن تتغير ذاته أو صفة من صفاته وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته فيشبه
And because of this we said: The meaning of the agency of the Creator, exalted is He, is that when He knew the existence of something at a specific time or a determined time, He willed it according to His knowledge, and brought it into existence by His power according to His will, without His essence or any attribute of His attributes changing, and His knowledge, will, and power resemble...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of divine agency (fā'ilīya). The author states that God's act of creation involves: His eternal knowledge of the thing at a specific time, His will that accords with that knowledge, and His power that brings it into existence. Critically, this occurs without any change in His essence or attributes. The text ends abruptly with 'resemble' (layshabihu), likely leading to a comparison with something else (perhaps the sun's light or a necessary emanation). The definition emphasizes that God's attributes (knowledge, will, power) are eternal and unchanging, and His action does not imply temporal change in Him.

The Impossibility of Two Deities with Differing Wills
Analyzes logical consequences of two deities having differing wills and powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the coexistence of two deities with independent wills and powers leads to logical contradiction or nullification of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Two independent wills cannot both govern one action.

This passage tackles a classic objection against tawḥīd: what if there were two gods? The author shows that the very definitions of will and power—specification and creation—make it impossible for two deities to both exercise their attributes on the same object without contradiction or deficiency. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Note that motion and rest are possible; there is no intrinsic impossibility. This sets the stage: the object of action is contingent.
The author uses this to show that the problem is not about the object but about the agents.
2
Follow the hypothetical
Imagine two deities with different wills. The author treats the imagined scenario as realized for argument's sake. Then consider what the second deity knows about an action performed by the first.
This is a rational exercise to expose contradictions.
3
Grasp the conclusion
If the second deity's will and power are directed to another's act, they lose their defining functions (specification and creation). This renders them inactive or deficient, nullifying divinity.
Thus, two fully independent deities cannot coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الأصحاب الحركة والسكون من جملة الجايزات جبلة؛ إذ ليس في وجود كل واحد منهما استحالة وإذا كانت القدرة صالحة وتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة متصور عقلا، فنحن نجعل المقدر كالمحقق وإن ما يلزم من التحقيق يلزم من التقدير؛ كتقدير قيام لون أو عرض آخر بذاته تعالى نازل منزلة التحقيق في الاستحالة، والعلم ليس يخرج الجايز عن قضية الجواز، فإن خلاف المعلوم جايز الوجود جنساً، وأقول: إذا علم أحدهما تحريكه بإرادته وقدرته أفيعلم الثاني تحريكه بإرادة نفسه وقدرته أم يعلم تحريكه بإرادة غيره وقدرته، فإن علم ذلك موجودًا بإرادته وقدرته، فيكون العلم الثاني جهلاً لا علماً، وإن علمه موجودًا بإرادة غيره وقدرته فيكون الغير إلها عالما مريدا قادرًا، ويكون الثاني متعطلاً ولم يوجد له إلا علم متعلق بفعل غيره فقط، وأما إرادته لفعل غيره وقدرته على فعل غيره فتقديرهما مستحيل الوجود أو ناقص الوجود، فإن أخص وصف الإرادة ما يتأتى به التخصيص، وأخص وصف القدرة ما يتأتى بها الإيجاد والإبداع، وهما إذا تعلقا بفعل الغير خرجا عن أخص حقيقتهما، ففي تعطيلهما عن الفعل بعد جواز الفعل إبطال حقيقتهما.
The scholars said: Motion and rest are among the possible things by nature, for there is no impossibility in the existence of either. And if power is sound and imagining difference in will is rationally conceivable, then we treat the imagined as realized, and whatever follows from realization follows from imagination; like imagining the subsistence of a color or another accident in His essence, exalted, which takes the place of realization in impossibility. Knowledge does not remove a possible thing from its status as possible, for what is contrary to the known is possible in existence as a genus. And I say: If one knows that he moves something by his will and power, does the second know that he moves it by his own will and power, or does he know that it is moved by the will and power of another? If he knows it to exist by his own will and power, then the second knowledge is ignorance, not knowledge. If he knows it to exist by the will and power of another, then the other is a god, knowing, willing, powerful, and the second is inactive, having only knowledge related to another's act. As for his will for another's act and his power over another's act, imagining them is impossible in existence or deficient in existence. For the most specific description of will is that by which specification (takhṣīṣ) is achieved, and the most specific description of power is that by which bringing into existence (ījād) and creation (ibdāʿ) are achieved. If they are related to another's act, they depart from their most specific reality. Thus, to render them inactive from action after the possibility of action is to nullify their reality.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the logical consequences of assuming two deities with differing wills and powers. The author first notes that motion and rest are possible; there is no intrinsic impossibility. If power is sound, then it is rationally conceivable that wills differ. The author then posits a scenario: if one god knows he moves an object by his will and power, what does the second god know? If he knows the movement as coming from himself, that would be false (ignorance). If he knows it as from the other, then he is dependent and not truly independent. The author then argues that will and power are specifically defined: will specifies, power creates. If they are applied to another's act, they lose their proper functions. Thus, having a god whose will and power are directed to another's act is either impossible or deficient. Therefore, the proper attributes of divinity would be nullified.

Key Terms
الجايزات
القدرة
الإرادة
التخصيص
الإيجاد
الإبداع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a deity can will another's act without deficiency.
Correction: The author shows that will's essence is specification; if it is for another's act, it loses its defining function, making the deity inactive or deficient.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the most specific description of power?
A) Ability to move.
B) Bringing into existence and creation.
C) Specification.
D) Knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The specific functions of will (specification) and power (creation) cannot be directed to another's act without negating their essence, proving that two fully independent deities cannot coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says knowledge does not remove a possible thing from its possibility. Does your belief in God's foreknowledge make you complacent about sin, as if it were inevitable? How does this passage call you to take responsibility?
?
Will is defined by specification—choosing one thing over another. When you will something that contradicts God's will, are you not setting up your own will as an independent authority? What would it take for you to submit your will fully to His specification?

The Ash'ari Answer: Implication of Action
The mutakallimun answer that the act's indication is its existence, not its being good or evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that actions indicate God's existence but not His moral qualities, and how good and evil are explained as relative or legal categories.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not inherent in existence; God wills existence itself.

This passage presents the Ash'ari response to a key Mu'tazili objection: if God wills evil, He would be evil. The answer clarifies that the implication of an act is based on its existence, not its moral quality, and that good and evil are either relative or determined by revelation. We'll unpack this sophisticated argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the general principle to the specific refutation.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence stating that the act's implication is based on possibility (jawaz) and contingency (imkan), and that this does not change whether the act is good or evil.
This is the foundation of the Ash'ari position.
3
Understand the two explanations of good and evil
The passage gives two possibilities: good and evil are either relative (iḍafi) or purely legal (shar'i). Notice how each removes inherent moral qualities from actions.
This counters the Mu'tazili assumption of objective good/evil.
4
See how this refutes the Mu'tazili objection
The Mu'tazila claim that willing evil makes one evil. The Ash'ari answer explains that evil is only attributed to the act from the perspective of its existence, not that God wills evil as such. God wills existence itself.
Thus, God is not a desirer of evil in reality.
5
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on how this protects God's perfection: His will is not tainted by moral categories that are not intrinsic to existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب على قاعدة المتكلمين : قال المحققون منهم : وجه دلالة الفعل على الفاعل هو الجواز والإمكان وترجح جانب الوجود على العدم، وذلك لم يختلف خيرا كان أو شرا، فالوجود من حيث هو وجود خير كله أو يقال : لا خير فيه ولا شر، والفعل من حيث وجوده ينسب إلى الفاعل لا من حيث هو خير أو شر والفاعل يريد الوجود من حيث هو وجود لا من حيث هو خير أو شر بل الخير والشر إما أمران إضافيان بأن يكون شيء خيرًا بالإضافة إلى شيء شراً بالإضافة إلى شيء، وإما أمران شرعيان فيرجع الحسن و القبح والخير والشر فيه إلى قول الشارع افعل لا تفعل. وهذا هو جوابنا عن قول المعتزلة حيث قالوا في إرادة الكاينات : إنه لو كان مريداً للشر لكان شراً، فإن الشر لم ينسب إليه إلا من جهة وجوده، والوجود من حيث هو وجود لا شر فيه، وهو مريد الموجود بمعنى أنه على صفة يتأتى منه التخصيص بالوجود دون العدم، وببعض الجايزات دون البعض، فلم يكن مريدا للشر في الحقيقة.
And the answer according to the method of the mutakallimun: The investigators among them said: The aspect of implication of the act upon the agent is possibility (jawaz) and contingency (imkan) and the preponderance of the side of existence over non-existence. And that does not differ whether good or evil. So existence inasmuch as it is existence is all good, or it is said: there is no good or evil in it. And the act inasmuch as its existence is attributed to the agent, not inasmuch as it is good or evil. And the agent wills existence inasmuch as it is existence, not inasmuch as it is good or evil. Rather, good and evil are either relative matters, such that a thing is good relative to something and evil relative to something, or they are legal matters (shar'iyan), so that beauty (husn) and ugliness (qubh), good and evil therein revert to the statement of the Lawgiver: 'Do' or 'Do not'. And this is our answer to the statement of the Mu'tazila when they said concerning the will of existents: If He were a desirer of evil, He would be evil. For evil is attributed to Him only from the aspect of its existence, and existence inasmuch as it is existence has no evil in it. And He is the desirer of the existent in the sense that He is in a state that enables the specification to existence rather than non-existence, and to some possibles rather than others. So He is not a desirer of evil in reality.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is a detailed answer to the objection. The mutakallimun explain that the act indicates the agent through its possibility and preponderance, not through its moral quality. Existence as such is either entirely good or devoid of moral categories. Good and evil are either relative (depending on perspective) or purely legal (determined by revelation). Thus, God wills existence as such, not specific moral qualities. The Mu'tazila's claim that willing evil makes one evil is refuted because evil is only in the object's existence from a certain perspective, but existence itself is not evil. God's will is the specification of existence, not of moral evil.

Key Terms
جواز (possibility)
إمكان (contingency)
ترجيح (preponderance)
إضافي (relative)
شرعي (legal)
كائنات (existents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's willing of an evil act makes Him morally evil.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer shows that evil is not an inherent quality of the act but either relative or legal, and God wills existence itself, not evil as a moral category.

Checkpoint
According to this answer, what is the real object of God's will?
A) Good.
B) Evil.
C) Existence as such.
D) Both good and evil equally.

Key Takeaway
God's will concerns existence itself, not moral qualities; therefore, evil in creation does not imply evil in the Creator.

Good and Evil as Relative or Revealed
Author defines good and evil as relative or revealed, then responds to Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's definition of good and evil as either relative or revealed, and how this definition answers the Mu'tazila objection about God willing evil.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not intrinsic to existence; they are relative or revealed.

This lesson introduces a key distinction: good and evil are not inherent qualities of actions or things. Instead, they are either relative—dependent on circumstance—or revealed, defined by divine command. This distinction becomes crucial for responding to the Mu'tazila, who argued that if God willed evil, He would be evil. Let's see how the author clarifies God's will and preserves His perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of good and evil
Read the passage where the author states that good and evil are either relative or revealed. Notice the distinction.
2
Understand the two categories
Identify what 'relative' and 'revealed' mean. Relative means context-dependent; revealed means determined by divine command.
3
Apply to the Mu'tazila objection
See how the author uses this definition to argue that God wills existence, not evil, and thus the Mu'tazila criticism fails.
The key point: evil is attributed to God only from the aspect of existence, not as evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينسب إلى الفاعل لا من حيث هو خير أو شر والفاعل يريد الوجود من حيث هو وجود لا من حيث هو خير أو شر بل الخير والشر إما أمران إضافيان بأن يكون شيء خيرًا بالإضافة إلى شيء شراً بالإضافة إلى شيء، وإما أمران شرعيان فيرجع الحسن و القبح والخير والشر فيه إلى قول الشارع افعل لا تفعل. وهذا هو جوابنا عن قول المعتزلة حيث قالوا في إرادة الكاينات : إنه لو كان مريداً للشر لكان شراً، فإن الشر لم ينسب إليه إلا من جهة وجوده، والوجود من حيث هو وجود لا شر فيه، وهو مريد الموجود بمعنى أنه على صفة يتأتى منه التخصيص بالوجود دون العدم، وببعض الجايزات دون البعض ، فلم يكن مريدا للشر في الحقيقة. ونقول للتنويه : إنَّا كما صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرا متمايزين، فقد صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرًا مختلطين، فإن عالم الخير المحض هو عالم الملائكة، وعالم الشر المحض هو عالم الشياطين، وعالم الاختلاط والامتزاج هو عالم البشر، فهلا أثبتم ثالثًا ينسب إليه الامتزاج، فإن الممتزج من حيث هو ممتزج على طبيعة غير ما كان المنفرد عليها، فهلا كانت الطبيعة المذكورة دالة على ثالث، لكن قلتم : الخير والشر لم يختلفا بالامتزاج بل دلالتهما واحدة، كذلك نقول : لم يختلفا في الوجود فإن دلالة الوجود واحدة.
It is attributed to the agent not insofar as it is good or evil, and the agent wills existence insofar as it is existence, not insofar as it is good or evil. Rather, good and evil are either relative matters, in that a thing is good in relation to one thing and evil in relation to another, or they are revealed matters, so that good, evil, beauty, and ugliness therein refer to the statement of the Lawgiver: 'Do' and 'Do not do.' This is our answer to the statement of the Mu'tazila when they said concerning the willing of beings: if He were willing of evil, He would be evil. For evil is attributed to Him only from the aspect of its existence, and existence insofar as it is existence contains no evil. He is the willer of the existent, meaning that He is described with a quality from which proceeds specification for existence rather than non-existence, and for some possibilities rather than others. So He is not in reality a willer of evil. And we say to the dualists: just as we encounter in existents good and evil that are distinct, we also encounter in existents good and evil that are mixed. For the world of pure good is the world of angels, and the world of pure evil is the world of devils, and the world of mixture and blending is the world of humans. So why do you not affirm a third [principle] to which mixture is attributed? For the mixed, insofar as it is mixed, has a nature different from that which the isolated has. So why is that nature not indicative of a third [principle]? But you say: good and evil do not differ through mixture; rather their indication is one. Similarly, we say: they do not differ in existence, for the indication of existence is one.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument concerning the nature of good and evil and their attribution to God. The author begins by stating that good and evil are not intrinsic to the agent's will; the agent wills existence purely as existence, not as qualified by good or evil. Good and evil are either relative (i.e., a thing is good in one context and evil in another) or revealed (i.e., defined by divine command). This definition serves as the foundation for responding to the Mu'tazila, who argued that if God willed evil, He would be evil. The author replies that evil is only attributed to God from the perspective of existence, and existence qua existence has no evil. God's will is simply the quality that specifies some possibilities for existence. Thus, He is not truly willing evil. Next, the author addresses the dualists (likely Manichaeans), who posit two separate principles for good and evil. The author observes that in the world, we find both distinct good and evil (angels vs. devils) and mixed good and evil (humans). The dualists, who consider good and evil as entirely separate, should logically admit a third principle for the mixture, since the mixture has a different nature. But the dualists claim that good and evil do not differ through mixture, i.e., their indication remains the same. The author turns this argument back: similarly, in existence, good and evil do not differ, for existence as such is one. Thus, the unity of existence supports the unity of the Creator, contrary to dualism.

شر
حسن
قبح
إضافي
شرعي
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God wills evil directly and thus is evil.
Correction: God wills existence as such; evil is only attributed to Him from the aspect of existence, which is neutral. So God is not a willer of evil.

Checkpoint
According to the author, good and evil are:
A) Intrinsic properties of actions
B) Either relative or revealed
C) Purely subjective
D) Unrelated to divine will

Key Takeaway
Good and evil are not intrinsic to existence; they are context-dependent or divinely commanded, so God's willing existence does not make Him a willer of evil.

This lesson teaches that good and evil are not intrinsic but either relative or revealed. Will you submit that judgment to divine command today, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Refuting Dualism: The Argument from Mixed Good and Evil
Author argues against dualism by pointing to mixed good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument against dualism: the existence of mixed good and evil in our world points to a single source, not two.

Core Idea
Mixture of good and evil demands a single cause, not two.

Dualists, like the Manichaeans, claimed good and evil come from two separate principles. The author observes that in our world, good and evil often blend together—as in human nature. This mixing, he argues, cannot be explained by two separate sources; it requires a single source that produces both. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the address to dualists
Read the passage where the author responds to the dualists, noting the three realms he describes.
2
Identify the three realms
The author lists: pure good (angels), pure evil (devils), and mixed (humans). Notice the challenge: the mixed realm has a nature different from the pure ones.
3
Understand the logical implication
The author argues that if dualists claim good and evil are separate, they cannot account for mixture. Their view that mixture doesn't change their nature is turned around to show that existence is one, supporting monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينسب إلى الفاعل لا من حيث هو خير أو شر والفاعل يريد الوجود من حيث هو وجود لا من حيث هو خير أو شر بل الخير والشر إما أمران إضافيان بأن يكون شيء خيرًا بالإضافة إلى شيء شراً بالإضافة إلى شيء، وإما أمران شرعيان فيرجع الحسن و القبح والخير والشر فيه إلى قول الشارع افعل لا تفعل. وهذا هو جوابنا عن قول المعتزلة حيث قالوا في إرادة الكاينات : إنه لو كان مريداً للشر لكان شراً، فإن الشر لم ينسب إليه إلا من جهة وجوده، والوجود من حيث هو وجود لا شر فيه، وهو مريد الموجود بمعنى أنه على صفة يتأتى منه التخصيص بالوجود دون العدم، وببعض الجايزات دون البعض ، فلم يكن مريدا للشر في الحقيقة. ونقول للتنويه : إنَّا كما صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرا متمايزين، فقد صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرًا مختلطين، فإن عالم الخير المحض هو عالم الملائكة، وعالم الشر المحض هو عالم الشياطين، وعالم الاختلاط والامتزاج هو عالم البشر، فهلا أثبتم ثالثًا ينسب إليه الامتزاج، فإن الممتزج من حيث هو ممتزج على طبيعة غير ما كان المنفرد عليها، فهلا كانت الطبيعة المذكورة دالة على ثالث، لكن قلتم : الخير والشر لم يختلفا بالامتزاج بل دلالتهما واحدة، كذلك نقول : لم يختلفا في الوجود فإن دلالة الوجود واحدة.
It is attributed to the agent not insofar as it is good or evil, and the agent wills existence insofar as it is existence, not insofar as it is good or evil. Rather, good and evil are either relative matters, in that a thing is good in relation to one thing and evil in relation to another, or they are revealed matters, so that good, evil, beauty, and ugliness therein refer to the statement of the Lawgiver: 'Do' and 'Do not do.' This is our answer to the statement of the Mu'tazila when they said concerning the willing of beings: if He were willing of evil, He would be evil. For evil is attributed to Him only from the aspect of its existence, and existence insofar as it is existence contains no evil. He is the willer of the existent, meaning that He is described with a quality from which proceeds specification for existence rather than non-existence, and for some possibilities rather than others. So He is not in reality a willer of evil. And we say to the dualists: just as we encounter in existents good and evil that are distinct, we also encounter in existents good and evil that are mixed. For the world of pure good is the world of angels, and the world of pure evil is the world of devils, and the world of mixture and blending is the world of humans. So why do you not affirm a third [principle] to which mixture is attributed? For the mixed, insofar as it is mixed, has a nature different from that which the isolated has. So why is that nature not indicative of a third [principle]? But you say: good and evil do not differ through mixture; rather their indication is one. Similarly, we say: they do not differ in existence, for the indication of existence is one.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument concerning the nature of good and evil and their attribution to God. The author begins by stating that good and evil are not intrinsic to the agent's will; the agent wills existence purely as existence, not as qualified by good or evil. Good and evil are either relative (i.e., a thing is good in one context and evil in another) or revealed (i.e., defined by divine command). This definition serves as the foundation for responding to the Mu'tazila, who argued that if God willed evil, He would be evil. The author replies that evil is only attributed to God from the perspective of existence, and existence qua existence has no evil. God's will is simply the quality that specifies some possibilities for existence. Thus, He is not truly willing evil. Next, the author addresses the dualists (likely Manichaeans), who posit two separate principles for good and evil. The author observes that in the world, we find both distinct good and evil (angels vs. devils) and mixed good and evil (humans). The dualists, who consider good and evil as entirely separate, should logically admit a third principle for the mixture, since the mixture has a different nature. But the dualists claim that good and evil do not differ through mixture, i.e., their indication remains the same. The author turns this argument back: similarly, in existence, good and evil do not differ, for existence as such is one. Thus, the unity of existence supports the unity of the Creator, contrary to dualism.

Key Terms
خير
شر
تنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil come from two separate, independent sources.
Correction: The mixture of good and evil in our world (e.g., humans) shows they share a common source, not two.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to argue against dualism?
A) The existence of pure good and pure evil
B) The existence of mixed good and evil
C) The non-existence of evil
D) The absolute power of God

Key Takeaway
The mixture of good and evil in the world (e.g., humans) requires a single source, refuting dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own inner struggles between good and evil, do you catch yourself acting as if these come from two separate powers? How does the unity of the Creator change how you face that struggle?
?
If every mixture of good and evil in your life ultimately points to one Source, then what does it mean for you to trust Him completely—even when evil seems to dominate?
?
The dualist refused to see mixture as evidence of a single source. In what area of your life have you refused to see God's hand behind both the good and the difficult, demanding an explanation that divides rather than unifies?

The Muʿtazila's Denial of Attributes from the Unity of Necessity and Existence
The author explains the Muʿtazila's rejection of attributes from the unity of necessity and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muʿtazila's rationale for denying that the divine attributes are additional to the essence, based on the non-distinction between necessity, existence, and quiddity.

Core Idea
The Muʿtazila denied God's attributes as additional because they saw a unity among necessity, existence, and quiddity.

In this passage, the author introduces the Muʿtazila's position on the divine attributes, which they deny as distinct from God's essence. Their reasoning is based on a metaphysical principle: necessity, existence, and quiddity are not separate realities. This sets the stage for a detailed discussion later in the book. Let's examine their argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once through, then read the literal English translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the relationship between the concepts.
2
Identify the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'necessity' (wujūb), 'existence' (wujūd), 'quiddity' (māhiyya), and 'reality' (ḥaqīqa). Understand that the Muʿtazila argue these are not distinct from each other.
These terms are foundational to Islamic metaphysics.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Because these concepts are not distinct, the Muʿtazila concluded that the divine attributes cannot be additional to the essence. Grasp this logical link.
This is the core of their position on attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من غير أن يتمايز وجوب عن وجود، ووجود عن ماهية وحقيقة، وعن هذا نفوا صفات الباري تعالى (۳) زائدة على الذات، كما سيأتي تفصيل ذلك(4) في مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى.
Without necessity being distinguished from existence, and existence from quiddity and reality, and on this basis they denied the attributes of the Exalted God (3) as additional to the Essence, as will be detailed (4) in the chapter on attributes, if God wills.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement summarizing the Muʿtazila position on the divine attributes. It begins by asserting that, according to their view, necessity (wujūb) is not distinct from existence (wujūd), and existence is not distinct from quiddity (māhiyya) and reality (ḥaqīqa). Because of this essential unity, they denied that God's attributes are additional (zāʾida) to His essence. The author then notes that this will be further elaborated in the later discussion on attributes.

Key Terms
وجوب
وجود
ماهية
حقيقة
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Muʿtazila denied attributes arbitrarily or based on simple rationalism.
Correction: They denied them based on a specific metaphysical principle: the non-distinction between necessity, existence, and quiddity, which they saw as unified in God.

Checkpoint
On what basis did the Muʿtazila deny that the divine attributes are additional to the essence?
A) Because they held that God has no attributes at all.
B) Because they argued that necessity is not distinct from existence, and existence is not distinct from quiddity.
C) Because they followed the Ashʿarī school of thought.
D) Because they denied the existence of attributes entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Muʿtazila's denial of additional divine attributes stems from their view that necessity, existence, and quiddity are not distinct from each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing and All-Powerful,' do you unconsciously imagine two separate additions to His essence? How does the Muʿtazila's insistence on unity challenge your mental image of God? Will you strive to see His attributes as expressions of His single, simple reality?
?
If God's attributes are not additional, then every mention of His mercy, wrath, or justice is not a different part of Him but a different face of the same perfect reality. How does this affect the way you turn to Him in prayer? Are you ready to abandon fragmented conceptions and surrender to the whole?

Eternal Will and Temporal Creation: Karramiyya Distinctions
Details Karramiyya distinctions between eternal will and temporal creation acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Karramiyya distinguish between eternal and temporal aspects of divine will and creation.

Core Idea
Karramiyya affirm both eternal and temporal attributes in God.

The Karramiyya theological school attempted to reconcile God's eternity with His creative action by distinguishing between two kinds of will. This lesson unpacks their key distinctions between mashi'a (eternal will) and irada (temporal will), and between the act of creation and the created thing. Understanding these nuances is essential for grasping their unique position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about will and creation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the Karramiyya avoid terms like 'indwelling' while still affirming temporal attributes.
Pay attention to the pairs: mashi'a/irada, takwin/mukawwan, ihdath/muhdath, khalq/makhluq.
2
Identify the distinction in will
The passage explicitly states that mashi'a is eternal and irada is temporal. Think about why this distinction matters for divine action.
3
Explore the creation pairs
Notice that for each pair, the first term (e.g., takwin) is temporal in God's essence, while the second (e.g., mukawwan) is separate. This shows the Karramiyya's effort to maintain God's transcendence.
The phrase 'الخلق حادث في ذاته والمخلوق مباين' is key.
4
Reflect on the overall method
Consider why the Karramiyya make these distinctions. How does it differ from the Ash'ari or Mu'tazili views?
This connects to their rejection of hulul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محدث أحدثه وربما احترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ الحلول والمحل وإن أطلقوا بالاتصاف بالحادث، وفرقوا بين المشيئة والإرادة فقالوا: مشيئة قديمة وإرادة حادثة، ولذلك فرقوا بين التكوين والمكون، والأحداث والمحدث، والخلق والمخلوق، فالخلق حادث في ذاته والمخلوق مباين وكذلك التكوين عبارة عن قوله كن والقول قائم بذاته والمكون مباين. وكذلك كلامه تعالى صفات تحدث له وهي عبارات منتظمة من حروف وأصوات عند بعضهم، وعند بعضهم من حروف مجردة، فهو حادث ليس بقديم ولا محدث، وأحالوا فناء ما حدث من الصفات في ذاته، ولم يطلقوا لفظ التقدم والتأخر على الحروف والكلمات، واجتهد محمد بن الهيصم منهم في كل.
An event that He caused; and perhaps they avoided using the term 'indwelling' (al-hulul) and 'locus' (al-mahal), even though they [otherwise] affirm being described by the temporal. And they distinguished between al-mashi'a (will) and al-irada (will), saying: al-mashi'a is eternal and al-irada is temporal. Therefore they distinguished between al-takwin (bringing into existence) and al-mukawwan (the brought into existence), and between al-ihdath (causing to happen) and al-muhdath (the caused), and between al-khalq (creation) and al-makhluq (the created). So creation is temporal in His essence and the created is separate. Likewise, al-takwin is an expression of His saying 'Be' (kun), and the saying subsists in His essence, while the thing brought into existence is separate. And similarly, His speech, exalted, are attributes that occur for Him, and they are composed expressions of letters and sounds according to some of them, and according to others of mere letters. So it is temporal, not eternal nor was it brought into being by an agent. And they deemed impossible the annihilation of what occurred of the attributes in His essence, and they did not apply the terms 'precedence' and 'postponement' to the letters and words. And Muhammad ibn al-Haysam among them exerted effort in all of this.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage continues the exposition of Karramiyya doctrines. They avoid the term 'indwelling' (حلول) and 'locus' (محل) even though they ascribe temporality to God's attributes. They make a key distinction between two types of will: al-mashi'a (مشيئة) is eternal, while al-irada (إرادة) is temporal. Based on this, they similarly distinguish between the act of bringing into existence (تكوين) and the thing brought into existence (مكون); between causing an event (إحداث) and what is caused (محدث); and between creation (خلق) and the created (مخلوق). For them, creation (الخلق) is a temporal act occurring in God's essence, while the created thing (المخلوق) is separate from Him. Likewise, takwin (التكوين) is explained as God's command 'Be' (كن), and this speech (قول) subsists in His essence, while the thing formed (المكون) is separate. Regarding God's speech (كلامه تعالى), they say it consists of temporal attributes that occur in God, composed of letters and sounds (or just letters for some). This speech is temporal (حادث), not eternal (قديم), nor was it caused by an external agent (لا محدث). They reject the possibility that these temporal attributes could cease to exist (فناء) in God's essence. They also avoid applying terms like 'precedence' (تقدم) and 'postponement' (تأخر) to the letters and words of speech. The passage ends by mentioning that Muhammad ibn al-Haysam (ابن الهيصم) among them exerted great effort in articulating these views. This section shows the Karramiyya's complex attempt to reconcile God's eternity with temporal attributes while avoiding some anthropomorphic implications.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإرادة
التكوين
المكون
الخلق
المخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya reject any temporal attributes in God.
Correction: They affirm temporal attributes like irada and creation acts, but distinguish them from eternal attributes like mashi'a.

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, which of the following is true about divine will?
A) Both mashi'a and irada are eternal
B) Both mashi'a and irada are temporal
C) Mashi'a is eternal, irada is temporal
D) Mashi'a is temporal, irada is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya distinguish between eternal and temporal aspects of God's will and creative action, maintaining that temporal attributes occur in God's essence while their effects are separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya teach that God's eternal will (mashi'a) is unchanging, while His temporal will (irada) unfolds in time. When you face unexpected trials, do you trust that God's eternal will remains good and wise, or do you react as if His intentions have changed? What specific situation will you rehearse this truth in today?
?
This lesson shows that God's command 'Be' subsists in His essence while the created thing is separate. Do you live as if your existence is entirely distinct from God, or do you sometimes behave as if God is confined to your circumstances or desires? What shift in your daily mindset could help you honor His transcendence and immanence properly?
?
The Karramiyya avoid terms like 'locus' and 'indwelling' to protect God's transcendence. Is there any belief or action in your life that subtly reduces God to a 'place' for your emotions or thoughts? How can you deliberately affirm His absolute otherness this week?
