The Nature of Divine Speech According to the Karramiyya
Details Karramiyya views on divine speech as temporal attributes and related issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view that divine speech is composed of temporal attributes (letters and sounds) and their rejection of its annihilation.

Core Idea
For the Karramiyya, God's speech is a temporal attribute consisting of letters and sounds.

The Karramiyya held a unique position on the nature of divine speech, viewing it as a series of temporal attributes that occur in God's essence. This lesson explores their description of speech as composed of letters and sounds, and their rejection of the idea that these attributes could cease to exist. We also note their avoidance of temporal terms like 'precedence' for the letters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine speech
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the description of God's speech.
Notice the phrases 'صفات تحدث له' and 'عبارات منتظمة من حروف وأصوات'.
2
Analyze the composition of speech
There is a disagreement among Karramiyya: some say speech consists of letters and sounds, others say only letters. Identify both views.
The word 'عند بعضهم' indicates a difference of opinion.
3
Understand the temporal nature
The passage states that speech is 'حادث ليس بقديم ولا محدث'. It is temporal but not caused by an external agent. This is a nuanced position.
لا محدث means it wasn't brought into being by an agent—it occurs in God's essence.
4
Consider the rejection of annihilation
They deem impossible the annihilation (فناء) of these attributes in God's essence. Why would they insist on that?
This connects to their view that God's essence can be a locus for temporal events without change.
5
Reflect on the avoidance of temporal terms
They avoid applying 'precedence' and 'postponement' to the letters and words. Consider why this matters for preserving divine eternity.
Muhammad ibn al-Haysam is mentioned as a key proponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Objection: Is God's Size Necessary or Possible?
Objection that God's size is necessary by essence using an analogy with the eight attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that God's specific size must be necessary by essence, and the dilemma it creates between the number of attributes and divine limits.

Core Idea
If God's size is necessary, it needs no cause; but all limits seem contingent.

The objector presents a challenge: if God's attributes are fixed at eight, is that number necessary or possible? If necessary, then God's size could also be necessary. If possible, then the size needs a cause. This dilemma questions the consistency of the author's position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the objection carefully, noting the logical steps the objector takes.
Pay attention to the analogy with the eight attributes.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objector presents a dilemma: either the number of attributes is necessary or possible. Observe how this leads to a conclusion about God's size.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Think about why this objection is difficult for the author's position. Why would it be problematic if God's size were necessary?
This will help you appreciate the author's answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم واجب له لذاته، فلا يحتاج إلى مخصص، والمقادير التي هي في الخلق إنما احتاجت إلى مقدر؛ لأنها جايزة، وذلك لأن الجواز في الجايزات إنما يعرف بتقدير القدرة، فلما كانت المقادير الخلقية مقدورة عرف جوازها واحتاج الجواز إلى مرجح، فإذا لم يكن فوق الباري سبحانه قادر يقدر عليه لم تكن إضافة الجواز وإثبات الاحتياج له، ألسنا اتفقنا على أن الصفات ثمان أفهي واجبة له على هذا العدد أم جايز أن توجد صفة أخرى؟ فإن قلتم: يجب الانحصار في هذا العدد كذلك نقول: الاختصاص بالحد المذكور واجب له؛ إذ لا فرق بين مقدار في الصفات عددًا وبين مقدار في الذات حدا. فإن قلتم: جايز أن توجد صفة أخرى، فما الموجب للانحصار في هذا الحد والعدد فيحتاج إلى مخصص حاصر. والجواب: قلنا: المقادير من حيث إنها مقادير طولا وعرضا وعمقاً لا تختلف شاهدا وغايبًا في تطرق الجواز العقلي إليها واستدعاء المخصص، فإنا لو قدرنا مثل ذلك المقدار بعينه في الشاهد تطرق الجواز العقلي إليه واختصاصه به دون مقدار آخر يستدعي المخصص، وتطرق الجواز إلى الجايزات لا يتوقف على تقدير القدرة عليها، بل معرفة ذلك بينة للعقل ضرورية حتى صار كثير من العقلاء إلى أن
For if it is said: Why is it not necessary for Him by His essence, so He does not need a particularizer? And the quantities that are in creation only needed a determiner because they are possible, and that is because possibility in contingent things is only known by the estimation of power. Since created quantities are objects of power, their possibility is known, and possibility needs a preponderator. If there is no power above the Creator that determines it, then ascription of possibility and proof of need is not established. Have we not agreed that the attributes are eight? Are they necessary for Him in this number or is it possible that another attribute exist? If you say: They must be confined to this number, likewise we say: The particularization to the mentioned limit is necessary for Him; for there is no difference between a quantity in the attributes by number and a quantity in the essence by limit. If you say: It is possible that another attribute exist, then what necessitates the confinement to this limit and number? So it needs a confining particularizer. The answer: We say: Quantities, in that they are quantities of length, width, and depth, do not differ in the seen and the unseen in the entry of rational possibility to them and their requiring a particularizer. For if we assume the very same quantity in the seen world, rational possibility enters into it, and its being particularized by it rather than another quantity requires a particularizer. And the entry of possibility into contingent things does not depend on the estimation of power upon them; rather, knowledge of that is evident to reason necessarily, to the point that many rational people have come to that...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's answer. The objector argues that God's specific 'size' (or limit) is necessary by His essence, so it does not need a particularizer (mukhasis). The objector claims that created quantities need a determiner because they are possible (ja'iz), and possibility is known by being objects of power (maqdur). Since there is no power above God, the notion of possibility and need does not apply to Him. The objector then draws an analogy with the eight attributes: if we agree that the number of attributes is fixed, is that necessary or possible? If necessary, then the specific limit of God's essence is also necessary; if possible, then it needs a particularizer. The objector forces a dilemma. The author answers by rejecting the premise that possibility depends on being an object of power. He states that quantities in themselves, whether in the seen or unseen world, are subject to rational possibility and require a particularizer. Even if we assume a quantity in the physical world, it still requires a cause for its specific measure. The possibility of contingent things is self-evident to reason and does not depend on the power of an agent. The answer is cut off, but the author's position is clear: any determinate quantity, even for God, would require a particularizer, contradicting the divine nature.

Misconception: If something is necessary by essence, it automatically does not need any cause or particularizer.
Correction: The objector uses this premise to argue that God's size would be necessary, but the author later shows that all specific quantities are contingent and require a cause.

The objector uses the necessity of attributes to argue that God's size could also be necessary, creating a dilemma for the author.

The Author's Answer: All Quantities Require a Particularizer
Author's refutation that all quantities are contingent and need a particularizer, even in the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation that quantities, including those in the unseen, are subject to rational possibility and require a particularizer, regardless of being objects of power.

Core Idea
All quantities are contingent and need a cause, even for God.

The author responds by rejecting the objector's premise that possibility depends on being an object of power. He argues that quantities in themselves, whether in the seen or unseen world, are rationally possible and require a particularizer. Rational possibility is self-evident and does not depend on a power's estimation.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's answer
Read the answer section and notice how the author directly responds to the objection.
The author's argument is concise.
2
Understand the key claim
The author claims that quantities require a particularizer regardless of being objects of power. Find the supporting example.
3
Grasp the self-evidence of possibility
Note the statement that rational possibility does not depend on estimation of power but is necessary evident to reason.
This is a crucial point in kalām methodology.

Attributes Derived from Action and Revelation
First response to the objection about attributes limited to eight, denying number and arguing from action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the objection about limiting God's attributes to eight, and how the evidence of action and revelation determines the known attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are derived from action and revelation, not from arbitrary numbering.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection: why are God's attributes limited to eight? The first response argues that the very notion of number does not apply to divine attributes; rather, attributes are known through the action of God and confirmed by revelation. The passage then considers whether additional attributes are possible, revealing a nuanced approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a denial of number, a positive argument from action, and then a hypothetical question with two answers.
The passage is a response to an objection, so follow the logic.
2
Identify the two main arguments
First, the author denies that the term 'number' applies to attributes at all. Second, he argues that attributes are proven by the evidence of action (fi'l) and confirmed by revelation (shar'). Identify which attributes are derived from action.
The three attributes are power, will, and knowledge.
3
Compare the two answers to the hypothetical
The passage asks: could there be another attribute? One answer says no, because action only indicates these three. The other says yes, it is rationally possible, but we refrain because revelation does not mention it. Notice how both answers rely on action and revelation.
Think about how this affects belief and obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الصفات وانحصارها في ثمان فقد اختلف الجواب عنه بوجوه؛ منها أنهم منعوا إطلاق لفظ العدد عليها فضلا عن الثمانية. وقالوا قد دل الفعل بوقوعه على أن الفاعل قادر وباختصاصه ببعض الجايزات على أنه مريد وبإحكامه على أنه عالم وعلم بالضرورة أن القضايا مختلفة، وورد في الشرع إطلاق العلم والقدرة والإرادة ولا مدلول سوى ما دل الفعل عليه، أو ورد في الشرع إطلاقه، ولهذا اقتصرنا على ذلك، فلو سئل : هل يجوز أن يكون له صفة أخرى؟ اختلف الجواب عنه؛ فقيل: لا يتطرق الجواز إليه، فإنا لم نثبت له الصفات بطريق التجويز العقلي، بل بدليل الفعل، والفعل ما دل إلا على تلك الصفات، وقيل: يجوز عقلا إلا أن الشرع لم يرد به فيتوقف في ذلك، ولا يضرنا الاعتقاد إذا لم يرد به تكليف فينسب إلى المكلف تقصير
As for the attributes and their limitation to eight, the answer differed in several aspects. Among them: they denied the application of the term 'number' to them, let alone eight. They said: The action, by its occurrence, indicates that the agent is powerful, and by its specification to some contingents that He is willing, and by its perfection that He is knowing. And it is known necessarily that judgments differ. And in the Law the terms knowledge, power, and will are mentioned, and there is no denotation other than what the action indicates, or it is mentioned in the Law, and for this we restricted ourselves to that. So if it is asked: Is it possible that He has another attribute? The answer differed: Some said: Possibility does not apply to it, for we did not establish the attributes by way of rational possibility, but by the evidence of action, and the action only indicates those attributes. Others said: It is rationally possible, but the Law has not mentioned it, so we refrain from that, and belief does not harm us if there is no obligation mentioned, for then a shortcoming would be attributed to the obligated person.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several responses to the objection regarding the limitation of God's attributes to eight. The response begins by denying that the concept of number ('adad) applies to divine attributes at all, let alone the number eight. Instead, the attributes are derived from the evidence of action (fi'l). Actions indicate power (qudra), will (irāda), and knowledge ('ilm). The Law also mentions these three. The author then considers a hypothetical question: could there be another attribute? Two answers are given: one denies possibility entirely, as attributes are proven by action alone; the other concedes rational possibility but defers to the Law, meaning we do not affirm such an attribute unless it is revealed. This does not harm belief since no obligation is attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The number of attributes is arbitrary or based solely on reason.
Correction: The attributes are derived from the evidence of action and confirmed by revelation, not from arbitrary numbering or pure reason.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, why are attributes limited to power, will, and knowledge?
A) Because reason proves them exclusively
B) Because action and Law only indicate these
C) Because the number eight is sacred
D) Because other attributes are impossible

Key Takeaway
Attributes are not enumerated arbitrarily; they are derived from the evidence of action and confirmed by revelation, and any additional attribute is either impossible or rationally possible but not obligatory to affirm.

Second Answer: Distinguishing Essential Attributes from Accidental Measures
Second response distinguishing essential attributes from accidental measures in the witnessed realm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians distinguish between essential attributes that constitute a thing’s reality and accidental measures that are caused by an agent, and why this distinction protects divine attributes from being compared to created measures.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are uncaused and intrinsic; accidental measures are caused and contingent.

This passage presents a second response to an objection that attempted to limit divine attributes by analogy with created things. The theologians point to a fundamental distinction in the witnessed realm: some properties form the very essence of a thing, while others are accidental measures added by an external agent. Understanding this distinction is key to refuting false analogies about Allah’s attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style as he distinguishes two categories.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the distinction presented.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two types: essential attributes (صفات ذاتية) and accidental measures (مقادير عرضية). Note their definitions: essential attributes constitute reality, accidental measures do not.
Essential attributes are without cause; accidental measures are added by an agent with a cause.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this distinction refutes the objection. The objection claimed divine attributes are limited like created measures, but the response shows that essential attributes are uncaused and intrinsic, unlike accidental measures which are caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم فرقوا في الشاهد بين الصفات الذاتية التي تلتئم منها حقيقة الشيء، وبين المقادير العرضية التي لا مدخل لها في تحقيق حقيقة الشيء، فإن الصفات الذاتية لا تثبت للشيء مضافة إلى الفاعل، بل هي له من غير سبب، والمقادير المختلفة تثبت للشيء مضافة إلى الفاعل، فإن جعلها له بسبب.
Among them: They distinguished in the witnessed realm between essential attributes, from which the reality of a thing is composed, and accidental measures, which have no part in realizing the reality of a thing. For essential attributes are not established for a thing as added to the agent; rather, they belong to it without cause. While different measures are established for a thing as added to the agent; if they are made for it due to a cause.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a second response to the objection about the limitation of attributes. The author describes a distinction drawn in the witnessed (shāhid) realm between essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya) and accidental measures (maqādīr 'araḍiyya). Essential attributes are those from which the reality (ḥaqīqa) of a thing is constituted; they are not attributed to the thing as added by an external agent but belong to it without cause. Accidental measures, such as length or width, do not constitute the reality of a thing; they are established as added by an agent and therefore require a cause. The implication is that God's essential attributes (like knowledge, power, will) are uncaused and intrinsic, not comparable to created measures that are contingent and caused. This refutes the analogy drawn in the objection.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية
مقادير عرضية
حقيقة الشيء
شاهد
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The misconception that divine attributes are like created measures, requiring a cause and implying limitation.
Correction: Essential attributes are intrinsic and uncaused, while accidental measures are caused and contingent; thus, divine attributes are not comparable.

Checkpoint
According to the second response, what distinguishes essential attributes from accidental measures?
A) Essential attributes are caused, accidental are not
B) Essential attributes are added by the agent
C) Essential attributes constitute reality and are uncaused
D) Accidental measures are part of the essence

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are intrinsic and uncaused, while accidental measures are caused and added by an agent; thus, divine attributes are not analogous to created measures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The distinction between essential and accidental in this lesson points to Allah's attributes being truly essential and uncaused. In contrast, every good attribute you possess is a created loan. Will you sincerely acknowledge your complete poverty before Him, or will you continue to subtly claim ownership of what is not yours?

Denying Number for Divine Attributes
First objection: denial of numbering and evidential basis for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars reject numbering God's attributes and how they base the known attributes on evidence from actions and scripture.

Core Idea
Attributes are known from action and revelation, not by counting.

The first objection to limiting attributes to eight is that we should not even speak of numbering them. The proponents argue that attributes are derived from the evidence of God's acts and scriptural terms, not from rational enumeration. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the denial of applying the word 'number' to attributes.
The passage begins with 'وأما الصفات وانحصارها في ثمان فقد اختلف الجواب عنه بوجوه؛ منها أنهم منعوا إطلاق لفظ العدد عليها فضلا عن الثمانية'.
2
Identify the basis of the three known attributes
Notice how each attribute is inferred from the act: power from occurrence, will from specification, knowledge from perfection.
The text says 'قد دل الفعل بوقوعه على أن الفاعل قادر وباختصاصه ببعض الجايزات على أنه مريد وبإحكامه على أنه عالم'.
3
Understand the role of revelation
The text mentions that the revealed law also uses these terms, reinforcing the restriction to these three.
The phrase 'وورد في الشرع إطلاق العلم والقدرة والإرادة' shows reliance on both action and scripture.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider why the author says 'لهذا اقتصرنا على ذلك' — the restriction is not arbitrary but based on evidence.
This counters the idea that attributes can be multiplied freely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الصفات وانحصارها في ثمان فقد اختلف الجواب عنه بوجوه؛ منها أنهم منعوا إطلاق لفظ العدد عليها فضلا عن الثمانية. وقالوا قد دل الفعل بوقوعه على أن الفاعل قادر وباختصاصه ببعض الجايزات على أنه مريد وبإحكامه على أنه عالم وعلم بالضرورة أن القضايا مختلفة، وورد في الشرع إطلاق العلم والقدرة والإرادة ولا مدلول سوى ما دل الفعل عليه، أو ورد في الشرع إطلاقه، ولهذا اقتصرنا على ذلك، فلو سئل: هل يجوز أن يكون له صفة أخرى؟ اختلف الجواب عنه؛ فقيل: لا يتطرق الجواز إليه، فإنا لم نثبت له الصفات بطريق التجويز العقلي، بل بدليل الفعل، والفعل ما دل إلا على تلك الصفات، وقيل: يجوز عقلا إلا أن الشرع لم يرد به فيتوقف في ذلك، ولا يضرنا الاعتقاد إذا لم يرد به تكليف فينسب إلى المكلف تقصير.
As for the attributes and their being confined to eight, the answer has differed in several ways: among them is that they denied applying the word 'number' to them, let alone eight. They said: The act, by its occurrence, indicates that the agent is powerful, and by its specification to some possible things indicates that He is willing, and by its perfection indicates that He is knowing; and it is known necessarily that the judgments differ, and in the revealed law (shar‘) the terms 'knowledge', 'power', and 'will' occur, and there is no signification other than what the act indicates, or the revealed law indicates them, and for this we restricted ourselves to that. So if one asks: Is it possible that He has another attribute? The answer differs: Some say: possibility does not apply to it, for we did not establish attributes for Him by rational possibility, but by evidence of the act, and the act only indicates those attributes. And some say: It is rationally possible but the revealed law has not mentioned it, so we withhold judgment, and belief does not harm us if no obligation is mentioned so that negligence could be attributed to the obligated person.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection against limiting God's attributes to eight. The first answer is a denial of applying numerical categories to attributes altogether. The proponents argue that attributes are known through the evidence of actions (dalīl al-fi‘l): actions show power (qudra), will (irāda), and knowledge (‘ilm). The revealed law also uses these terms, so they restrict themselves to these. When asked if another attribute is possible, two opinions arise: one denies possibility because attributes are proven by action, and action only indicates these; the other allows rational possibility but requires scriptural evidence, and if the law is silent, no blame attaches.

Key Terms
الصفات
انحصارها
دل الفعل
قادر
مريد
عالم
إطلاق لفظ العدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes can be multiplied arbitrarily by rational speculation.
Correction: The passage asserts that attributes are limited to those indicated by action and scripture, not by rational possibility alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do we know that God is powerful?
A) By the act's occurrence
B) By the act's specification
C) By the act's perfection
D) By scriptural mention alone

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are known from the evidence of His actions and scriptural terms, not through rational counting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you witness events in your life—some occurring by your choice, others without—do you consciously see in them traces of Allah's power, will, and knowledge? If not, what prevents you from reading His attributes in His creation starting today?
?
The author says we should refrain from even applying the word 'number' to Allah's attributes. Does your heart feel uneasy without a precise enumeration? Are you willing to accept that knowing Allah through His acts and revelation is sufficient, even when your mind craves a countable list? What practical step can you take this week to deepen your experiential knowledge of His attributes rather than intellectual counting?

Question of additional attributes and two scholarly responses.

Core Idea
Two opinions: one denies any other attribute, the other allows rational possibility but requires scriptural evidence.

After establishing the basis for the known attributes, the text addresses a natural question: could God have another attribute not mentioned? Two answers emerge. This lesson examines both positions and their implications for theological method.

Essential vs. Accidental Attributes: A Key Distinction
Distinguishes essential attributes from accidental measures in created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental attributes in creation and how this distinction is used to argue about God's attributes.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are intrinsic; accidental measures depend on an external cause.

This passage introduces a second argument from the created world to clarify how we think about attributes. By examining how things in our world have essential and accidental features, the author provides a model for understanding God's attributes. We will explore this distinction and see how it guards against confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the key terms: الصفات الذاتية and المقادير العرضية.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two categories mentioned and note how each is defined. Essential attributes constitute the reality of a thing; accidental measures do not.
3
Reflect on the logical implication
Ask yourself why essential attributes cannot be attributed to an external agent. This will help you grasp the argument for God's attributes.
This distinction is a key tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم فرقوا في الشاهد بين الصفات الذاتية التي تلتئم منها حقيقة الشيء، وبين المقادير العرضية التي لا مدخل لها في تحقيق حقيقة الشيء، فإن الصفات الذاتية لا تثبت للشيء مضافة إلى الفاعل، بل هي له من غير سبب، والمقادير المختلفة تثبت للشيء مضافة إلى الفاعل، فإن جعلها له بسبب.
And among them is that they distinguished in the witnessed (shāhid) between the essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya) which constitute the reality of a thing, and the accidental measures (maqādīr ‘araḍiyya) which have no entry in the realization of the reality of a thing. For the essential attributes are not established for the thing as being attributed to the agent, but rather they belong to it without cause, while the different measures are established for the thing as being attributed to the agent, for they are made for it by a cause.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-response. The argument makes a distinction applied to created things (shāhid): essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya) are those that constitute the essence of a thing and are not added to the thing by an external agent; they are intrinsic. In contrast, accidental measures (maqādīr ‘araḍiyya) like size or quantity are accidental and are attributed to an external agent. This analogy is used to argue that God's attributes are essential, not accidental measures, and thus cannot be multiplied like accidental properties.

Key Terms
الصفات الذاتية
المقادير العرضية
حقيقة الشيء
مضافة إلى الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes of a thing are added by an external cause.
Correction: Only accidental measures depend on an external agent; essential attributes are intrinsic to the thing's reality.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which type of attribute is not attributed to an external agent?
A) Essential attributes
B) Accidental measures
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are intrinsic to a thing's reality and have no external cause, unlike accidental measures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson challenge you to rethink your sense of self-worth and dependence on Allah for your very essence?

This passage says essential attributes are intrinsic and without cause. But as a creature, your entire existence is caused by Allah. Does this mean you have no essential attributes at all? Or does your essence itself depend on Allah every moment? How does this truth humble you before your Creator?
?
If accidental measures are attributed to an agent, then consider your successes and failures—are they truly yours, or are they gifts or trials from Allah? Will you let this realization shift your pride into gratitude and your despair into reliance on Him?

Third Answer: Additional Attribute Impossible
Dichotomy proving impossibility of extra attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ashʿarīs argue that an additional attribute beyond the essential eight is impossible using a simple dichotomy.

Core Idea
Any hypothetical extra attribute must be perfect or deficient — both impossible for God.

This lesson presents a neat argument against the possibility of God having more than the eight essential attributes. The argument works by dividing all possible additional attributes into two categories: those that are perfections and those that are deficiencies. Each category is then shown to be impossible for God. By the end, you'll see why the Ashʿarī tradition concludes that only the eight attributes are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: the author sets up a dichotomy, examines each branch, and then concludes.
The argument is short but logically tight.
2
Identify the two categories
The author says any additional attribute must be either an attribute of praise and perfection (صفة مدح وكمال) or an attribute of blame and deficiency (صفة ذم ونقصان). Underline these two in the text.
This is a key step: all possibilities are exhausted.
3
Understand why each is impossible
If it were an attribute of perfection, its current absence would be a deficiency in God, impossible. If it were an attribute of deficiency, its absence is necessary. Both are invalid.
The argument assumes God already possesses all perfections.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since both possibilities are invalid, the only conclusion is that no additional attribute is permissible. Note how the conclusion is derived from the dichotomy.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم قالوا: لو قدرنا صفة زائدة على الصفات الثمانية لم يخل الحال فيها إما أن تكون صفة مدح وكمال أو تكون صفة ذم ونقصان، فإن كانت صفة كمال فعدمها في الحال نقص وقد اتصف الباري سبحانه بصفات الكمال من كل وجه، وإن كانت صفة نقصان فعدمها عنه واجب، وإذا بطل القسمان تعين أنه لا يجوز أن يتصف بصفة زائدة على الصفات الذاتية.
And among them is that they said: If we suppose an additional attribute beyond the eight attributes, the situation regarding it would not be free from either being an attribute of praise and perfection or being an attribute of blame and deficiency. If it is an attribute of perfection, then its absence in the present state is a deficiency, and the Creator (al-Bārī) is described with attributes of perfection in every respect. If it is an attribute of deficiency, then its absence from Him is necessary. Since both categories are invalid, it is established that it is not permissible for Him to be described with an attribute beyond the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection-response using a dichotomy: any hypothetical additional attribute must be either perfective (kamāl) or deficient (nuqṣān). If it were perfective, its current absence would imply a lack in God, which is impossible because God possesses all perfections. If it were deficient, its absence is necessarily required. Both cases are impossible, so no attribute beyond the essential eight can exist.

Key Terms
صفة مدح وكمال
صفة ذم ونقصان
صفات الكمال
الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could have additional attributes beyond the eight, perhaps because of His infinite nature.
Correction: This argument shows that any additional attribute would necessarily be either a perfection (which would imply a deficiency in God if absent) or a deficiency (which cannot be attributed to God). Hence, no additional attribute is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is an additional attribute of perfection impossible?
A) Because it would be redundant
B) Because its absence would mean God lacks a perfection
C) Because all perfections are already known
D) Because reason cannot grasp it

Key Takeaway
Any hypothetical additional attribute beyond the eight essential attributes is impossible because it would have to be either a perfection (whose absence would be a deficiency) or a deficiency (which cannot be attributed to God).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that something is 'missing' from God's perfection? When in your life have you questioned whether God's attributes are sufficient for your needs? This proof shows that any addition would be either redundant or impossible. Can you trust that God is completely perfect without wanting to add anything?
?
If you were to add one more attribute to God, what would it be? Is that desire rooted in a dissatisfaction with God as He has revealed Himself? Let this proof challenge your heart: Are you truly content with God's perfect description of Himself?
?
When you think about your own shortcomings, do you project them onto God? This dichotomy forces you to recognize that God cannot have deficiency. How often do you subconsciously expect God to have human flaws? Repent of any diminishing of His perfection and reaffirm that He is free from all deficiency.

The Question of God's Unique Specific Description
Presents two opinions on whether God has a unique specific description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether God has a unique specific description (akhass wasf) that distinguishes Him from creatures and the two main positions on this issue.

Core Idea
Whether God has a unique description beyond essence and attributes was a point of disagreement.

After discussing the possibility of additional attributes, the text turns to a more refined question: does God possess a single, unique description (akhass wasf) that distinguishes Him from all creation? This question touches on how we understand divine uniqueness. The author presents two opposing answers from the scholars of the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes this question from the previous one about additional attributes.
The passage begins by stating it follows from earlier discussion.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the two positions: the first denies a specific description, the second affirms it as 'divinity'. Notice the reasoning given for each.
Look for 'قال بعضهم' and 'وقال بعضهم'.
3
Compare the arguments
The first opinion argues that distinction is already achieved through essence and attributes. The second argues that any two distinct realities must have a specific description. Consider which argument you find more compelling.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'divinity'
The second opinion calls the specific description 'al-ilāhiyya' (divinity). Why might this description be unknowable? How does this connect to God's transcendence?
This touches on the limits of human knowledge of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويترتب على ما ذكرناه أنه هل يجوز للباري سبحانه أخص وصف لا ندركه وفرق بين هذا السؤال والسؤال الأول، فإن السائل الأول سأل: هل يجوز أن تزيد صفة على الصفات الثمانية؟ والسائل الثاني سأل: هل له أخص وصف به يتميز عن المخلوقات؟ واختلف جواب الأصحاب عنه أيضا؛ فقال بعضهم: ليس له أخص وصف، ولا يجوز أن يكون؛ لأنه بذاته وصفاته يتميز عن ذوات المخلوقات وصفاتها من حيث إن ذاته لا حد لها زمانا ومكانا، ولا يقبل الانقسام فعلا ووهما، بخلاف ذوات المخلوقات وصفاتها فإنهما غير متناهية في التعلق بالمتعلقات، فلو كان الغرض أن يتحقق أخص وصف به يقع التميز فقد وقع التميز بما ذكرنا فلا أخص سوى ما عرفنا. وقال بعضهم: له أخص وصف الإلهية لا ندركه، وذلك أن كل شيئين لهما حقيقتان معقولتان فإنهما يتمايزان بأخص وصفيهما.
And it follows from what we mentioned: Is it permissible for the Creator to have a specific description (akhass wasf) that we do not perceive? There is a difference between this question and the first question. The first question asked: Is it permissible for an attribute to exceed the eight attributes? The second question asked: Does He have a specific description by which He is distinguished from creatures? The answer of the companions also differed: Some said: He has no specific description, nor is it possible, because by His essence and attributes He is distinguished from the essences and attributes of creatures, by virtue that His essence has no limitation in time or place, and does not admit division actually or hypothetically, unlike the essences and attributes of creatures, for they are infinite in connection with objects of connection. If the purpose was to verify a specific description by which distinction occurs, then distinction has occurred by what we mentioned, so there is no specific description other than what we know. And some said: He has a specific description, divinity (al-ilāhiyya), which we do not perceive, because any two things with two intelligible realities are distinguished by their specific descriptions.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage poses a new question: Does God have a unique specific description (akhass wasf) that we cannot perceive? The author distinguishes it from the previous question about additional attributes. Two opinions are given: The first denies any specific description beyond essence and attributes, arguing that distinction from creatures is already achieved through God's timeless, spaceless, indivisible essence and infinite attributes. The second opinion affirms that God has a specific description, namely 'divinity' (al-ilāhiyya), but it is unknowable to us, just as any two distinct realities are ultimately distinguished by their unique specific descriptions.

Key Terms
أخص وصف
الإلهية
التميز
ذاته
صفاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a specific description must be something we can name or understand.
Correction: The passage shows that the specific description might be unknowable to us, as in the second opinion where it is identified as 'divinity' but we do not perceive it.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, what is God's specific description?
A) Power
B) Knowledge
C) Divinity (al-ilāhiyya)
D) There is no specific description

Key Takeaway
The debate about a specific divine description highlights the tension between affirming God's transcendence and acknowledging that our categories may not fully capture His uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God, do you ever assume that you have a full grasp of His uniqueness? How does the idea of an imperceptible specific description (al-ilāhiyya) challenge your intellectual pride?
?
The first opinion says God's distinction is already evident through His essence and attributes. Do you live with the constant awareness that God is utterly unlike anything in creation, or do you subconsciously picture Him in human terms? What will you do to cultivate that awareness today?
?
If some aspects of God's description are beyond your perception, does that trouble you or bring you peace? Reflect on whether you trust that God's reality is greater than your mind can contain, and what that trust demands of your worship.

The Inconceivability of an Attribute Beyond the Eight
Sanusi proves the impossibility of an additional attribute via exhaustive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof by division that shows God cannot have any attribute beyond the eight essential attributes.

Core Idea
Any hypothetical attribute beyond the eight would be either perfect or deficient – both impossible.

Imam Sanusi now delivers a crisp logical proof that the eight essential attributes are exhaustive. He does not rely on scriptural texts but on pure rational division. The argument is elegant: any additional attribute must be either a perfection or a deficiency, and both alternatives are impossible. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof structure
Recognize that the argument uses an exhaustive division (qismah) into two mutually exclusive categories.
This is a standard rational proof method in kalam.
2
Analyze the perfection branch
If the hypothetical attribute were a perfection, its absence would mean God lacks a perfection, contradicting His perfection in every respect.
Thus, the first branch is impossible.
3
Analyze the deficiency branch and conclusion
If the attribute were a deficiency, its absence is obligatory, so it cannot be attributed to God. Since both branches fail, no additional attribute is possible.
The conclusion is that the eight essential attributes are sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم قالوا: لو قدرنا صفة زائدة على الصفات الثمانية لم يخل الحال فيها إما أن تكون صفة مدح وكمال أو تكون صفة ذم ونقصان، فإن كانت صفة كمال فعدمها في الحال نقص وقد اتصف الباري سبحانه بصفات الكمال من كل وجه، وإن كانت صفة نقصان فعدمها عنه واجب، وإذا بطل القسمان تعين أنه لا يجوز أن يتصف بصفة زائدة على الصفات الذاتية.
From them is that they said: If we suppose an attribute additional to the eight attributes, the situation regarding it would not be free from either being an attribute of praise and perfection or being an attribute of blame and deficiency. If it is an attribute of perfection, then its absence in the present state is a deficiency, while the Exalted Creator is already described with attributes of perfection in every respect. If it is an attribute of deficiency, then its absence from Him is obligatory. Since both divisions are invalidated, it is determined that it is not permissible for Him to be described with an attribute additional to the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by division (qismah) that God cannot have any attribute beyond the eight essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-dhātiyyah). The proof begins by assuming a hypothetical additional attribute. It then divides the possible nature of such an attribute into two exhaustive categories: either it is an attribute of perfection and praise (ṣifat madḥ wa kamāl) or an attribute of blame and deficiency (ṣifat dhamm wa nuqṣān). The first alternative is impossible because if it were a perfection, its absence in God would be a deficiency, but God already possesses all perfections. The second alternative is impossible because a deficiency cannot be attributed to God, and its absence is necessary. Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the original assumption that there could be an additional attribute is false. The conclusion is that God cannot have any attribute beyond the essential attributes.

Key Terms
الصفات الذاتية
صفة كمال
صفة نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the eight attributes are exhaustive without reason.
Correction: The proof does not assume exhaustiveness; it proves it by showing that any additional attribute leads to contradiction, thus the eight are sufficient.

Checkpoint
Into which two categories does the author divide any possible additional attribute?
A) Attribute of essence and attribute of action
B) Attribute of perfection and attribute of deficiency
C) Attribute of praise and attribute of blame
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The eight essential attributes are rationally exhaustive; no additional attribute is possible without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that any deficiency is impossible for Allah, does your heart truly affirm that He is free from all shortcomings, or do you sometimes feel that He has let you down? What specific deficiency have you been attributing to Him in your trials, and how can you repent from that today?
?
If Allah is already perfect in every way, why do you often seek comfort in created things rather than in His perfect attributes? What does that reveal about your trust in His sufficiency, and how can you begin to turn to Him first?
?
The proof shows that an additional perfection would imply a previous lack. Do you live as if Allah needs your worship to be 'complete'? Or do you truly believe He is perfect regardless of your actions? How does this change the way you approach your prayers today?

Does God Have a Unique Distinctive Attribute?
Two views on whether God has a unique distinctive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing positions on whether God has a unique distinctive attribute and the arguments from negation vs. positive attribution.

Core Idea
The debate is whether distinction from creation requires a positive attribute or suffices through negations.

This passage from al-Shahrastani’s Nihayat al-Aqdam presents a theological dispute about whether God has a single most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) that distinguishes Him from everything else. The author first clarifies that this question is different from the earlier one about additional attributes beyond the eight. He then records two responses: one denying such an attribute and one affirming it as divinity (al-ilāhiyyah). By engaging with this passage, you will see how negative and positive attributes function in distinguishing God from creation.

Guided Path
1
Read and situate the passage
Notice the distinction the author draws between this question and the previous one about adding attributes to the eight. This sets the stage for the debate.
The previous question asked about an extra attribute; this one asks about a most specific attribute for distinction.
2
Identify the first view and its reasoning
The first group argues that God has no most specific attribute because His essence and attributes already distinguish Him through limitlessness, indivisibility, etc. Distinction is already achieved.
They rely on negative attributes to show uniqueness.
3
Identify the second view and its reasoning
The second group contends that God must have a positive distinctive attribute (al-ilāhiyyah) because negation alone cannot distinguish; all intelligible realities require a positive differentiator.
They argue that without a positive attribute, the very reality would be nullified.
4
Reflect on the role of negative vs. positive attributes
Compare how each side uses negative attributes (ṣifāt salb) and positive attributes (ṣifāt ithbāt). The first view holds that negations suffice; the second demands a positive basis for distinction.
This reflects deeper theological commitments about divine transcendence and our knowledge of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويترتب على ما ذكرناه أنه هل يجوز للباري سبحانه أخص وصف لا ندركه؟ وفرق بين هذا السؤال والسؤال الأول، فإن السائل الأول سأل: هل يجوز أن تزيد صفة على الصفات الثمانية؟ والسائل الثاني سأل: هل له أخص وصف به يتميز عن المخلوقات؟ واختلف جواب الأصحاب عنه أيضا؛ فقال بعضهم: ليس له أخص وصف، ولا يجوز أن يكون؛ لأنه بذاته وصفاته يتميز عن ذوات المخلوقات وصفاتها من حيث إن ذاته لا حد لها زمانًا ومكانًا، ولا يقبل الانقسام فعلا ووهما، بخلاف ذوات المخلوقات وصفاتها فإنها غير متناهية في التعلق بالمتعلقات، فلو كان الغرض أن يتحقق أخص وصف به يقع التميز فقد وقع التميز بما ذكرنا فلا أخص سوى ما عرفنا. وقال بعضهم: له أخص وصف الإلهية لا ندركه، وذلك أن كل شيئين لهما حقيقتان معقولتان فإنهما يتمايزان بأخص وصفيهما، وجميع ما ذكرنا من أن لا حد ولا نهاية ولا انقسام للذات ولا تناهي الصفات، كل ذلك سلوب وصفات نفي، وبالنفي لا يتميز الشيء عن الشيء، بل لا بد من صفة إثبات يقع بها التميز وإلا فترتفع الحقيقة رأسًا.
And it follows from what we have mentioned: Is it permissible for the Exalted Creator to have a most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) that we do not perceive? And there is a difference between this question and the first question. For the first asker asked: Is it permissible that an attribute be added to the eight attributes? The second asker asked: Does He have a most specific attribute by which He is distinguished from creatures? The answer of the companions (al-aṣḥāb) also differed regarding it. Some of them said: He does not have a most specific attribute, and it is not permissible that He have one, because by His essence and attributes He is distinguished from the essences of creatures and their attributes, in that His essence has no limit in time or place, and does not accept division actually or mentally, unlike the essences of creatures and their attributes, which are infinite in their relationship to their objects. So if the purpose were to realize a most specific attribute by which distinction occurs, distinction has already occurred by what we mentioned; so there is no most specific attribute other than what we know. And some of them said: He has a most specific attribute, namely al-ilāhiyyah (divinity), which we do not perceive, because any two things that have two intelligible realities are distinguished by their most specific attributes. And all that we mentioned—that there is no limit, no end, no division for the essence, and no finitude of the attributes—all that is negations and attributes of negation. And by negation a thing is not distinguished from another thing; rather a positive attribute (ṣifat ithbāt) is necessary for distinction, otherwise the reality would be entirely nullified.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two competing views on whether God has a unique distinctive attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf) that sets Him apart from creatures. The author distinguishes this question from the previous one about an additional attribute beyond the eight. The first group argues that God has no such special attribute because His essence and attributes already suffice for distinction: His essence is limitless in time and place, indivisible actually and mentally, whereas creatures are limited and divisible. Therefore, the distinction is already achieved, so no additional special attribute is needed. The second group argues that God must have a positive distinctive attribute, namely al-ilāhiyyah (divinity), because all things with intelligible realities are distinguished by their most specific attributes. Moreover, all the properties mentioned—limitlessness, indivisibility, etc.—are negative attributes (ṣifāt salb) or negations, and negation alone cannot positively distinguish one thing from another. A positive attribute (ṣifat ithbāt) is required for real distinction; otherwise, the reality would vanish. Thus, they affirm a special positive attribute that we do not perceive.

Key Terms
أخص وصف
الإلهية
صفات سلبية
صفات إثباتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether God has any attributes at all beyond the eight.
Correction: The debate specifically concerns whether God has a single unique positive attribute that is the basis of distinction, not whether He has attributes in general. The two positions agree that God has attributes; they differ on the need for a most specific positive one.

Checkpoint
According to the second group, why is a positive attribute (ṣifat ithbāt) necessary for distinction?
A) Because God's essence is unlimited
B) Because negation alone cannot distinguish one thing from another
C) Because creatures have infinite attributes
D) Because the eight attributes are insufficient

Key Takeaway
The debate reveals two approaches to divine distinction: through negative attributes (what God is not) or through a positive unique attribute (what God is).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you primarily rely on negations—what He is not—or do you have a positive, concrete attribute that anchors your worship? How does that affect your intimacy with Him?
?
The passage says a positive attribute is needed for true distinction; otherwise 'the reality would be entirely nullified.' Does your concept of God ever feel like a collection of 'nots'? What positive reality do you affirm about Him today that keeps your faith from evaporating into abstraction?
?
If God has a most specific attribute we cannot perceive, how does that humble your intellect? Are you willing to let an unknown 'al-ilāhiyyah' be the ground of your certainty, or do you insist on complete comprehension?

Can We Perceive God's Special Attribute?
Three opinions on perceiving God's special attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three scholarly opinions on whether humans can perceive God's most specific attribute, and why this question is considered perplexing.

Core Idea
Three opinions on perceiving God's special attribute, none conclusive.

The author raises a natural follow-up: even if we intellectually establish that God has a most specific attribute, can we actually perceive it? He reports three positions: absolute denial, general permission, and a mediated perception through a sixth sense at the vision of God. This debate highlights the limits of human inquiry into the divine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the opening conditional: if God has a special attribute, can we perceive it? This frames the entire discussion.
The question is about perception, not conceptual knowledge.
2
Learn the three opinions
Identify each distinct opinion: Imām al-Ḥaramayn's absolute denial, some scholars' permission, and Ḍirār ibn ʿAmr's conditional permission via a sixth sense at the vision.
Notice that the second opinion is unnamed, while the third is attributed to a specific early theologian.
3
Reflect on the perplexity
Read the author's concluding remark that this issue is among the perplexities of theologians and that conceptualizing the special attribute is bewildering.
This remark shows the author's humility before the difficulty of the topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا أثبت أن له أخص الوصف فهل يجوز أن ندركه؟ قال إمام الحرمين: لا يجوز أن ندركه أصلا، وقال بعضهم: يجوز أن يدرك، وقال ضرار بن عمرو: يدرك عند الرؤية بحاسة سادسة، ونفس المسألة من محارات المتكلمين وتصوير الأخص من محارات العقول.

Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage follows from the previous debate and addresses whether humans can perceive God's special distinctive attribute, assuming it exists. The author reports three opinions. Imām al-Ḥaramayn holds that it is absolutely impermissible to perceive it. Some unnamed scholars hold that it is permissible. Ḍirār ibn ʿAmr specifies that it will be perceived at the vision of God (al-ruʾyah) in the afterlife by a sixth sense, implying a non-ordinary sense. The author then remarks that this question is one of the perplexities (muḥārāt) of the theologians, and that conceptualizing the most specific attribute is itself bewildering. The passage does not decide between the views but presents the disagreement.

Necessity of Positive Attributes for Distinction
Author argues that negation alone cannot distinguish; positive attributes are necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative attributes alone are insufficient to distinguish God and why positive attributes are necessary for distinction.

Core Idea
Negation cannot distinguish; only positive attributes make distinction possible.

In this brief but crucial passage, the author argues that attributes of negation—such as having no limit or end—do not provide a basis for distinguishing one thing from another. Without a positive attribute, the very reality of a thing would be lost. This logic applies directly to the divine essence, establishing the necessity of positive attributes.

Guided Path

The passage is a single, dense sentence.
2
Understand the argument

3
Reflect on the implication

This connects to the broader theological discussion of divine attributes.

The author states that attributes of negation (like having no limit, no termination) are not sufficient to make the essence distinct from other things. Without a positive attribute, the very reality of the thing would disappear. So positive attributes are essential for distinguishing God.

Refuting the Second Doubt: Attributes Do Not Imply Spatial Location
The author refutes the claim that eternal attributes imply God's spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming eternal attributes for God does not necessitate spatial location, by distinguishing the theological meaning of 'qiyam' (inherence) from its physical sense.

Core Idea
Describing God by attributes does not put Him in a place.

This passage tackles a pressing objection: if God has attributes that 'inhere' in His essence, doesn't that make Him spatial like bodies? The author clarifies that the term 'qiyam' does not imply spatial location but rather the act of description. Understanding this distinction is crucial for preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Doubt
Identify the objection raised: since both the Karramiyya and the Sifatiyya agree that God has attributes inhering in His essence, the opponent claims this implies spatial location like accidents in bodies.
The doubt is presented as an 'ilzām' (pressing objection).
2
Grasp the Key Distinction
Focus on the response: the term 'qiyam' (inherence) does not mean spatial inherence. It means that God is described by those attributes. The act of describing does not require the described to occupy a place.
The author emphasizes that 'qiyam' here is a description, not physical location.
3
Connect to Divine Transcendence
Reflect on the broader principle: God's attributes do not imply any spatial or directional relation. This preserves Allah's transcendence (tanzih) and avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشك الثاني الذي أوردوه على سبيل الإلزام، وهو الاتفاق من الكرامية والصفاتية أن الله سبحانه ذات وصفات والصفات قائمة بالذات وليست كل صفة بحيث الثانية بل بحيث الذات. قيل: من أثبت الصفات الأزلية قائمة بذات الباري تعالى ليس يعني بالقيام ما عنيتم، ولا أطلق أنها بحيث هو كما أطلقتم، بل يعني بالقيام وصف الباري سبحانه وتعالى بها، والوصف من حيث هو وصف لا يقتضي أن يكون الموصوف بحيز وجهة، ثم الحكم للوصف بأنه معنى موجود أو حال، فهو بمعنى آخر، وإلا فمجرد الوصف والصفة من حيث هو وصف لا يقتضي ذلك، ثم إطلاق لفظ.
As for the second doubt which they raised by way of elzām, and it is the agreement of the Karramiyya and the Sifatiyya that God is an essence and attributes, and the attributes inhere in the essence, and not every attribute is in the manner of the second [attribute] but in the manner of the essence. It was said: Those who affirm the eternal attributes as inhering in the essence of the Most High do not mean by 'inherence' what you mean, nor do they apply that they are in the manner of Him as you apply, but rather they mean by 'inherence' the description of the Most High by them, and the description insofar as it is a description does not necessitate that the described be in a spatial location or direction. Then the judgment that the description is an existing meaning or a state is with another meaning. Otherwise, mere description and attribute insofar as it is a description does not necessitate that. Then the utterance of the word.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second doubt (al-shakk al-thānī) raised by the Karramiyya and the Sifatiyya. The doubt is an elzām (pressing objection): since both groups agree that God has an essence and attributes, and the attributes inhere (qāʼima) in the essence, and each attribute is not in the manner of another attribute but in the manner of the essence—this supposedly implies spatial location. The response distinguishes the meaning of qiyām (inherence). When theologians affirm that eternal attributes inhere in God's essence, they do not mean 'inherence' in the spatial sense of occupying a place. Rather, they mean that God is described by those attributes. The act of describing, as such, does not require the described to be in a spatial location or direction. Additionally, considering attributes as distinct entities (existing meanings or states) is a different conceptual issue. The mere fact of being described does not entail spatiality. The passage ends abruptly with 'thumma iṭlāq lafẓ' (then the utterance of the word), possibly introducing further clarification cut off by the manuscript break.

Key Terms
شك
إلزام
صفات أزلية
قيام
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'qiyam' (inherence) of attributes implies a spatial relation like a body having an accident.
Correction: In theological discourse, 'qiyam' means that God is described by the attribute, not that the attribute occupies a location within God.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what do theologians mean when they say attributes 'inhere' (qāʼima) in God's essence?
A) That the attributes occupy specific locations within God's essence
B) That God is described by those attributes, not that they are spatial
C) That the attributes are separate from God but connected
D) That God is the locus for accidents

Key Takeaway
Affirming eternal attributes does not make God spatial; 'inherence' means description, not physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes like His Knowledge or Power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as 'things' located somewhere? What specific thought or habit will you change today to purify your conception of Allah from spatial limits?
?
The author says that describing Allah does not put Him in a place. Yet our own descriptions of Allah often come from our experience of created things. Which of your own words or thoughts about Allah might indirectly limit Him, and how will you correct them starting this prayer?
?
If affirming that Allah has attributes is true, but you still feel a subtle unease that it might reduce His transcendence, what does that reveal about your heart's trust in the scholars who clarified this? Will you commit to studying more deeply until that unease is replaced with certainty?

The Karamiyya Doctrine of Accidents in God's Essence
Author describes the Karamiyya view that accidents occur in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karamiyya view that accidents can occur in God's essence, and their distinction between the accident of creative command and other divine speech.

Core Idea
The Karamiyya accept accidents in God but limit them to will and the word 'Kun'.

This passage presents a controversial sectarian doctrine. The Karamiyya, unlike mainstream Sunni theologians, permit the occurrence of accidents (ahdath) in God's essence. They specify that these accidents are will and the creative command 'Kun', while other divine attributes like reports and scriptures are not considered accidents. Understanding this view helps clarify boundaries of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and locate the statement that connecting accidents to God necessitates anthropomorphism (tashbih). Notice that the Karamiyya allow this.
This sets up the controversial nature of their doctrine.
2
Define 'accidents' according to the Karamiyya
The text defines accidents (al-ahdath) as attributes occurring in God's essence: will (irada) and speech composed of letters, specifically the word 'Kun'. Understand that these are the only two types.
Note that will is an accident because it specifies an act in time.
3
Distinguish creative speech from other divine speech
The Karamiyya differentiate between the creative command 'Kun' (which is an accident) and other sayings such as reports, commands, stories, etc. These other sayings occur by God's eternal power and are not considered accidents. Grasp this nuance.
This distinction is key to their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما أوجب التشبيه قيام الحوادث بذاته سبحانه، وقد ذهبت الكرامية إلى جواز ذلك، ومن مذهبهم أنما يحدث من المحدثات فإنما يحدث بإحداث الباري سبحانه، والأحداث عبارة عن صفات تحدث في ذاته من إرادة لتخصيص الفعل بالوجود ومن أقوال مرتبة من حروف مثل قوله كن، وأما سائر الأقوال كالأخبار عن الأمور الماضية والآتية، والكتب المنزلة على الرسل عليهم السلام، والقصص والوعد والوعيد، والأحكام والأوامر والنواهي، والتسمعات للمسموعات والتبصرات للمبصرات فتحدث في ذاته بقدرته الأزلية، وليست هي من الأحداث في شيء، وعلى طريقة إنما الأحداث في الخلق على مذهب أكثرهم قول وإرادة والقول هما صورتان هما حرفان.
And among things that necessitate anthropomorphism is the occurrence of accidents in His essence, exalted be He. The Karamiyya have held the permissibility of that. From their doctrine is that whatever occurs among originated things, it occurs only by the origination of the Creator, exalted be He. And 'accidents' (al-ahdath) are attributes that occur in His essence, consisting of will to specify the act with existence, and of sayings arranged from letters like His saying 'Be' (Kun). As for other sayings—such as reports about past and future matters, the books revealed to the messengers, peace be upon them, the stories, the promise and threat, the rulings, commands and prohibitions, the hearings of heard things, and the seeings of seen things—they occur in His essence by His eternal power, and they are not among the accidents at all. And according to the way, indeed the accidents in creation, on the doctrine of most of them, are speech and will, and the speech is two forms which are two letters.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage describes the Karamiyya doctrine that accidents occur in God's essence. The author begins by stating that belief in accidents in God leads to anthropomorphism (tashbih). The Karamiyya permit this, holding that all originated things come about by God's origination. They define accidents (ahdath) as attributes occurring in God's essence, specifically will (irada) to specify an act, and sayings composed of letters like 'Kun' (Be). They distinguish between two types of speech: first, the creative command 'Kun', which is an accident; second, other speech like reports, scriptures, stories, promises, commands, etc., which also occur in His essence by His eternal power but are not considered 'accidents' in the same sense. Finally, they state that according to most of them, the accidents in creation are only speech and will, and speech consists of two forms which are two letters (i.e., the kaf and nun of 'Kun').

الكرامية

كن
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karamiyya believe all divine attributes are accidents.
Correction: They only consider will and the word 'Kun' as accidents; other attributes like reports, commands, etc. occur but are not accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the Karamiyya as described, what are the two types of accidents that occur in God's essence for each originated thing?
A) Will and hearing
B) Will and speech (the word 'Kun')
C) Power and seeing
D) Knowledge and life

Key Takeaway
The Karamiyya believe that accidents (will and 'Kun') occur in God's essence, but other divine attributes like reports and commands are not accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God wills' or 'God speaks', do you ever inadvertently imagine Him having human-like actions? This lesson warns that attributing accidents to God leads to anthropomorphism. Examine your own heart: have you ever pictured God's will or speech as resembling your own? How will you guard your thoughts to ensure you worship God as He truly is, not as you imagine?
?
The Karamiyya limited accidents in God to will and the word 'Kun', but the author still condemns this as anthropomorphism. Does it ever bother you that even seemingly respectful descriptions of God (like 'God said') might be tainted by human imagination? Are you willing to embrace the paradox that God's speech is not like our speech, even if you cannot fully grasp it?
?

Do you live as though every moment of your life is perceived by God who is not affected by time? How does this truth challenge your private thoughts and actions?

Ibn al-Haysam's View: Accidents in God's Essence
Ibn al-Haysam's view on accidents and five attributes in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversial view that accidents occur in God's essence, including the five attributes per event, and how they are distinguished from God's attributes.

Core Idea
Accidents in God's essence do not become His attributes.

The Ash'ari theologian Ibn al-Haysam proposed a unique theory about accidents (hawadith) occurring within God's essence. This passage outlines his view that for each created thing, five specific attributes occur in God, yet these are not equated with God's eternal attributes. This was a highly debated position among early theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the original Arabic and its literal English translation. Note the technical terms like إرادة, إيثار, كاف, نون.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the five attributes
The text lists five attributes per originated thing. Find them: إرادة (will), كاف (kaf), نون (nun), تسمع (hearing), تبصر (seeing).
Kaf and nun are the letters of the command 'Kun'.
3
Note the distinction between accidents and attributes
The passage explicitly states that these accidents do not become attributes of God. Instead, God is described by creatorship (خالقية), will-ness (إرادية), and speaker-ness (قائلية).
This is a crucial point to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Understand the role of the eternal will
An eternal will (مشية قديمة) relates to originated things, originator, accidents, and creation. This underlies the occurrence of accidents.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this view differs from orthodox Ash'ari theology, which denies any change or temporality in God's essence.
This was a minority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى طريقة محمد بن الهيصم الأحداث إرادة وإيثار وذلك مشروط بالقول شرعًا، وجوز بعضهم تعلق أحداث واحد بمحدثين إذا كانا من جنس واحد، وأكثرهم على أن لكل محدث أحداث فيحدث في ذاته لكل محدث خمس صفات؛ إرادة وكاف ونون وتسمع وتبصر، وقد أثبتوا مشية قديمة تتعلق بالحادث والمحدث والأحداث والخلق، ثم قالوا هذه الحوادث لا تصير صفات الله تعالى، وإنما هو خالق بخالقيته لا بخلق مريد بإراديته لا بإرادة قايل بقائليته، وهي واجبة البقاء، ويستحيل عدمها بعد وجودها في ذاتها.
And according to the way of Muhammad ibn al-Haysam, the accidents are will and preference, and that is conditioned by speech legally. And some of them permitted that one accident be attached to two originated things if they are of one kind. And most of them hold that for every originated thing there are accidents, so there occur in His essence for every originated thing five attributes: will, kaf, nun, hearing, and seeing. And they have affirmed an eternal will that pertains to the originated thing, the originator, the accidents, and creation. Then they said: these accidents do not become attributes of God, exalted. Rather, He is a creator by His creatorship, not by a creating; willing by His will-ness, not by a will; a speaker by His speaker-ness. And they are necessary of remaining, and it is impossible for them to cease after their existence in His essence.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Muhammad ibn al-Haysam and other sectarians on the nature of accidents in God's essence. Ibn al-Haysam specifies that the accidents are will (irada) and preference (ithar), with the condition that they are accompanied by speech (qawl) according to the Law. Some of them allowed a single accident to be related to two originated things if they are of the same kind. Most, however, hold that for each originated thing, five attributes occur in God's essence: will, the letters kaf and nun (i.e., the word 'Kun'), hearing (tasmua') and seeing (tabsur). They also affirm an eternal will (mashiyya qadima) that relates to the originated thing, the originator, accidents, and creation. Importantly, they assert that these accidents do not become attributes of God; rather, God is described as creator by His creatorship (khalqiyya), not by a creating act; willing by His 'will-ness' (iradiyya), not by a will; and speaker by His 'speaker-ness' (qailiyya). These accidents are necessary in their subsistence—it is impossible for them to cease after they exist in His essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the accidents occurring in God's essence become attributes of God.
Correction: The text explicitly states that these accidents do not become attributes of God. Instead, God is described by creatorship (خالقية), will-ness (إرادية), and speaker-ness (قائلية), which are not the accidents themselves.

Checkpoint

A) Two: will and speech
B) Three: will, preference, and speech
C) Five: will, kaf, nun, hearing, seeing
D) Seven: the above plus power and knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Haysam's theory introduces accidents in God while maintaining they are not attributes, a nuanced position in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that these accidents 'do not become attributes of God,' do you find yourself assuming that everything that occurs in God's essence must be His attribute? How does this distinction challenge your own understanding of God's transcendence? Write a short prayer asking God to help you know Him as He truly is, not as you imagine.
?
The passage affirms an 'eternal will' that pertains to everything. Have you ever struggled to accept that God's will includes even your own small actions? Sit with the idea that your every choice is encompassed by His eternal will—how does that change the way you approach your next decision?
?
Ibn al-Haysam's group said accidents are necessary of remaining and cannot cease. Consider something in your life that you thought was temporary but God has sustained. Are you quick to attribute His ongoing creation to Him, or do you take existence for granted? Make a list tonight of five things that 'remain' by God's power, and thank Him.
