First Proof: Accidents in God Would Entail Change
Author presents the first burhan that accidents cannot subsist in God because that would entail change and contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot subsist in God's essence: the first demonstration shows that qualification by accidents entails change, and change is evidence of contingency.

Core Idea
Change in God would prove His contingency—so accidents cannot be in Him.

This lesson presents the first of two methods theologians use to argue that accidents cannot subsist in God. The author lays out a straightforward demonstration: if accidents were to subsist in God, He would become qualified by them after not being qualified, which constitutes change (taghayyur), and change implies contingency (ḥudūth). This foundational argument sets the stage for deeper discussions on divine attributes and the rejection of temporal qualities in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Pay attention to the author's precise phrasing, especially the terms 'taghayyur' (change) and 'ḥudūth' (contingency).
Notice the dense style: the author packs many premises into a few lines.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The author mentions two methods (demonstration and contradiction-in-implication) but only elaborates the demonstration. Break down the demonstration: premise 1: accidents subsisting in God would make Him qualified; premise 2: qualification after non-qualification is change; premise 3: change requires a changer, thus contingency.
The opponent's only rebuttals are to deny qualification or deny change—both of which the author claims to have established.
3
Understand the opponent's distinction
The opponent distinguishes between attributes like knowledge (which yield 'being knowing') and attributes like motion (which yield 'being moving'). The author argues this distinction is irrelevant because qualification by any attribute, whether pre-eternal or temporal, is the same in terms of being an attribute.
The author insists that once a meaning subsists in an essence, it necessarily becomes an attribute.
4
Reflect on the implications
This argument refutes the idea that God can have accidental attributes. Reflect on how this connects to divine simplicity: if God could have accidents, He would change, making Him contingent.
This is a cornerstone of classical kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين عليه طريقان في الكلام؛ أحدهما البرهان، والثاني المناقضة في الإلزام. أما البرهان فنقول: لو قامت الحوادث بذات الباري سبحانه وتعالى لاتصف بها بعد أن لم يتصف، ولو اتصف لتغير والتغير دليل الحدوث؛ إذ لا بد من مغير، وتحقيق المقدمة الأولى أن معنى قيام الأعراض بمحالها كونها أوصافا لها، كالعلم إذا قام بجوهر وصف الجوهر بأنه عالم، وكذلك ساير المعاني والأعراض، فليس ذلك كالوصف بكون الباري تعالى خالقا صانعا على مذهب من لم يفرق بين الخلق والمخلوق، فإن المخلوق لا يقوم بذات الخالق والخلق قائم بذاته تعالى عندكم، فيجب أن يكون وصفا له. وكذلك يقال: أراد فهو مريد بإرادة، وقال فهو قائل بقول، وإذا تحقق كونه وصفا له بعد أن لم يكن موصوفا به فقد تحقق التغير، والتغير خروج شيء إلى غير ما كان عليه، ولا يشترط فيه بطلان صفة وتجدد صفة، فإنه إذا كان خاليا من صفات ثم اعتراه صفات فقد تغير عما كان عليه، فليس للخصم اعتراض على هذه الطريقة إلا منع الاتصاف أو منع التغير، وقد أثبتناهما، ولكنه وضع لنفسه اصطلاحًا في أحكام المعاني القائمة بالغير، وميز بين حكم العلم والقدرة وهي العالمية والقادرية والمريدية، وبين حكم الحركة والسكون القائمين بالجوهر وهو المتحركية والساكنية، فإن كان هذا التميز غير مقبول عقلا، فإن اتصاف المحال بالأوصاف الحادثة واتصاف الذات بالأوصاف من حيث إنها صفات وموصوفات ليس تختلف، ولا تأثير للقدم والحدوث فيها أصلا فإنه إن كان الوصف راجعا إلى القول واللسان فلا يختلف الحال بين وصف ووصف، وإن كان الوصف راجعا إلى حقيقة في الموصوف يعبر عنها لسان الواصف فلا يختلف الحال بين حقيقة وحقيقة، والمعنى إذا قام بذات أو محل صار وصفا وصفة لها حكمه إليه ورجع بالضرورة.
And for the theologians there are two ways regarding this: one is demonstration (al-burhān), and the second is contradiction in implication (al-munāqaḍa fī al-ilzām). As for the demonstration, we say: If accidents (al-ḥawādith) were to subsist in the essence of the Creator (al-Bārī), may He be exalted, He would become qualified by them after not having been qualified, and if He became qualified, He would change, and change (al-taghayyur) is evidence of contingency (al-ḥudūth); for there must be a changer. The verification of the first premise is that the meaning of accidents subsisting in their loci is that they are attributes for them, like knowledge when it subsists in a substance, the substance is described as knowing, and similarly for other meanings and accidents. This is not like the description of the Creator, exalted, as Creator and Maker according to the view of one who does not differentiate between the act of creation (al-khalq) and the created (al-makhlūq), for the created does not subsist in the essence of the Creator, while the act of creation subsists in His essence, exalted, according to you, so it must be an attribute for Him. Likewise it is said: He willed, so He is a willer (murīd) by a will (irāda), and He said, so He is a speaker (qā’il) by a speech (qawl). And when it is established that it is an attribute for Him after He was not described by it, then change has occurred. Change is the exiting of a thing from what it was upon, and it is not conditional on the nullification of one attribute and the renewal of another; for if something was devoid of attributes and then attributes befell it, it has changed from what it was upon. So the opponent has no objection to this way except to deny the qualification or to deny the change, and we have established both. But he has set for himself a convention concerning the judgments of meanings subsisting in another, and he distinguishes between the judgment of knowledge and power—namely being knowing (al-‘ālimiyya), being powerful (al-qādriyya), and being willing (al-murīdiyya)—and between the judgment of motion and rest subsisting in a substance—namely being moving (al-mutaḥarrikiyya) and being at rest (as-sākiniyya). If this distinction is not intellectually acceptable, then the qualification of loci by accidental attributes (al-awṣāf al-ḥāditha) and the qualification of the essence by attributes, in terms of them being attributes and described things, do not differ, and the fact of pre-eternity or contingency has no effect on them at all. For if the description (al-waṣf) refers to speech and tongue, then the situation does not differ between one description and another; and if the description refers to a reality in the described which the tongue of the describer expresses, then the situation does not differ between one reality and another. And a meaning, when it subsists in an essence or a locus, becomes a description and an attribute for it, its judgment is referred to it and returns by necessity.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the two ways theologians use to argue that accidents cannot subsist in God's essence: the way of demonstration (burhān). The author begins by stating that there are two methods: demonstration and contradiction-in-implication. Then he lays out the demonstration: If accidents (hawādith) were to subsist in God's essence, He would become qualified by them after not being so qualified; such qualification would entail change (taghayyur), and change is evidence of contingency (ḥudūth) because a changer is needed. The author then clarifies the first premise: the meaning of accidents subsisting in their loci is that they become attributes of those loci. For example, when knowledge subsists in a substance, the substance is described as 'knowing'. He distinguishes this from the description 'Creator', which does not imply an accident subsisting in God. According to the opposing view (the Ashʿarites), the act of creation (khalq) subsists in God's essence without being an accident in the usual sense, but the author argues that if we hold that accidents would be attributes, then God's becoming qualified by them after not being so is a change. He then argues that change does not require the nullification of one attribute and the advent of another; it is sufficient that something receives new attributes after lacking them. The opponent can only deny either that such accidents would be attributes (i.e., deny that they qualify God) or deny that this constitutes change. The author asserts he has established both. He then addresses an opponent's distinction between types of attributes: some like knowledge and power result in 'being knowing', 'being powerful' etc., while others like motion and rest result in 'being moving', 'being at rest'. The author rejects this distinction as intellectually unacceptable: qualification by accidental attributes is the same whether the attributes are pre-eternal or temporal; the fact of pre-eternity or contingency does not affect the nature of being an attribute. If 'description' (waṣf) is a linguistic matter, then there is no difference between describing God as 'knowing' and describing Him as 'moving' if the latter were an attribute; if 'description' refers to a real attribute in the described, then again there is no difference. In short, whenever a meaning subsists in an essence, it becomes an attribute for it by necessity.

Key Terms
البرهان
المناقضة في الإلزام
قيام الأعراض
التغير
الحدوث
الاتصاف
الوصف
الصفة
العالمية
القادرية
المريدية
المتحركية
الساكنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change only occurs when one attribute is replaced by another.
Correction: The author clarifies that receiving new attributes after having none is also change; nullification of a previous attribute is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first burhan, why would accidents subsisting in God's essence entail that He is contingent?
A) Because accidents are temporal and would make God temporal.
B) Because qualification by accidents after not being qualified is change, and change indicates contingency.
C) Because God cannot be described by multiple attributes.
D) Because accidents are created and cannot be attributed to the Creator.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot subsist in God because they would entail change and thus contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot change, what does that mean for your prayers when you ask Him to alter your circumstances? Are you truly content with His unchangeable will, or do you secretly wish He would become different for your sake?
?
The argument proves that change in God would make Him contingent. How does that truth expose any hidden desire you have for God to act according to your expectations rather than His perfect, unchanging nature?
?
When you face disappointment, do you find yourself doubting God's goodness because He did not change your situation? How can this lesson about God's unchangeable attributes strengthen your trust in His wisdom and sovereignty?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Nature of Attributes and Their Distinction from Accidents
The author distinguishes between divine attributes and accidental qualities, arguing that time does not affect the reality of description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the reality of description does not change between eternal and temporal attributes according to the author.

Core Idea
Eternity and temporality have no effect on the reality of description.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection against divine attributes: that attributing eternal qualities to God is different from describing temporal things. He argues that the act of description—whether in speech or in the reality of the described—is uniform. Let's see how he presents this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
The author first distinguishes between divine attributes (like knowerhood, powerhood) and accidents (like movinghood, restinghood). Note that attributes subsist in another (the Essence), while accidents subsist in a substance.
This sets up the contrast.
2
Grasp the conditional argument
If this distinction is not rationally acceptable, then describing impossible things (temporal accidents) and describing the divine Essence are not different. Eternity and temporality have no effect on the reality of description.
The author uses a reductio-like reasoning.
3
Follow the two cases
The author explains that description either refers to linguistic expression or to an actual reality in the described. In both cases, there is no difference between eternal and temporal attributes.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The meaning, when it subsists in an essence or locus, becomes a description and an attribute with its ruling, regardless of time. Thus, the reality of description is uniform.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعاني القائمة بالغير، وميز بين حكم العلم والقدرة وهي العالمية والقادرية والمريدية، وبين حكم الحركة والسكون القائمين بالجوهر وهو المتحركية والساكنية، فإن كان هذا التميز غير مقبول عقلا، فإن اتصاف المحال بالأوصاف الحادثة واتصاف الذات بالأوصاف من حيث إنها صفات وموصوفات ليس تختلف، ولا تأثير للقدم والحدوث فيها أصلا فإنه إن كان الوصف راجعا إلى القول واللسان فلا يختلف الحال بين وصف ووصف، وإن كان الوصف راجعا إلى حقيقة في الموصوف يعبر عنها لسان الواصف فلا يختلف الحال بين حقيقة وحقيقة، والمعنى إذا قام بذات أو محل صار وصفا وصفة لها حكمه إليه ورجع بالضرورة.
The meanings subsisting in another [entity], and he distinguished between the ruling of knowledge and power, which are knowerhood and powerhood and willhood, and between the ruling of motion and rest subsisting in the substance, which are movinghood and restinghood. If this distinction is not rationally acceptable, then the description of impossible things with temporal attributes and the description of the Essence with attributes, inasmuch as they are attributes and described, does not differ, and there is no effect of eternity and temporality upon them at all. For if the description refers to speech and tongue, the case between one description and another does not differ. And if the description refers to a reality in the described expressed by the tongue of the describer, the case between one reality and another does not differ. And the meaning, when it subsists in an essence or a locus, becomes a description and an attribute having its ruling, and it refers and returns necessarily.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of attributes. The author begins by stating that attributes like knowledge and power (which are knowerhood, powerhood, willhood) are distinct from accidents like motion and rest (movinghood, restinghood). He then argues that if this distinction is not accepted rationally, then one must admit that describing impossible things (temporal accidents) and describing the divine Essence are not different in reality. Eternity and temporality have no effect on the reality of description. The author explains that description either refers to the linguistic expression or to the actual reality in the described. In both cases, there is no difference—whether the attribute is eternal or temporal, the act of describing is the same. Thus, the reality of a meaning subsisting in a subject making it an attribute is uniform.

Misconception: Some may think that eternal and temporal attributes differ in their reality.
Correction: The author shows they are identical in description; time has no effect.

Checkpoint

A) They change the reality.
B) They have no effect.
C) They affect only linguistic description.
D) They affect only the description of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The reality of description is unaffected by whether the attribute is eternal or temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the description is the same, what separates your awe of God from your acknowledgment of human ability? Will you let this truth deepen your reverence, or will you remain casual?
?
When you attribute mercy to God, do you subconsciously think of mercy as a passing emotion? This lesson says that description does not differ whether eternal or temporal. But the reality behind the description does. How does knowing that God's mercy is not like your temporary feelings change how you seek it in your prayers today?
?
The author says there is 'no effect of eternity and temporality' on the reality of description. If the act of describing God and describing a creature is the same, then you cannot excuse your weak descriptions of God by saying 'He is beyond my words'. Will you commit to using precise, reverent language for God, knowing that your description is not lacking compared to describing temporal things?

The Objection from Likeness of Speech and Will
An objection and its response regarding the nature of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objection (ilzam) against equating divine speech with human speech and the response that distinguishes them by divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's speech creates; ours does not — the difference is in the divine power, not the words.

This passage presents a dilemma posed to those who claim God's speech is of the same kind as human speech. If true, then our words should also be able to create the universe. The response clarifies that divine speech is uniquely accompanied by eternal power and will. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the gist. Note the two parts: the objection and the response.
The objection begins with 'وأما طرق الإلزام' and the response with 'فإن قيل'.
2
Identify the objection's premise and conclusion
The objection assumes that God's speech and will are of the same kind as ours, then concludes that our words should also create.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine the response's distinctions
The response points out three differences: God's speech is by direct involvement of His power, will, and eternal volition; it is in His essence; and He intended creation by it.
These distinctions show that the analogy fails.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why the answer is necessary to defend the uniqueness of divine attributes.
This lesson guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما طرق الإلزام عليهم فمنها أن قالوا : قول الله سبحانه وتعالى وإرادته من جنس قولنا وإرادتنا، ثم لقوله وإرادته مفعولات مثل العالم بما فيه من السموات والأرض فيلزم أن يحصل بإرادتنا وقولنا مثل ذلك، فإن قوله كن كاف ونون من جنس أقوالنا من غير فرق. فإن قيل : إنما حصل قوله بمباشرة قدرته وإرادته ومشيئته القديمة، وقولنا لم يحصل بهما، وقوله في ذاته قول له لا لغيره، وقد قصد به التكوين لا بغيره.
And among the ways of compelling them is that they said: The speech of God, glorified and exalted, and His will are of the same kind as our speech and our will. Then His speech and will have objects like the world and what it contains of the heavens and the earth. So it would be necessary that by our will and speech something like that occurs. For His saying 'Be' is kaf and nun, of the same kind as our words with no difference. If it is said: His speech occurred only by the direct involvement of His power, will, and eternal volition, and our speech did not occur by these; and His speech in His essence is speech for Him, not for another, and He intended by it bringing into existence, not by another.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (ilzam) against those who equate God's speech (qawl) and will (irada) with human speech and will. The argument is that if God's speech 'Be' (kun) is of the same kind (jins) as our words, and His speech produces the entire universe, then our words should also be able to produce similar effects. This is a reductio ad absurdum. The answer (fa-in qila) is given: God's speech occurs by the direct involvement (mubashara) of His power, will, and eternal volition (mashiyya qadima), whereas our speech does not. Moreover, God's speech is in His essence and is for Him alone, and He intended by it creation (takwin). Thus the difference is not in the linguistic form but in the divine attributes with which it is accompanied. The answer shows that the analogy fails because the divine speech is uniquely linked to omnipotence.

Key Terms

إرادة (irada)
مشیئة (mashi'a)
كن (kun)
مباشرة (mubashara)
تكوين (takwin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's word 'Be' is a literal sound or word like human speech without any difference.
Correction: The passage clarifies that although the word 'Be' is of the same linguistic kind, it is accompanied by eternal attributes such as power, will, and volition, which make it uniquely creative.

Checkpoint

B) If God's speech is like ours, then our speech should also be able to create
C) If God's speech is like ours, then it must be created
D) If God's speech is like ours, then it would have letters and sounds

Key Takeaway
The objection attempts to reduce equality of speech to absurdity, but the response shows that divine speech is distinguished by its association with eternal power and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Be' (kun) in the Quran, do you recognize that it is not a lifeless word but a command issued with the full weight of God’s eternal power? How does that change your awareness when you utter God's names or read His speech?
?
The objection assumes God's speech is like yours. But your words often fail to create even a change of heart. In what area of your life have you relied on your own speech or will to produce a result that only God's power can bring about? Will you turn to Him today and acknowledge that only His speech accompanied by eternal power can truly transform?
?
If God’s word 'Be' brought the heavens and earth into existence, what does that tell you about the seriousness with which you should listen when He speaks to you through the Quran? Is there a command of His that you have treated as if it were just a human suggestion rather than a creative decree?

The Objection and Response on God's Speech Being Like Ours
The author presents an objection and its response against the claim that God's speech is like human speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's speech is like human speech and the subtle response that distinguishes them based on how speech relates to its substrate.

Core Idea
God's speech is not like ours because it does not qualify His essence as ours qualifies us.

This passage presents a critical objection from those who claim that God's speech is of the same kind as ours, leading to absurd consequences. The author counters by showing that even if both are temporal, human speech qualifies its speaker's essence whereas God's speech only has a mere attribution. This distinction preserves divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents the opponents' claim that God's speech and will are like ours, so our speech should create the world.
Notice the mention of 'كن' (Be) as a kaf and nun.
2
Examine the counter-argument
The text then presents a possible defense: that God's speech is produced by His eternal power and intended for creation, whereas ours is not.
3
Analyze the response
Read the author's rebuttal carefully. He argues that the attribution to power does not change the nature of speech as a temporal event with letters and sounds.
Focus on the point about power only affecting existence, not giving special properties.
4
Understand the key distinction
The author concludes that human speech qualifies its substrate (the speaker), but God's speech does not qualify His essence—only a mere attribution. This is the decisive difference.
Consider why this distinction preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما طرق الإلزام عليهم فمنها أن قالوا: قول الله سبحانه وتعالى وإرادته من جنس قولنا وإرادتنا، ثم لقوله وإرادته مفعولات مثل العالم بما فيه من السموات والأرض فيلزم أن يحصل بإرادتنا وقولنا مثل ذلك، فإن قوله كن كاف ونون من جنس أقوالنا من غير فرق. فإن قيل: إنما حصل قوله بمباشرة قدرته وإرادته ومشيئته القديمة، وقولنا لم يحصل بهما، وقوله في ذاته قول له لا لغيره، وقد قصد به التكوين لا بغيره. قيل له: إن كان هذا فرقًا فلم يكن قوله إذا من جنس أقوالنا، بل الحق أن القول إذا حدث بعد أن لم يكن فهو كقولنا الحادث بعد أن لم يكن، ولم يكن لإضافته إلى القدرة أثر بعد الحدوث، فإنه إنما أثر في حال الانفصال عن القدرة لا في حال تعلق القدرة به، وإنما أثرت القدرة في حصول ذاته فقط لا في شيء آخر يحدث به، وعن هذا لو أحدث قولا لنفسه في شجرة وقصد به التكوين لم يحصل به شيء، فبطل قولهم: إنما قصد به الأحداث، وبطل ما اعتذروا به، وحصل أن قوله لا ينبغي أن يوجد كقولنا، أو قولنا يوجد كقوله؛ إذ لا فرق بين قول وقول في الحدوث والحروف والأصوات والاحتياج إلى المحل، بل قولنا أوكد فإنه إذا قام بمحل اتصف المحل به وتحققت له النسبة إلى المحل وعندكم قول الله سبحانه قائم به غير أن تتصف به الذات ولم تتحقق له نسبة إلى الذات إلا مجرد الإضافة، وقد انقطع حكمه عن القدرة القديمة، فإنه إنما يوجد بعد أن يحدث لا حال أنه يحدث.
As for the ways of pressing them, among them is that they said: The speech and will of God Most High are of the same kind as our speech and will. Then His speech and will have effects like the world including the heavens and the earth, so it would be necessary for something like that to be produced by our will and speech. For His word 'Be' is a kaf and a nun, of the same kind as our words without any difference. If it is said: His word is produced by the direct application of His power, will, and eternal decree, whereas our words are not produced by them, and His word is in His essence a word for Him and not for others, and it is intended for bringing into being (takwin) not for other purposes. It is said to him: If this is a distinction, then His word is not of the same kind as our words. Rather, the truth is that when speech occurs after not having been, it is like our speech that occurs after not having been. Its attribution to power has no effect after occurrence, for it only has effect in the state of separation from power, not in the state of power's connection to it. Power only affects the coming into existence of its essence alone, not something else that occurs with it. Because of this, if He were to produce a word for Himself in a tree and intend by it to bring into being, nothing would come into being by it. Thus their statement that it intends to bring about events is invalidated, and their excuse is invalidated. The conclusion is that His word should not exist like our word, or our word exists like His word; for there is no difference between one word and another in terms of occurrence, letters, sounds, and need for a substrate. In fact, our word is more emphatic, for when it subsists in a substrate, the substrate is qualified by it and the relation to the substrate is realized for it. According to you, the word of God Most High subsists in Him, but the essence is not qualified by it, and no relation to the essence is realized for it except mere attribution. Its effect has been cut off from the eternal power, for it only exists after it occurs, not at the moment of its occurrence.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its rebuttal regarding the nature of God's speech. The objection claims that God's speech is of the same genus as human speech, so if God's speech creates the world, then human speech should also have creative power. The rebuttal counters that God's speech is produced by His eternal power and will, while ours is not. However, the answer further argues that even if attributed to power, that does not change the essential nature of speech as a temporal event with letters and sounds. The power only brings the speech into existence, not giving it any special property. In fact, human speech has a stronger connection to its substrate (the speaker) because when a human speaks, the substrate becomes qualified by that speech. For God, His speech subsists in His essence but does not qualify it, and no relation to the essence is realized beyond a mere attribution. The answer concludes that the objection fails because the claimed distinction does not make God's speech different in kind.

Correction: No, the author argues that even if both are temporal, human speech qualifies its substrate whereas God's speech does not, preserving divine transcendence.

God's speech is not like human speech because human speech qualifies its speaker, whereas God's speech does not qualify His essence.

The Dilemma of Temporal Letters in Divine Speech
The author presents an objection about the impossibility of the two letters in 'كن' coexisting or being sequential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed against the view that divine speech consists of temporal letters.

Core Idea
The two letters of 'Be' cannot be sequential or simultaneous without contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents an objection that challenges the coherence of God's command 'Be' if it is composed of temporal letters. The dilemma forces us to examine the nature of divine speech and its relationship to time. We will analyze the two horns of the dilemma and see why each leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it presents a dilemma: the two letters must either be sequential or simultaneous.
The passage is incomplete, but the dilemma is clear.
2
Identify the two horns
Identify the two possibilities: precedence (one letter before the other) or coexistence. Then note the sub-possibilities for precedence.
3
Analyze the consequences
For precedence, if the first letter remains, the second cannot occur; if it ceases, it undergoes non-existence which is impossible for divine speech. For coexistence, one letter would have to produce the other, which is also impossible.
This shows the objection's strength.
4
Reflect on what this means
Consider why this objection is significant for the doctrine of divine attributes, especially for those who hold that God's speech is composed of temporal sounds.
The answer to this objection is not in our passage, but the dilemma itself is worth understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات أن قوله: كن لا يخلو إما أن يسبق أحد الحرفين على الثاني، أو يتلازمان في الوجود إما في حال الوجود أو حال البقا، فإن سبق أحدهما وتلاه الثاني، فإما أن يبقى الأول أو لم يبق، فإن بقي مقولا مسموعاً لم يكن كن بل الأول ما لم ينعدم لا يوجد الثاني حتى يكون مترتباً متعاقباً، وإلا فالكاف المسموع امع النون دواما لا يتصور أصلا، وإن لم يبق فقد انعدم، وعندكم ما حل في ذاته تعالى لا يجوز عليه العدم، وإن كانا يتلازمان في الوجود فقد أوجد أحدهما مع
Among the objections is that His word 'Be' cannot avoid either one of the two letters preceding the other, or they coexist in existence either in the state of coming into existence or in the state of continuance. If one precedes and the other follows, then either the first remains or does not remain. If it remains as a spoken word that is heard, it would not be 'Be' unless the first does not cease to exist so that the second can come into existence in a sequential and consecutive manner. Otherwise, the heard kaf together with the nun continuously cannot be conceived at all. And if it does not remain, then it has ceased to exist, and according to you, what subsists in His essence, may He be exalted, cannot undergo non-existence. And if they coexist in existence, then one of them has produced the other with...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents an objection regarding the divine command 'كن' (Be!). It poses a dilemma: the two letters (kaf and nun) must either be sequential or simultaneous. If sequential, then the first letter must either remain or cease. If it remains, the second cannot occur, so the word 'كن' is not formed. If it ceases, then the divine speech would undergo non-existence, which is impossible for what subsists in God's essence. If they are simultaneous, then one letter would have to produce the other, leading to absurdity. The objection aims to show that the divine speech cannot be composed of temporal letters. The argument is incomplete in the given pages, but the dilemma is clear.

Why God's Speech Is Not Like Human Speech
Refutes the objection that God's speech is like human speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key differences between divine and human speech according to this refutation, particularly regarding power and substrate.

Core Idea
God's speech does not describe His essence; human speech does.

The objection raised is that if God's speech is temporal like human speech, then they are identical. The author refutes this by showing that human speech inheres in a substrate and describes it, while God's speech only has a mere attribution to Him. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a response, a thought experiment, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that if there is a distinction, then God's speech would not be like ours. The author begins by responding directly to that.
The phrase 'قيل له' signals the objection.
3
Compare the roles of power and substrate
The author argues that power only affects the initial existence of speech, not after. Human speech then inheres in a substrate and describes it, whereas God's speech does not.
Consider the tree example: a word spoken in a tree does not create anything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : إن كان هذا فرقًا فلم يكن قوله إذا من جنس أقوالنا، بل الحق أن القول إذا حدث بعد أن لم يكن فهو كقولنا الحادث بعد أن لم يكن، ولم يكن لإضافته إلى القدرة أثر بعد الحدوث، فإنه إنما أثر في حال الانفصال عن القدرة لا في حال تعلق القدرة به، وإنما أثرت القدرة في حصول ذاته فقط لا في شيء آخر يحدث به، وعن هذا لو أحدث قولا لنفسه في شجرة وقصد به التكوين لم يحصل به شيء، فبطل قولهم : إنما قصد به الأحداث، وبطل ما اعتذروا به، وحصل أن قوله لا ينبغي أن يوجد كقولنا ، أو قولنا يوجد كقوله ؛ إذ لا فرق بين قول وقول في الحدوث والحروف والأصوات والاحتياج إلى المحل، بل قولنا أوكد فإنه إذا قام بمحل اتصف المحل به وتحققت له النسبة إلى المحل وعندكم قول الله سبحانه قائم به غير أن تتصف به الذات ولم تتحقق له نسبة إلى الذات إلا مجرد الإضافة، وقد انقطع حكمه عن القدرة القديمة، فإنه إنما يوجد بعد أن يحدث لا حال أنه يحدث.
It was said to him: If this is a distinction, then his speech would not be of the genus of our speeches. Rather, the truth is that speech when it occurs after having not existed is like our speech—the contingent after having not existed. And its attribution to power has no effect after its occurrence, for it only had effect in the state of separation from power, not in the state of power's attachment to it. Power only effected the existence of its essence, not something else that occurs with it. And because of this, if He were to produce a speech for Himself in a tree intending thereby to bring something into existence, nothing would come into being by it. Thus their statement that He intended thereby to originate events is invalid, and their excuse is invalid. It is established that His speech should not be like our speech, nor our speech like His; for there is no difference between one speech and another in temporality, letters, sounds, and need for a substrate. Rather, our speech is more certain, for when it inheres in a substrate, the substrate is described by it and its relation to the substrate is realized. While with you, God's speech subsists in Him, but the essence is not described by it, nor does it have a relation to the essence except mere attribution. And its connection to the eternal power is severed, for it only exists after it occurs, not while it is occurring.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the nature of God's speech. The author addresses an opponent who argued that if God's speech is like human speech, then there is no real distinction. The author responds by explaining that human speech is contingent and depends on power only at the moment of creation, after which it becomes independent. In contrast, God's speech, though also contingent, does not inhere in a substrate to describe it; it merely has an attribution to God. If power were effective after the speech's existence, then a word spoken in a tree would cause creation, but it does not. Therefore, there is a fundamental difference: human speech qualifies its substrate, while God's speech does not qualify His essence. The argument concludes that the opponent's position is invalid.

Key Terms
قول
قدرة
محل
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since both divine and human speech are temporal and consist of letters/sounds, they are identical in every respect.
Correction: The author shows that the key difference is that human speech inheres in a substrate and describes it, while God's speech does not describe His essence—only a relation of attribution exists.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is God's speech not like human speech?
A) Because God's speech is eternal while human speech is temporal.
B) Because human speech inheres in a substrate and describes it, while God's speech does not describe His essence.
C) Because God's speech is created in a different manner.
D) Because human speech is composed of letters but God's speech is not.

Key Takeaway
The difference between divine and human speech lies not in their temporality but in their relation to power and substrate.

?

?
The author argues that human speech inheres in a substrate and describes it, but divine speech only has attribution. If your words only had attribution to you but did not describe you, would you be less careful with them? What does that tell you about the weight of your own speech?

The First Objection: Sequence of Letters in 'Kun'
Presents the first objection: the dilemma of sequential letters in 'Kun'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against the possibility of sequential letters in God's command 'Kun' and why it leads to a dilemma.

Core Idea
God's speech 'Kun' cannot be a sequential word because that would imply impermanence in the divine essence.

The author presents a powerful objection against those who claim that God's command 'Be' consists of sequential letters. If the letters are sequential, the first must perish for the second to appear—yet what inheres in God cannot perish. If they are simultaneous, then 'Be' is not a sequential word at all. Either way, the notion fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either one letter precedes the other, or they are simultaneous. Find these two branches and see how each leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand why each horn is problematic
If sequential, the first letter must perish for the second to appear—but the opponent holds that what inheres in God cannot perish. If simultaneous, then the word is not truly sequential, contradicting the nature of speech.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This objection shows that God's speech cannot be composed of temporal letters; it must be eternal and simple. Consider how this reinforces the doctrine of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات أن قوله : كن لا يخلو إما أن يسبق أحد الحرفين على الثاني، أو يتلازمان في الوجود إما في حال الوجود أو حال البقاء، فإن سبق أحدهما وتلاه الثاني، فإما أن يبقى الأول أو لم يبق، فإن بقي مقولا مسموعاً لم يكن كن بل الأول ما لم ينعدم لا يوجد الثاني حتى يكون مترتباً متعاقباً، وإلا فالكاف المسموع مع النون دواما لا يتصور أصلا، وإن لم يبق فقد انعدم، وعندكم ما حل في ذاته تعالى لا يجوز عليه العدم، وإن كانا يتلازمان في الوجود فقد أوجد أحدهما مع الثاني، فليس الكاف بالتقديم أولى من النون، وكذلك كل حرف يجب أن يسبق حرفا في القول والتكلم حتى يكون بترتبه وتعاقبه كلامًا مسموعا. ومن الإلزامات إثبات أكوان حادثة مع الكون القديم، وإثبات علوم حادثة مع العلم القديم ؛ كإثبات إرادة حادثة مع المشيئة القديمة، وقد ذكر المتكلمون ذلك في كتبهم.
And from the required conclusions (al-ilzamāt) is that His saying 'Be' (kun) is either such that one of the two letters precedes the other, or they are concomitant in existence—either in the state of existence or in the state of permanence. If one precedes and the other follows, then either the first remains or it does not. If it remains as a spoken thing, then it is not 'Be'; rather, as long as the first has not perished, the second does not come into existence so that they would be ordered and successive. Otherwise, the 'kāf' is heard with the 'nūn' simultaneously—which is not conceivable at all. And if it does not remain, then it has perished, and according to you, what inheres in His essence is not subject to non-existence. And if they are concomitant in existence, then He brought one into existence along with the other, so the 'kāf' is not more entitled to precedence than the 'nūn'. Likewise, every letter must precede another in speech and utterance so that by its order and succession it is audible speech. And from the required conclusions is the positing of temporal accidents along with the eternal accident, and the positing of temporal knowledges along with the eternal knowledge, like the positing of temporal will along with the eternal will; and the theologians have mentioned this in their books.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (al-ilzamāt) against the opponent's view. The first objection concerns the word 'Kun' (Be). If God says 'Kun', it comprises two letters: kāf and nūn. These letters must either be sequential or simultaneous. If sequential, the first must perish for the second to appear—but in the opponent's view, what inheres in God's essence cannot perish. If simultaneous, then both letters exist at once, making 'Kun' impossible as a sequential word. Either way, the notion fails. The second objection is that positing a temporal attribute (e.g., a created accident) alongside an eternal attribute (e.g., eternal knowledge) leads to contradictions, as it would require multiple temporal attributes co-existing with the eternal, which theologians have elaborated.

Key Terms
كن
حرف
ترتب
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech 'Be' is a temporal sequence of letters like human speech.
Correction: If it were sequential, it would require impermanence in God, which is impossible. Thus, divine speech must be eternal and not composed of temporal parts.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma regarding the word 'Kun'?
A) The letters are simultaneous.
B) One letter precedes the other.
C) The word is not Arabic.
D) The word is created.

Key Takeaway
The objection proves that God's speech cannot be composed of sequential letters without implying impermanence in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's command 'Kun' is not a temporal word but an eternal act, how should that transform your understanding of His decrees in your own life? Are you willing to submit to a command that is beyond your full comprehension, trusting in His eternal wisdom?

The Second Objection: Temporal Accidents with Eternal Attributes
Presents the second objection: the impossibility of temporal accidents alongside eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that positing temporal accidents alongside eternal attributes leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Temporal accidents cannot coexist with eternal attributes without implying change in God.

The author presents a second objection: if one posits temporal accidents (like temporal knowledge or will) alongside eternal attributes (like eternal knowledge or will), then we would have both temporal and eternal instantiations of the same attribute, leading to multiplicity and change in the divine essence. This objection builds on the first, further showing the coherence of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection. Understand the examples: temporal accidents with eternal accident, temporal knowledges with eternal knowledge, temporal will with eternal will.
2
Identify the examples given
The author mentions three pairs: temporal accidents (akwān) with eternal accident, temporal knowledges (ʿulūm) with eternal knowledge, temporal will (irāda) with eternal will (mashīʾa).
Note the parallel structure.
3
Understand the logical problem
If temporal attributes exist alongside eternal ones, then the same attribute (e.g., knowledge) would have both eternal and temporal instances, which is contradictory. It also implies change in God from having only eternal attributes to adding temporal ones.
4
Connect to broader kalām debates
This objection is used to defend the Ash'ari view that all divine attributes are eternal and not subject to temporal creation. Theologians have elaborated this point in their books, as the author notes.
Reflect on how this reinforces divine immutability.

And from the required conclusions is the positing of temporal accidents along with the eternal accident, and the positing of temporal knowledges along with the eternal knowledge, like the positing of temporal will along with the eternal will; and the theologians have mentioned this in their books.

Refuting the Karramiyya on God's Hearing and Seeing
Refutation of Karramiyya's argument that new hearing requires new attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school refutes the Karramiyya claim that God's hearing of new sounds implies new attributes of hearing and seeing.

Core Idea
God’s attributes of hearing and seeing are eternal; only the objects change.

The Karramiyya argued that if God hears something new, He must acquire a new attribute of hearing. This lesson examines the Ash'ari response: the attribute is eternal, and the act of hearing a new sound does not entail a new attribute. The refutation uses a dilemma and a critique of the opponents' logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, a dilemma, and the true answer.
The passage uses a rhetorical question to trap the opponent.
2
Identify the Karramiyya's claim and the dilemma
The Karramiyya claim that new sounds require new attributes. The author poses a dilemma: either God changes (becomes hearing after not hearing) or He does not. Both lead to contradictions for the opponent.
The dilemma forces the opponent to admit either change (which is denied) or inconsistency.
3
Understand the true Ash'ari answer
The true answer is that the negation 'was not hearing' is not conceded. God is eternally hearing and seeing. The attribute is eternal, so no new attribute arises when a new sound is heard.
This preserves God's immutability while allowing new objects of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبهة الكرامية أن قالوا : سمع الباري سبحانه ما لم يسمع قبله، ورأى ما لم ير قبله فيجب أن يحدث له تسمع وتبصر. فقيل لهم : أتقولون لم يكن الباري سبحانه سامعاً للأصوات فصار سامع لها، ولا رائيًا للمدركات فصار رائيا لها ، ولم يكن قايلا للأوامر والنواهي فصار قايلا ، ولم يرد وجود العالم في الوقت الذي سبق وجوده فصار مريدا في الوقت الذي أوجده، فيدل كل ذلك على تجدد وصف له ، وإن لم تقولوا : إنه صار سميعاً بصيرا مريدا فقد تناقض قولكم : إنه سمع ما لم يسمع ، ورأى ما لم ير، وأراد ما لم يرد. ثم الجواب الحقيقي قلنا : قد جمعتم بين كلمتي نفي وإثبات في قولكم : لم يكن كذا فصار كذا، فلا النفي على أصلنا مسلم مذهب لكم، فأما النفي على أصلنا فغير مسلم أنه لم يكن سميعاً بصيراً بل هو بصير
As for the specious argument of the Karramiyya, they said: God heard what He had not heard before, and saw what He had not seen before, so there must arise for Him a hearing and a seeing. So it was said to them: Do you say that God was not hearing sounds and then became hearing of them, and was not seeing perceptibles and then became seeing of them, and was not speaking commands and prohibitions and then became speaking of them, and did not will the existence of the world at the time before its existence and then became willing at the time He brought it into existence? All that indicates new attributes for Him. But if you do not say that He became hearing, seeing, willing, then you contradict your statement that He heard what He had not heard, saw what He had not seen, and willed what He had not willed. Then the true answer: We said: You have combined a word of negation and affirmation in your statement: 'He was not such and then He became such'. As for negation, according to our school it is not conceded; according to your school it is conceded. But as for negation according to our school, it is not conceded that He was not hearing, seeing; rather, He is hearing, seeing.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage refutes the Karramiyya's argument that God's hearing new sounds implies new attributes of hearing and seeing. The opponents claim that if God hears something new, it requires a new auditory attribute (tasmī‘) and visual attribute (tabṣīr). The author first poses a dilemma: either you admit that God becomes hearing after not hearing (implying change), or you maintain He is eternally hearing and seeing. If you admit change, then you allow temporal attributes in God. If you deny change, you contradict yourself because you said He heard something new. The true answer is that the Karramiyya combine negation ('was not') and affirmation ('became') in their statement. According to the Ash'ari school, the negation is not conceded; God was always hearing and seeing, so there is no temporal becoming. The attribute is eternal, even though the act of hearing a particular sound is temporal. Thus, no new attribute is required.

Correction: The attribute of hearing is eternal and does not change; only the object heard is new.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing and seeing are eternal; temporal objects do not affect the attributes.

beginner
5 min
The True Answer: Change is in Objects, Not in God
The author gives the true answer: God is eternally seeing and hearing; change pertains to objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the objection that God's attributes change—they do not; only the objects of His perception change.

Core Idea
God's seeing and hearing are eternal; change is only in what He sees and hears.

The Karramiyya argued that statements like 'He was not seeing and then became seeing' imply change in God. In this passage, the author gives the definitive Ash'ari answer: God is eternally seeing and hearing, just as He is eternally knowing and powerful. The renewal pertains only to the objects of perception, not to God's attributes. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get an overall sense of the argument without analyzing yet.
Notice that the author directly addresses the opponents' phrase 'was not... then became'.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and the refutation
The opponents combine a negation and affirmation. On the author's school, the negation is not accepted because God is eternally seeing and hearing.
The author says: 'بل هو بصير سميع أبدًا وأزلا' (rather He is seeing, hearing eternally and forever).
3
Understand the key concept of 'tajaddud irja'' (renewal pertains to the object)
The renewal (tajaddud) is in the objects perceived, not in God. The author compares it to knowledge and power: renewal is in the objects known and objects of power.
4
Examine the analogy of the Throne and direction
The author gives an analogy: statements like 'He was not established on the Throne' are understood by agreement (bi-l-ittifāq) as not implying a new attribute in God. The change is only in the relation.
This analogy reinforces that no attribute occurs in God that was not.
5
Grasp the conditionality of attachment
The attachment (ta'alluq) of hearing and sight to objects is conditioned by the existence of those objects. The absence of that condition does not negate the attribute itself.
The last sentence emphasizes that the attribute remains even if its object is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجواب الحقيقي قلنا: قد جمعتم بين كلمتي نفي وإثبات في قولكم: لم يكن كذا فصار كذا، فلا النفي على أصلنا مسلم مذهب لكم، فأما النفي على أصلنا فغير مسلم أنه لم يكن سميعاً بصيراً بل هو بصير سميع أبدًا وأزلا كما هو عليم قدير أبدا وأزلا، وإنما التجدد يرجع إلى المدرك، كما يرجع التجدد إلى المعلوم والمقدور فهو كقولكم: لم يكن مستويًا على العرش فصار مستويًا، ولم يكن بجهة فوق فصار بجهة فوق، وبالاتفاق لم تحدث له صفة لم تكن، بل النفي إنما يرجع إلى المدركات، فنقول: لم تكن المسموعات والمرئيات موجودة فيسمع ويرى فوجدت فتعلق بها السمع والبصر، فكان التعلق مشروطًا بالوجود لا بالمتعلق ورب تعلق شرط فيه الوجود والحياة والعقل والبلوغ ثم عدم الشرط لم يقتض عدم المتعلق.
Then the true answer: we said: You have combined a negative and an affirmative in your statement: 'It was not such-and-such and then became such-and-such.' As for the negative, according to our principle it is not conceded to you as a doctrine. As for the negative according to our principle, it is not conceded that He was not hearing, seeing; rather He is seeing, hearing eternally and forever, just as He is knowing, powerful eternally and forever. Renewal only pertains to the object perceived, just as renewal pertains to the object known and the object of power. It is like your saying: 'He was not established upon the Throne and then became established,' and 'He was not in a direction of above and then became in a direction of above.' By agreement, no attribute occurred for Him that was not. Rather, the negative pertains only to the objects perceived. So we say: The things heard and seen were not existing, so He hears and sees; they came into existence, so hearing and sight attached to them. The attachment is conditioned by existence, not by the thing attached. And there is perhaps an attachment whose condition is existence, life, intellect, and maturity; then the absence of the condition does not necessitate the absence of the thing attached.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides the definitive answer to the Karramiyya objection. The author points out that the opponents' statement combines a negation and an affirmation: 'He was not X, then became X.' On the author's school (the Ash'ari position), the negation is not accepted because God is eternally seeing and hearing, just as He is eternally knowing and powerful. The renewal (tajaddud) is only in the objects (mudrak), not in the divine attributes. The author draws an analogy: if someone says God was not established on the Throne and then became established, or was not in a direction and then became in a direction, the change is only in the relation, not in God's essence or attributes. By agreement (bi-l-ittifāq), no new attribute arises in God. The negation of a past state refers only to the non-existence of the objects. The attachment (ta'alluq) of hearing and sight to objects is conditioned by the objects' existence, not by a change in God. Furthermore, even if a condition is absent (like existence), that does not imply the absence of the attribute itself.

Key Terms
الجواب الحقيقي
نفي وإثبات
التجدد
المدرك
التعلق
مشروط بالوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an object does not exist, God's attribute of seeing/hearing is absent or changes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the attachment of hearing/sight to objects is conditioned by their existence, but the attribute itself remains eternally present in God.

Checkpoint
According to the true answer, where does the renewal (tajaddud) occur?
A) In God's essence.
B) In God's attributes.
C) In the objects perceived.
D) In both God and objects.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of seeing and hearing are eternal and unchanging; only the objects of perception change.

?

Impossibility of Temporal Attributes in God
Refutation of temporal attributes in God, using change as evidence of contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal attributes cannot be attributed to God, based on the argument that they imply change (which indicates contingency) and that eternal attributes related to temporal objects lead to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
God cannot acquire new attributes because change is a sign of contingency.

In this passage, the author refutes the Karramiyya school's claim that God can be described with temporal attributes. He argues that acquiring a new attribute implies change, and change is evidence of contingency. Furthermore, he shows that the concept of an eternal attribute related to a perishable object is logically impossible. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument from the opponents' own principle to the conclusion of impossibility.
The author begins by citing the opponents' own view that God is not subject to temporal events.
2
Identify the key argument from change
Find the line where the author says change is evidence of contingency. Understand that if God acquires an attribute He did not have before, that is change, and change implies createdness.
The phrase 'التغيير دليل الحدوث' is central.
3
Examine the distinction between attribute types
The author contrasts two kinds of attributes: those whose attachment or object can be delayed (like knowledge, power, will) and those that are lasting but whose attachment precedes (impossible). Understand why the latter is impossible using the example of will and the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإثبات فعلى أصلكم لم يوصف الباري سبحانه بالحوادث في ذاته، فكيف يصح على مذهبكم أنه صار سميعاً بصيراً، وإن التزمتم الاتصاف فقد سلمتم لنا المسألة، فإن الاتصاف بصفة لم يكن قبل صريح التغيير والتغيير دليل الحدوث، وهذه الحوادث باقيات على مذهبكم والمتعلقات فانية، وإثبات صفة الصفات من المتعلقة يتأخر تعلقها أو متعلقها غير مستحيل؛ كالعلم والقدرة والمشيئة، أما إثبات صفة باقية يتقدم تعلقها أو متعلقها فهو مستحيل، فإنه إذا أراد وجود العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته ووجد العالم وبقي وفني فهو مريد أبد الدهر وقايل: كن أبد الدهر لشيء قد تكون وفني، وسامع لصوت وحرف قد مضى وانقضى، ويصير لجسم ولون قد انقرض في غاية الاستحالة.
As for the affirmation, according to your school, the Creator, glorified, is not described by temporal events in His essence, so how is it correct according to your madhhab that He became hearing, seeing? And if you commit to the description, then you have conceded the issue to us. For the description with an attribute that was not before is pure change, and change is evidence of contingency. And these temporal events are lasting according to your madhhab, while the objects are perishing. And the affirmation of an attribute of attributes that is related, its attachment or its object being delayed is not impossible, like knowledge, power, and will. But the affirmation of a lasting attribute whose attachment or its object precedes is impossible. For if He willed the existence of the world with a temporal will in His essence, and the world existed and persisted and perished, then He is willing for all time and saying 'Be' for all time for something that has become and perished, and hearing a sound and a letter that have passed and ended, and becoming for a body and a color that have perished — this is in the utmost impossibility.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of affirming temporal attributes (hawādith) in God. The author argues that according to the opponents' own school (the Karramiyya's principles), God is not described by temporal events in His essence. So how can they claim He became hearing, seeing? If they admit such descriptions, they concede the point: describing God with an attribute that was not there before implies change (taghyīr), and change is a sign of contingency (ḥudūth). Furthermore, they hold that temporal events (these attributes) are lasting (bāqiyāt) while the objects of those attributes are perishable. The author then distinguishes between two types of attributes: those whose attachment (ta'alluq) or object can be delayed (like knowledge, power, will) — these are possible — and those that are lasting but have their attachment or object precede — this is impossible. The example given: if God wills the world's existence with a temporal will in His essence, and the world exists, persists, and perishes, then God would be eternally willing something that has already ceased to exist, which is absurd. Similarly, being eternally hearing a sound that has passed, or becoming for a body that has perished, is impossible. Thus, temporal attributes in God lead to logical contradictions.

Key Terms
الإثبات
الحوادث
التغيير
الحدوث
الصفة
التعلق
باقيات
فانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'صفة الصفات من المتعلقة' refers to an attribute of attributes, implying a meta-attribute.
Correction: The phrase actually refers to attributes that are relational (like will), not an attribute of attributes. It means 'an attribute of the [type of] attributes that are related'.

Checkpoint
Why is a lasting attribute with a preceding attachment considered impossible?
A) Because God cannot have lasting attributes.
B) Because it would mean God is eternally related to something that no longer exists, which is absurd.
C) Because all attributes must be temporal.
D) Because the attachment must be simultaneous with the attribute.

Key Takeaway
Temporal attributes in God are impossible because they imply change (which indicates contingency), and eternal attributes with temporal relations lead to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined God as 'becoming' angry with you after a sin, or 'becoming' pleased after your repentance? How does this lesson's insistence on divine immutability challenge that mental image? Will you now consciously correct that thought when it arises?
?
The lesson says change is a sign of contingency. If you truly internalize that God does not change, what does that imply about His love and mercy toward you? Are they conditional on your actions or eternally present? How might this transform your relationship with Him today?
?
The absurdity of an eternal will that wills something that has perished is used as a proof. But consider: your own repentance is something that happens in time. Does God's eternal forgiveness mean He forgave you before you sinned? Or does His forgiveness respond to your repentance? Wrestle with this using the distinction between the attribute (eternal) and its attachment (temporal). What comfort or challenge does that bring to your heart?

God's Hearing and Sight Are Eternal
Explains that God's hearing and seeing are eternal, change pertains to objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attributes of hearing and sight are eternal and unchanging, and that any apparent change refers to the objects perceived, not to God's essence.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight never change—only the objects do.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's Nihayat al-Aqdam tackles a subtle point: if God hears new things, doesn't that mean His attribute changes? The author explains why this is not so by distinguishing between the eternal attribute and its temporal attachments. Read on to see how the analogy of God's 'establishment' on the Throne clarifies the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses analogies from other attributes like knowledge and power.
2
Identify the key analogy
The author compares hearing and sight to God's 'establishment' on the Throne. Ask yourself: what point is he making with this analogy?
The analogy shows that change is attributed to objects, not to God.
3
Understand the condition of attachment
The passage says attachment (ta'alliq) is conditioned by existence. Reflect on what this means for God's eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سميع أبدًا وأزلا كما هو عليم قدير أبدا وأزلا ، وإنما التجدد يرجع إلى المدرك، كما يرجع التجدد إلى المعلوم والمقدور فهو كقولكم : لم يكن مستويًا على العرش فصار مستويًا، ولم يكن بجهة فوق فصار بجهة فوق، وبالاتفاق لم تحدث له صفة لم تكن، بل النفي إنما يرجع إلى المدركات، فنقول : لم تكن المسموعات والمرئيات موجودة فيسمع ويرى فوجدت فتعلق بها السمع والبصر، فكان التعلق مشروطًا بالوجود لا بالمتعلق ورب تعلق شرط فيه الوجود والحياة والعقل والبلوغ ثم عدم الشرط لم يقتض عدم المتعلق.
Hearing eternally, just as He is Knowing and Powerful eternally. Renewal only pertains to the object of perception, just as renewal pertains to the known and the willed. It is like your statement: He was not established on the Throne and then became established, He was not in a direction of above and then became in a direction of above. By consensus, no attribute that was not emerges in Him. Rather, negation only pertains to the objects of perception. So we say: The heard and seen things were not existing, so He hears and sees; they came to exist, so hearing and sight became attached to them. The attachment was conditioned by existence, not by the object of attachment. And sometimes attachment is conditioned by existence, life, intellect, and maturity; then the absence of the condition does not necessitate the absence of the object of attachment.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of God's attributes of hearing and sight. It begins by stating that God is hearing and seeing eternally, just as He is knowing and powerful eternally. The author clarifies that any renewal or change only affects the objects of perception, not the attributes themselves. He uses the analogy of God's establishment on the Throne and direction: God was not previously established then became established; rather, the negation of such states is applied to the objects, not to God's essence. He then explains that when previously non-existent things come into being, God's hearing and sight attach to them. This attachment is conditioned by the existence of the objects, not by anything in God. He adds that some attachments depend on conditions like existence, life, intellect, and maturity, and the absence of such conditions does not imply the absence of the object altogether.

Key Terms
سميع (hearing)
بصير (seeing)
التجدد (renewal)
المدرك (object of perception)
التعلق (attachment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes change when He perceives new things.
Correction: The passage clarifies that change is only in the objects, not in God's attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for God's hearing and sight to attach to things?
A) The existence of the objects
B) The change in God's essence
C) The passage of time
D) The object's futility

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are eternal and unchanging; any change is only in the objects perceived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing is eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for the whispers of your heart that you think are hidden? Does your awareness of being eternally heard change how you speak to Him?
?
The passage says change only pertains to the objects, not to God's attributes. Are there areas in your life where you treat God as if He were changeable—reacting to your actions—rather than recognizing His eternal constancy? How does this affect your reliance on Him?
?
You claim to believe God hears all, yet do you live as if He is actually listening? When was the last time your prayer was shaped by the certainty that His hearing is eternally present, not a result of your words coming into existence?

Change in God Implies Temporality
Refutes God's acquisition of attributes via change argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why change in God's attributes necessarily implies temporality and why that is impossible for the Eternal.

Core Idea
Change in God is a sign of temporality, which is impossible for the Eternal.

In this passage, the author presents a concise refutation of the claim that God acquires new attributes like hearing and seeing. He argues that if God becomes hearing and seeing, that means He changed, and change is direct evidence of temporal existence. Since God is eternal, this cannot be.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the rhetorical question at the start.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify the key claim: describing God with an attribute that was not there before is explicit change, and change proves temporality.
The author uses 'taghyir' (change) and 'huduth' (temporality).
3
Understand the concession
The author says that if the opponent accepts that God becomes hearing/seeing, they have conceded the issue because change implies temporality, which contradicts God's eternity.
This is a logical trap: either deny change (and thus concede the point) or accept temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإثبات فعلى أصلكم لم يوصف الباري سبحانه بالحوادث في ذاته، فكيف يصح على مذهبكم أنه صار سميعاً بصيراً، وإن التزمتم الاتصاف فقد سلمتم لنا المسألة، فإن الاتصاف بصفة لم يكن قبل صريح التغيير والتغيير دليل الحدوث، وهذه الحوادث باقيات على مذهبكم والمتعلقات فانية، وإثبات صفة الصفات من المتعلقة يتأخر تعلقها أو متعلقها غير مستحيل ؛ كالعلم والقدرة والمشيئة، أما إثبات صفة باقية يتقدم تعلقها أو متعلقها فهو مستحيل، فإنه إذا أراد وجود العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته ووجد العالم وبقي وفني فهو مريد أبد الدهر وقايل : كن أبد الدهر لشيء قد تكون وفني، وسامع لصوت وحرف قد مضى وانقضى، ويصير لجسم ولون قد انقرض في غاية الاستحالة.
As for affirmation, according to your principle, the Exalted God is not described as having accidents in His essence. So how can it be correct in your school that He becomes hearing and seeing? If you accept the description, then you have conceded the issue to us. For describing with an attribute that did not exist before is explicit change, and change is evidence of temporality. And these accidents are eternal in your school while the objects of attachment are annihilated. To affirm an attribute of attributes that attaches late its attachment or its object is not impossible, like knowledge, power, and will. As for affirming an eternal attribute whose attachment or object preceded, that is impossible. For if He willed the existence of the world with a temporal will in His essence, and the world exists, remains, and then perishes, He is willing for eternity and saying 'Be' for eternity for something that once was and perished, and hearing a sound and letter that has passed and elapsed, and becoming a body and color that has vanished, that is utmost impossibility.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation of the claim that God becomes hearing and seeing. The author starts by appealing to the opponent's own principle: God is not described as having accidents (hawadith) in His essence. He asks how, on their own view, it can be that God becomes hearing and seeing. If they accept such a description, they concede the issue because describing God with an attribute that was not there before is explicit change (taghyir), and change indicates temporality (huduth). The author then points out an inconsistency: the opponent says accidents are eternal (baqiyat) while the objects of attachment are transient (faniya). He distinguishes between two types of attributes: those whose attachment can be delayed (like knowledge, power, will) and eternal attributes whose attachment cannot precede their object. The latter is impossible, as it would lead to absurdities: God willing eternally something that perished, commanding something that ceased, hearing sounds that passed, and becoming a body or color that vanished. This demonstrates the impossibility of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
الإثبات
الحوادث
التغيير
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God can change His attributes in response to creation (e.g., becoming 'seeing' when He observes).
Correction: This passage shows that change in God is direct evidence of temporal origin; an eternal being cannot undergo change.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that accepting God becomes hearing and seeing is a concession?
A) Because change is evidence of temporality
B) Because the opponent does not believe in change
C) Because hearing and seeing are not real attributes
D) Because the opponent's school allows accidents in God

Key Takeaway
Change in God is impossible because it directly indicates temporality, contradicting His eternal nature.

Eternal Attributes and Temporal Objects
Distinguishes attribute types and derives absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes whose attachment can be delayed and those that cannot, and the absurdities that arise from affirming eternal attributes with temporal objects.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute cannot have a temporal relation without absurdity.

The author continues his refutation by pointing out an inconsistency in the opponent's view: they claim accidents are eternal while their objects are transient. He distinguishes two types of attributes to show that a certain kind is impossible, leading to ridiculous consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the two cases the author presents.
Look for the key terms 'bāqiyāt' (eternal) and 'fāniya' (transient).
2
Identify the distinction
The author distinguishes between attributes whose attachment can be delayed (like knowledge, power, will) and those that require a preceding object (impossible for an eternal attribute).
The first type is possible, the second is impossible.
3
Consider the absurdities
The author gives examples: God willing eternally something that perished, commanding something that ceased, hearing sounds that passed. These show the impossibility of the opponent's view.
These absurdities demonstrate logical contradiction.
