The Absurdity of Letters and Sounds in God's Speech
Shows that affirming letters and sounds in God's speech leads to absurdity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming letters and sounds in God's speech leads to the absurd conclusion that God is a body with motion and collisions.

Core Idea
Affirming letters in God's speech forces either unintelligibility or corporealism.

This passage presents a powerful dilemma against those who claim that God's speech consists of letters and sounds. The author forces a choice: either letters are abstract (unintelligible) or they are sounds (which require bodies and motion). The chain of reasoning shows that the second option leads to an impossible conclusion about God's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the passage carefully. Note the two choices: letters without sounds vs. letters as sounds. Understand why the first is rejected as unintelligible.
The author defines a letter as the articulation of sound, making abstract letters meaningless.
2
Trace the causal chain
Follow the logical sequence: motion leads to collision, collision to sound, sound to letter, letter to word, word to utterance, utterance to story. Notice how each step depends on the previous.
This chain is necessary if letters are to be real sounds.
3
Draw the theological conclusion
See how the chain forces the conclusion that God would be a moving body with collisions among parts. Reflect on why this is absurd for the divine nature.
The author declares God exalted above such corporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : هذه الحروف التي أثبتموها هي حروف مجردة من غير أصوات، أم هي حروف هي تقطيع أصوات، فإن أثبتموها حروفًا من غير أصوات فهي غير مسموعة ولا هي معقولة، فإن حقيقة الحرف تقطيع صوت، والصوت بالنسبة إلى الحروف كالجنس بالنسبة إلى النوع والعرض بالنسبة إلى اللون، فإن أثبتوا حروفا هي أصوات مقطعة فلا بد لها من اصطكاكات أجرام حتى يتحقق الصوت، فإن اصطكاك الأجرام كالنوع بالنسبة إلى الحركة، والصوت كالنوع بالنسبة إلى الاصطكاك، ولا بد من حركة حتى يتحقق اصطكاك أو تفكيك، ولا بد من [اصطكاك؟] حتى يتحقق الصوت، ولا بد من صوت حتى يتحقق حرف، ولا بد من حرف حتى تتحقق كلمة، ولا بد من كلمة حتى يتحقق قول تام، ولا بد من قول حتى تتحقق قصة وحكاية فيلزم على ذلك أن يكون الباري جسماً متحركاً ذا اصطكاك في أجزاء جسمية، ويتعالى الرب سبحانه عن ذلك علوا كبيرًا.
We say: These letters that you affirm — are they abstract letters without sounds, or are they letters that are articulated sounds? If you affirm them as letters without sounds, they are not heard nor are they intelligible. For the reality of a letter is the articulation of sound. Sound in relation to letters is like genus in relation to species, and like accident in relation to color. If they affirm letters that are articulated sounds, then they must have collisions of bodies to realize sound. Collision of bodies is like species in relation to motion, and sound is like species in relation to collision. There must be motion to realize collision or separation, and there must be [collision?] to realize sound, and there must be sound to realize a letter, and there must be a letter to realize a word, and there must be a word to realize a complete utterance, and there must be a complete utterance to realize a story and narrative. Therefore, it follows that the Creator is a moving body with collisions in bodily parts. Exalted is the Lord above that greatly.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God's speech consists of letters and sounds. The author presents a dilemma: either these letters are abstract without sounds, in which case they are not heard nor intelligible since a letter is by definition an articulation of sound, or they are articulated sounds. If they are sounds, then they require collisions of bodies to produce sound, which in turn require motion. The author traces a chain: motion produces collision, collision produces sound, sound produces a letter, letters produce words, words produce complete utterances, and utterances produce stories. This chain necessarily implies that God would be a moving body with collisions among parts—an impossibility. The passage highlights the absurdity of anthropomorphic interpretations of divine speech, affirming God's transcendence.

Key Terms
الحروف
الأصوات
اصطكاك أجرام
الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is composed of letters and sounds like human speech.
Correction: This leads to the absurd conclusion that God is a moving body with collisions, which is impossible for the Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate consequence if God's speech is composed of articulated sounds?
A) God must have collisions and be a body
B) God must be silent
C) God's speech becomes eternal
D) God's speech is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Affirming letters and sounds in God's speech implies that God is a body with motion and collisions, which is impossible and contradictory to divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God speaking to you, what image forms in your mind? Does it involve sounds or words? After reading this passage, can you still hold that image without falling into the absurdities described? What must you adjust in your understanding of divine speech to truly transcend creation?
?
This passage reveals that even seemingly harmless affirmations about God can logically lead to declaring Him a body. In what areas of your faith have you been comfortable with vague or unexamined assumptions about God's attributes? Name one area and commit to studying it with the same rigorous logic as this passage.
?
If someone told you that God spoke to them in words, how would you respond using the reasoning of this lesson? Does this challenge you to be more careful in how you speak about God's actions, and to hold yourself accountable for every attribute you affirm about Him?

The Philosophers’ Denial of Positive Divine Attributes
The author cites the philosophers' denial of essential attributes and their classification of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God can only have relational or negative attributes, not positive essential ones, and why this view sets up a major theological debate.

Core Idea
God's absolute oneness, according to philosophers, permits only relational or negative attributes.

The author begins by presenting the view he intends to refute: that of the 'divine philosophers' who deny that God has any positive essential attributes. Their reasoning hinges on God's absolute simplicity and oneness, which they argue precludes any composition or multiplicity of attributes. This passage lays out their position, which will later be critically examined.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key claim: God is one from every aspect, so no principles combine in Him.
The author is presenting their view, not endorsing it.
2
Understand the argument from oneness
The philosophers argue that the Necessary of Existence has no parts—neither quantitative nor logical (like genus and differentia). Any attribute would imply composition.
They deny even conceptual parts that correspond to attributes.
3
Identify the two allowed categories
They conclude that any description of God must be either relational (like 'Creator') or negative (like 'one' meaning not multiple).
Positive attributes like 'Living' or 'Knowing' are excluded.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this matters: if only relational/negative attributes are allowed, then God's essence is unknowable in any positive sense. The author will later refute this.
This sets the stage for the debate over divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تعطيل الباري سبحانه عن الصفات الذاتية والمعنوية وعن الأسماء والأحكام، فذلك مذهب الإلهيين من الفلاسفة، فإنهم قالوا: واجب الوجود لذاته واحد من كل وجه فلا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادئ تجتمع فيتقدم واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا أجزاء حد؛ سواء كانت كالمادة والصورة، أو كانت على وجه آخر بأن يكون أجزاء لقول الشارح لمعنى اسمه، ويدل كل واحد منهما على شيء هو في الوجود غير الآخر بذاته، وذلك لأن ما هذا صفته فذات كل واحد منه ليس ذات الآخر ولا ذات المجتمع، وإنما تعينه واجب بالذات، فليس يقتضي معنى آخر سوى وجوده، وإن وصف بصفة فليس يقتضي ذلك معنى غير ذاته، واعتبارا ووجها غير وجوده، بل الصفات كلها إما إضافية كقولنا: مبدأ العالم وعلة العقل، كما تقولون خالق ورازق، وأما سلبية كقولنا واحد أي ليس بمتكثر
"As for the negation of the Creator, glory be to Him, from essential and qualitative attributes, and from names and judgments, that is the doctrine of the divine philosophers. For they said: The Necessary of Existence by essence is one from every aspect, so it is not permissible that it should have principles that combine in it, and the Necessary of Existence has no quantitative parts nor parts of definition; whether they are like matter and form, or in another manner such that they are parts for the expression of the explainer for the meaning of His name, and each one of them indicates something that in existence is other than the other by essence. That is because that which has this description, the essence of each one of them is not the essence of the other nor the essence of the combination. Rather, its specification as necessary by essence does not imply another meaning besides its existence. And if it is described by an attribute, that does not imply a meaning other than its essence, nor a consideration and aspect other than its existence. Rather, all attributes are either relational (idafiyya) like our saying: the beginning of the world and the cause of the intellect, just as you say Creator and Provider; or negative (salbiyya) like our saying 'one', meaning not multiple."
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the 'divine philosophers' (الإلهيين من الفلاسفة) who deny that God has essential or qualitative attributes (الصفات الذاتية والمعنوية) and only allow relational or negative attributes. Their reasoning is that God is absolutely simple and one, so He cannot have any composition, whether of physical parts or logical parts (like genus and differentia). Any supposed attribute would either indicate something other than the essence, leading to multiplicity, or would be merely relational (like being the cause of the world) or negative (like being not multiple). The author here presents their view, which he intends to refute, as part of his larger argument against the denial of divine attributes. The passage ends mid-sentence but sets up the refutation.

الصفات الذاتية والمعنوية
الإلهيون
واجب الوجود
إضافية
سلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes of God are either relational (like 'Creator') or negative (like 'One'), and that positive essential attributes are impossible.
Correction: The passage presents this as the philosophers' view, but the author will refute it, showing that positive essential attributes are necessary and do not compromise God's oneness.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers mentioned, what kind of attributes are considered allowable for God?
A) Only essential attributes
B) Only relational and negative attributes
C) Only active attributes
D) All attributes are allowable

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's absolute oneness forces all attributes to be either relational or negative, denying positive essential attributes.

Philosophers' View of Negating Attributes
Describes the philosophers' doctrine of negating attributes and their classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' doctrine of negating positive attributes and their classification of divine attributes into relational, negative, and composite.

Core Idea
Philosophers reduce God's attributes to relational or negative descriptions, never positive additions to essence.

This passage introduces a key challenge to orthodox theology: the philosophical assertion that God is absolutely simple, devoid of any positive attributes. Before addressing the response, al-Shahrastani presents their view in their own terms. You will see how they reinterpret names like 'One' and 'Wise' as either relational or negative, denying any real multiplicity in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic text carefully to absorb its rhythm and terminology. Note the key terms: تعطيل, واجب الوجود, إضافية, سلبية.
The passage is dense; reading aloud helps with comprehension.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that the Necessary Existent is one in every respect and has no parts. This is the basis for negating attributes.
3
Understand the three attribute categories
The passage divides attributes into relational (إضافية), negative (سلبية), and composite. Locate the definitions and examples for each.
Examples: 'Creator' is relational; 'One' is negative (not multiple); 'Wise' is composite.
4
Notice the author's intention
The passage ends with 'فألزم عليهم مناقضات' indicating the author will expose contradictions. This sets up the forthcoming refutation.
This is a preview of the author's own stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما تعطيل الباري سبحانه عن الصفات الذاتية والمعنوية وعن الأسماء والأحكام، فذلك مذهب الإلهيين من الفلاسفة، فإنهم قالوا: واجب الوجود لذاته واحد من كل وجه فلا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادئ تجتمع فيتقدم واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا أجزاء حد؛ سواء كانت كالمادة والصورة، أو كانت على وجه آخر بأن يكون أجزاء لقول الشارح لمعنى اسمه، ويدل كل واحد منهما على شيء هو في الوجود غير الآخر بذاته، وذلك لأن ما هذا صفته فذات كل واحد منه ليس ذات الآخر ولا ذات المجتمع، وإنما تعينه واجب بالذات، فليس يقتضي معنى آخر سوى وجوده، وإن وصف بصفة فليس يقتضي ذلك معنى غير ذاته، واعتبارا ووجها غير وجوده، بل الصفات كلها إما إضافية كقولنا: مبدأ العالم وعلة العقل، كما تقولون خالق ورازق، وأما سلبية كقولنا واحد أي ليس بمتكثر وعقل أي مجرد عن المادة، وقد يكون تركيبه من إضافة وسلب، كقولنا: حكيم مريد وسيأتي شرح ذلك في كتاب الصفات فألزم عليهم مناقضات.
As for divesting the Creator (may He be exalted) of the essential and meaningful attributes and of the names and judgments, that is the doctrine of the metaphysicians among the philosophers, for they said: The Necessary Existence by essence is one in every respect, so it is not permissible that there be in His essence principles that combine so that the Necessary Existence precedes; He has no quantitative parts nor parts of definition; whether they are like matter and form, or whether they are in another way such that they are parts for the statement of the commentator regarding the meaning of His name, and each one of them indicates something that in existence is other than the other by its essence, and that is because what has this description, the essence of each one of them is not the essence of the other nor the essence of the composite, and rather its determination is necessary by essence, so it does not require a meaning other than its existence; and if described by an attribute, that does not require a meaning other than its essence, nor a consideration and aspect other than its existence; rather, the attributes are all either relational, like our saying: the origin of the world and the cause of the intellect, just as you say Creator and Provider, or negative, like our saying: one, meaning not multiple, and intellect, meaning abstracted from matter; and sometimes it is a combination of relation and negation, like our saying: wise, willing; and the explanation of that will come in the Book of Attributes; so I forced upon them contradictions.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage describes the view of the divine philosophers (الإلهيون من الفلاسفة) regarding the negation of attributes. They assert that the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود لذاته) is absolutely one, having no parts—neither quantitative nor conceptual (like matter and form or genus and differentia). He does not require any meaning beyond His existence; any attribute ascribed to Him is either relational (إضافية) like 'Creator' or 'Provider', or negative (سلبية) like 'one' (meaning not multiple) or 'intellect' (meaning not material), or a combination. The author notes that he will explain this further in the Book of Attributes, and then declares that he will refute them by showing contradictions.

Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny any description of God at all, including relational or negative ones.
Correction: The passage shows they accept relational and negative attributes; they only deny positive attributes that add to the essence.

A) Only positive essential attributes.
B) Only relational and negative attributes, or a combination.
C) Only names, not attributes.
D) Only attributes that are both positive and negative.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers reduce all divine attributes to either relational or negative descriptions, denying any positive attribute distinct from God's essence.

Existence is Shared, Distinction Requires Additional Attributes
Refutes philosophers' use of univocal existence with gradation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' claim that 'existence' is applied to God and contingent beings by gradation, showing that any distinction implies additional attributes.

Core Idea
Gradation is itself a differentia, confirming the need for added attributes.

In this passage, the author addresses a key philosophical objection: If existence is univocal for God and creatures, how can we distinguish them without adding attributes? Some philosophers resort to gradation (tashkik). But the author turns this argument against them, showing that gradation itself is a differentiating feature. This lesson unpacks that subtle refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' starting position
They say 'existence' is used univocally for God and contingent beings.
Univocal means the same term with the same meaning.
2
Identify the author's criticism
Necessity (wujub) is an added meaning beyond existence, implying multiplicity.
Existence does not include necessity in its meaning.
3
Consider the philosophers' counter-argument
They appeal to gradation (tashkik) to avoid adding attributes.
Gradation means the term applies more strongly to one.
4
Follow the author's rebuttal
Gradation itself is a differentia, confirming the criticism rather than repelling it.
Being 'first' or 'prior' is an additional distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها أنهم أطلقوا لفظ الوجود على الواجب بذاته، وعلى الواجب لغيره شمولا وعموما على سبيل الاشتراك، ثم خصصوا أحد الوجودين بالوجوب والثاني بالجواز ومن المعلوم أن الوجوب لم يدخل في معنى الوجود حتى يتصور الوجود، فهذا إذا معنى آخر وراء الوجود، والمعنيان إن اتحدا في الخارج من الذهن فقد تمايزا في العقل مثل: العرضية واللونية، وليس يغني عن هذا الإلزام قولهم: إطلاق لفظ الوجوب على الواجب بذاته والجائز لذاته بالتشكيك؛ أي هو في أحدهما أولى وأولى، وفي الثاني لا أولى ولا أول؛ إذ الأولى والأول فصل آخر تميز به أحد الوجودين عن الثاني، وذلك يؤكد الإلزام ولا يدفعه.
Among them is that they applied the word existence to the Necessary by essence and to the necessary through another comprehensively and generally in a univocal way, then they specified one of the two existences with necessity and the second with contingency; and it is known that necessity has not entered into the meaning of existence such that existence is conceptualized, so this is then another meaning beyond existence; and the two meanings, if they are united externally in the mind, they have become distinct in the intellect, like accidental-ness and color-ness; and their saying does not suffice against this criticism: the application of the word necessity to the Necessary by essence and to the contingent by essence in a gradational way; i.e., it is in one of them prior and more prior, and in the second neither prior nor more prior; since the prior and the more prior are another differentia by which one of the two existences is distinguished from the second, and that confirms the criticism and does not repel it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a specific refutation against the philosophers. They use the term 'existence' (الوجود) univocally (على سبيل الاشتراك) for both God (واجب بذاته) and contingent things (واجب لغيره), but then they distinguish them by saying one is necessary (الوجوب) and the other is contingent (الجواز). The author argues that necessity is a meaning added to existence, because existence does not include necessity within its meaning. So this is an additional distinction beyond existence. If existence and necessity are the same in external reality but distinct in the mind (like whiteness and color), that still implies multiplicity. The philosophers might respond by saying 'existence' (or 'necessity'? the text says الوجوب) is applied by gradation (التشكيك) — meaning it is more appropriate for God. But the author counters that this gradation (أولى) is itself a differentia that distinguishes the two, thus reaffirming the criticism rather than answering it.

Key Terms
الوجود
الوجوب
الجواز
الاشتراك
التشكيك
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That gradation (tashkik) solves the problem of univocal existence without adding attributes.
Correction: Gradation itself is a differentiating attribute, so it confirms the need for added attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the philosophers' use of gradation (tashkik)?
A) It successfully avoids the problem of added attributes.
B) It is a differentia that confirms the criticism.
C) It is not mentioned in the passage.
D) It shows that existence is purely univocal.

Key Takeaway
Gradation (tashkik) between God and creation is itself a differentiating attribute, confirming that attributes are added to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever feel a subtle pride in 'sharing' it with God? What specific thought can you carry into your next prayer to remind yourself of this?
?
The philosophers tried to protect God's simplicity by using gradation, but the author shows it fails. Are there areas in your life where you try to protect your own comfort by softening distinctions—like between your rights and others'? How might grasping that even a 'grade' implies a real difference change how you treat those distinctions?
?
If gradation itself is an attribute, then your existence is not just 'less intense' than God's—it is utterly dependent. Does your daily life reflect that dependence, or do you live as if you have some independent share of existence? What is one concrete change you can make today to live out that dependence?

Divine Attributes

Refuting Philosophers: Multiple Considerations Entail Attributes
Refutes philosophers by arguing multiple conceptual considerations imply multiple attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun use the philosophers' own conceptual distinctions to argue for the necessity of affirming divine attributes.

Core Idea
If the philosophers allow multiple concepts of God, they cannot deny multiple attributes.

This passage presents a powerful refutation: the philosophers themselves apply many labels to God—origin, cause, intellect, intelligent, intelligible. These considerations are distinct and do not follow from the single notion of Necessary Existence. If the philosophers accept these distinctions, the mutakallimun too have the right to affirm real attributes. The author also dismisses an extreme view that strips God of all names and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the considerations
List the five considerations the philosophers apply to God: مبدأ (origin), علة (cause), عقل (intellect), عاقل (intelligent), معقول (intelligible). Notice how these are not reducible to each other.
The passage argues that none of these considerations follow from the simple notion of Necessary Existence.
3
Understand the logical move
The author argues that if the philosophers are allowed to multiply such conceptual considerations, then the mutakallim is equally justified in affirming real attributes. Denying attributes would be equivalent to denying the considerations.
This is an ad hominem argument: using the philosophers' own methodology against them.
4
Examine the extreme view
Note the mention of a small group of philosophers who claim God is utterly devoid of attributes, names, and judgments, calling Him an 'eternal vessel' (آنية أزلية). The author dismisses this view, implying that it is an ancient opinion not followed by the mutakallimun.
The phrase 'فلسنا قدماء' (we are not ancients) likely refers to not following that old opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات كونه مبدأ وعلة وعاقلا ومعقولا، فإن اعتبار كونه واجب الوجود لذاته لا يفيد اعتبار كونه مبدأ وعلة واعتبار كونه مبدأ وعلة لا يفيد اعتبار كونه عقلا وعاقلا ومعقولا، فإن ساغ لكم الحكم بتكثير الاعتبارات ساغ للمتكلم إثبات الصفات وتعطيله عن الصفات تعطيل عن الاعتبارات، وأما الباري عن الصفات والأسماء والأحكام أزلا وأبدا وما صار إليه صاير إلا شرذمة من الحكماء قالوا: إن المبدع الأول آنية أزلية وهو قبل الإبداع عديم الاسم، فلسنا قدماء.
And among the criticisms is His being an origin, a cause, an intellect, and an intelligible; for the consideration of His being Necessary Existence by essence does not yield the consideration of His being an origin and a cause, and the consideration of His being an origin and a cause does not yield the consideration of His being an intellect, an intelligent, and an intelligible; so if it is permissible for you to judge by multiplying considerations, it is permissible for the mutakallim to affirm attributes; and divesting Him of attributes is a divestment of considerations. As for the Creator being above attributes and names and judgments eternally and forever, none has adopted that except a few of the philosophers who said: The first innovator is a vessel [or: eternal entity] and He has no name before innovation; we are not ancients.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents another refutation. The philosophers apply multiple conceptual distinctions to God: He is a beginning (مبدأ), a cause (علة), an intellect (عقل), intelligent (عاقل), and intelligible (معقول). None of these considerations are entailed by the single notion of Necessary Existence. Therefore, if the philosophers allow these multiple considerations, the mutakallim also has the right to affirm real attributes. Denying attributes is in effect denying these very considerations. The author then notes that only a handful of philosophers (شرذمة من الحكماء) go further and claim that God is utterly devoid of attributes, names, and judgments eternally, saying that the First Innovator is an eternal vessel (آنية أزلية) and had no name before innovation. The author dismisses this as an extreme view, concluding 'We are not ancients' (فلسنا قدماء) — perhaps meaning we do not follow this ancient opinion.

Key Terms
مبدأ
علة
عقل
عاقل
معقول
اعتبارات
آنية أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all conceptual distinctions about God.
Correction: The author only argues that if conceptual distinctions are allowed, they must lead to affirming real attributes; he does not reject distinctions per se.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if the philosophers allow multiple considerations (e.g., being an origin, a cause, an intellect) for God?
A) The mutakallim is justified in affirming real attributes.
B) God must have a real multiplicity.
C) The philosophers must deny all considerations.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun can legitimately affirm divine attributes because the philosophers themselves acknowledge multiple conceptual considerations of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever reduce Him to a mere abstract 'force' or 'existence' because it feels more intellectually comfortable? This passage shows that even philosophers who deny attributes cannot avoid multiple concepts. How does that challenge your own mental image of God? Will you commit today to affirming specific attributes (like mercy, power, justice) in your private thoughts?
?
The author equates a denial of attributes with a denial of conceptual distinctions about God. Are there any attributes of God that you find unsettling or prefer to ignore (e.g., His wrath, His sovereignty over your plans)? What would it cost you to fully embrace them? Take a moment to confess a specific attribute you have resisted and ask God to reshape your understanding.
?
Imagine you are the philosopher described in the passage: you allow yourself to think of God as 'origin', 'cause', 'intellect', etc., but you refuse to call them 'attributes'. This passage exposes that inconsistency. Where in your own life do you hold double standards about God? For example, do you treat Him as powerful when you need a miracle but distant when you sin? Will you bring this inconsistency to God in prayer today?

The Philosophers' Multiple Designations and the Case for Divine Attributes
Al-Shahrastani presents a second imposition: the philosophers' multiple designations imply multiple considerations, supporting attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani uses the philosophers' own designations for God to argue for the validity of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Multiple designations imply multiple considerations, just like attributes.

Al-Shahrastani continues his critique by pointing out an inconsistency in the philosophers' view. They describe God with several designations—such as mabda' and 'illa—that go beyond the simple concept of Necessary Existence. If they allow these, they cannot reject the mutakallimun's attributes, which are similarly conceptual distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: mabda', 'illa, 'āqil, ma'qūl, i'tibārāt.
The argument is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the designations and their implication
List the four designations the philosophers use. Then note that none of these follow from the simple concept of wājib al-wujūd. They are additional considerations.
This is the core of the imposition.
3
Understand the argument's conclusion
If the philosophers allow multiple considerations for themselves, they must allow the mutakallimun to do the same with attributes. Denying attributes would mean denying their own designations.
This highlights a contradiction in their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات كونه مبدأ وعلة وعاقلا ومعقول، فإن اعتبار كونه واجب الوجود لذاته لا يفيد اعتبار كونه مبدأ وعلة واعتبار كونه مبدأ وعلة لا يفيد اعتبار كونه عقلا وعاقلا ومعقولا، فإن ساغ لكم الحكم بتكثير الاعتبارات ساغ للمتكلم إثبات الصفات وتعطيله عن الصفات تعطيل عن الاعتبارات.
And among the impositions is that He is a beginning (mabda'), a cause ('illa), intellect ('āqil) and intelligible (ma'qūl). For considering Him to be Necessary of Existence in Himself does not entail considering Him to be a beginning and a cause, and considering Him to be a beginning and a cause does not entail considering Him to be intellect and intelligible. So if it is permissible for you to judge by multiplying considerations (i'tibārāt), then it is permissible for the mutakallim to affirm attributes; and divesting Him of attributes is a divestment of considerations.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a second imposition. The philosophers use multiple designations for God: mabda', 'illa, 'āqil, ma'qūl. These are not entailed by the simple concept of wājib al-wujūd. Therefore, if they allow themselves to multiply these considerations, they cannot deny the mutakallim the right to affirm attributes, because attributes are also considerations. If they deny attributes, they must also deny their own designations, which is absurd.

Key Terms
مبدأ
علة
عاقل
معقول
اعتبار
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine attributes are separate entities added to God's essence.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani argues that attributes, like the philosophers' designations, are merely conceptual considerations (i'tibārāt) and not additional entities.

Checkpoint
What is the point of the author's second imposition regarding mabda', 'illa, etc.?

B) The philosophers’ multiple designations imply a multiplication of considerations, which is the same as affirming attributes.
C) The philosophers must deny all designations.
D) The philosophers agree with the mutakallimun on attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani shows that the philosophers' multiple designations for God imply multiple considerations, which undermines their rejection of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson forces you to choose: are you consistent in how you treat the philosophers' designations and the attributes of Allah? If you accept that God is 'intellect' and 'intelligible' without compromising His oneness, then why do you hesitate to accept His attributes such as 'Knowledge' and 'Power'? Be honest: is your hesitation based on reason or on a preconceived notion? Resolve to examine your own theological method today.
?
Al-Shahrastani points out that the philosophers themselves multiply considerations. In your own life, do you apply double standards? Are you quick to accept rational arguments from one tradition but reject similar reasoning from another? This lesson demands consistency. Where in your religious thinking have you been inconsistent? Write down one area and commit to reconciling it.
?
The passage ends: 'divesting Him of attributes is a divestment of considerations.' If you deny God's attributes, you are not just negating some add-ons; you are stripping Him of the very considerations that make Him knowable. Do you truly want a God who is so simple that He cannot be described as All-Knowing or All-Powerful? Reflect on whether your intellectual stance actually impoverishes your worship. Will you now embrace the attributes as necessary considerations?

Refutation of Philosophers Denying Divine Names
Al-Shahrastani refutes the handful of philosophers who deny God any names in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Shahrastani considers the philosophers who deny God any names in eternity as the true 'negators' (mu'aṭṭila) and the implications of their view.

Core Idea
Denying God names in eternity is a radical negation of divine attributes.

Al-Shahrastani presents a brief but sharp refutation of a philosophical school that claims God has no names before creation. According to them, names only arise from God's effects. The author labels them the true mu'aṭṭila, showing that denying names and knowledge about God's essence is an extreme form of negation. This lesson unpacks their claims and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'المبدع الأول' and 'المعطلة'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the philosophers' claims
List the specific claims made by the philosophers: that God has no name in eternity, that names are only from effects, that there is no form in His essence (no knowledge).
These are radical denials.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Shahrastani calls them 'the true mu'aṭṭila'. Reflect on why this label is significant and how their position undermines divine attributes.
Contrast with the orthodox view that God has eternal names and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الباري عن الصفات والأسماء والأحكام أزلا وأبدا وما صار إليه صاير إلا شرذمة من الحكماء قالوا إن المبدع الأول آنية أزلية وهو قبل الإبداع عديم الاسم، فلسنا ندرك له اسما في نحو ذاته، وإنما أسماؤه من نحو آثار، وأفاعيله، وأبدع الذي أبدع ولا صورة له عنده في الذات، أي لا علم ولا معلوم، وإنما العلم والمعلوم في المعلول الأول الذي هو العنصر، فهم المعطلة حقا.
As for [the topic of] the Creator being free from attributes, names, and judgments eternally and forever, only a handful of the philosophers have arrived at that. They said: 'The first innovator (al-mubdi' al-awwal) is a pre-eternal vessel, and before innovation he has no name. Thus we do not perceive for Him a name regarding His essence. Rather, His names are derived from His traces and effects. And He innovated that which He innovated, and there is no form with Him in the essence, i.e., no knowledge and no known. Rather, knowledge and the known are in the first effect, which is the element.' They are the true negators (mu'aṭṭila).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage refutes a group of philosophers who deny that God has any names or attributes in eternity. They claim the first principle is a pre-eternal vessel with no name before creation; names come only from effects. They deny God has any form in His essence, implying no knowledge or known. The author declares them the true mu'aṭṭila (those who divest God of attributes) and labels them as a handful of extremists.

Key Terms
الباري
المبدع الأول
الاسم
العنصر
المعطلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only those who deny God's existence are mu'aṭṭila.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani shows that denying God names and knowledge in His essence also constitutes ta'til, making them the true mu'aṭṭila.

Checkpoint

A) They deny God's existence.
B) They deny God has any names or knowledge in His essence.
C) They affirm that God has many names.
D) They agree with the mutakallimun on attributes.

Key Takeaway
Denying God names and knowledge in eternity is a radical form of ta'til, not just denying His existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson shows that denying God's names is a serious error equated with negation. If you ever struggle with a specific attribute of Allah (like His wrath or His planning), how does this lesson compel you to submit to His revealed description rather than rationalize it away? Two Reasons for Avoiding Names and the Correct View
Al-Shahrastani discusses two reasons extremists avoid applying names to God and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two flawed reasons that some groups avoid using names for God, and why the correct approach is to use revealed names without fear of anthropomorphism or negation.

Core Idea
Shared names do not imply shared meanings; revealed names show the right way.

Al-Shahrastani addresses a radical position held by some philosophers and extremists who refuse to apply any name to God, including 'existing'. They give two reasons rooted in fear of anthropomorphism and negation. In this passage, we learn why their reasoning is flawed and how revelation provides the proper methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the author's description of the extremist view and his refutation.
The passage includes a direct quote from those philosophers.
2
Identify the two reasons
The extremists give two distinct reasons for avoiding names. Find each one and understand the logic behind them.
The first reason concerns shared names implying shared meanings; the second concerns opposites.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Shahrastani refutes both reasons by stating that shared names do not necessitate shared meanings, and that the correct names come from revelation.
Revelation itself uses names like 'Alīm, Qadīr without implying deficiency.
4
Connect to broader principles
Reflect on how this refutation protects both from tashbīh (anthropomorphism) and ta'tīl (negation). The middle path is to use revealed names.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل عن بعض الحكماء أنهم قالوا هو هو ولا نقول موجود ولا معدوم ولا عالم ولا جاهل ولا قادر ولا عاجز، ولا مريد ولا كاره ولا متكلم ولا ساكت وكذلك سائر الأسماء والصفات، وينسب هذا المذهب إلى الغالية من الشيعة والباطنية، ولا شك أن من أثبت صانعا لم يكن له أبد من عبارة عنه وذكر اسم له، ثم الاشتراك في الأسماء ليس يوجب اشتراكا في المعاني، فهؤلاء احترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ الوجود عليه، وما أشبهه من الأسماء لأحد أمرين : أحدهما : لاعتقادهم أن الاشتراك في الاسم يوجب اشتراكا في المعنى فيتحقق له مثل ذلك الوجه. والثاني : لاعتقادهم أن كل اسم من الأسامي التي تطلق عليه يقابله اسم آخر تقابل التضاد ؛ مثل : الموجود يقابله المعدوم، والعالم يقابله الجاهل، والقادر يقابله العاجز، فيتحقق له ضد من ذلك الوجه، فاحترزوا عن إطلاق لفظ وجودي لكيلا يقعوا في التمثيل والتعطيل، ونحن نعلم بأن الأسامي المشتركة هي التي تختلف حقائقها من حيث المعاني، فإن أسماء الباري تبارك وتعالى إنما تتلقى من السمع، وقد ورد السمع أنه سبحانه عليم قدير حي قيوم سميع قيوم سميع بصير لطيف خبير
And it is reported from some philosophers that they said: 'He is He; we do not say existing nor non-existing, nor knowing nor ignorant, nor powerful nor powerless, nor willing nor averse, nor speaking nor silent, and likewise the rest of the names and attributes.' And this doctrine is attributed to the extremists among the Shī'a and the Bāṭiniyya. There is no doubt that whoever affirms a Creator must have some expression about Him and mention a name for Him. Then, the sharing of names does not necessitate sharing in meanings. So these people refrained from applying the term existence to Him and what resembles it from names for one of two reasons: first, because they believe that sharing in the name necessitates sharing in meaning, so that a similar aspect would be realized for Him; and second, because they believe that every name from the names applied to Him has another name opposite it in the relation of opposition, such as 'existing' is opposite to 'non-existing', 'knowing' opposite to 'ignorant', 'powerful' opposite to 'powerless', so that an opposite would be realized for Him from that aspect. So they refrained from applying any existential term to Him lest they fall into anthropomorphism (tamthīl) and negation (ta'tīl). And we know that shared names are those whose realities differ in terms of meanings. For the names of the Creator, blessed and exalted, are only received from revelation (sam'), and revelation has come that He, exalted, is Knowing, Powerful, Living, Self-Subsisting, Hearing, Hearing, Seeing, Kind, Aware.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage discusses a group of philosophers and extremists (ghāliyya of Shī'a and Bāṭiniyya) who avoid applying any names to God, including existence. They give two reasons: fear that shared names imply shared meanings (leading to tashbīh), and fear that each name has an opposite, which would imply that God has an opposite (leading to ta'tīl). The author refutes both by stating that shared names do not necessitate shared meanings, and that the correct names of God are taken from revelation. Revelation itself has given many names like 'Alīm, Qadīr, etc., without implying any deficiency or similarity. The author thus affirms the legitimacy of using revealed names while denying any anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
الأسماء
الاشتراك
التضاد
التمثيل
التعطيل
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same name for God and creatures implies similarity in essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that names are shared without sharing meanings; the realities differ.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason these philosophers give for avoiding names for God?
A) They believe names are unnecessary.
B) They fear that each name implies an opposite, leading to a view of God having opposites.
C) They reject revelation entirely.
D) They claim God has no existence.

Key Takeaway
Using names for God that come from revelation is safe and does not imply similarity or negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing', do you truly believe His knowledge is completely unlike anything you know, or does your heart secretly imagine a superhuman version of your own intellect? Will you today consciously purify your understanding of God's attributes by affirming their absolute uniqueness?

This lesson says revelation gives us the names; are you neglecting that gift? Commit to learning and using at least one of His revealed names this week with full confidence that He has taught it to you.
?

Did you inadvertently imagine His mercy as limited like human mercy? Now, knowing that the name 'Ar-Raheem' is shared only in word, not in meaning, how will you train your heart to expect a mercy that is truly incomparable? What practical step will you take to remind yourself of this difference?

The Command to Use the Best Names for Allah
Author cites the verse and criticizes excessive caution in naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic command to call Allah by His best names and the author's critique of excessive caution in avoiding anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Allah commands us to use His best names without fear or affectation.

The author begins by citing the well-known Quranic verse that commands believers to call upon Allah using His most beautiful names. He then criticizes a certain 'cold precaution' and 'strange affectation' that some people adopt, perhaps referring to those who avoid using certain names out of an exaggerated fear of anthropomorphism. This short but weighty statement sets the stage for discussing proper theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic citation
Read the Arabic verse from Surah al-A'raf. Notice that Allah Himself commands us to use His best names and to abandon those who deviate.
The verse is a foundational proof for the permissibility of using names.
2
Identify the author's criticism
The author says 'this cold precaution did not avoid, nor this strange affectation'. He is referring to a specific approach that is overly cautious. Try to understand what he means by 'cold precaution' and 'strange affectation'.
3
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why the author starts with this verse. It shows that using names is a commanded act, not optional. The caution he criticizes might be a form of ta'til (negation) in disguise.
This leads to the next lesson on the Shia interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمرنا أن ندعوه عز وجل بأحسن الاسمين المتقابلين، كما قال تعالى : ( وَلِلَّهِ الْأَسْمَاءُ الحُسْنَى فَادْعُوهُ بِهَا وَذَرُوا الَّذِينَ يُلْحِدُونَ فِي أَسْمَائِهِ سَيُجْزَوْنَ مَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ) [الأعراف: 180]، ولم يحترز هذا الاحتراز البارد، ولا تكلف هذا التكلف الشارد. وقالت طائفة من الشيعة لا يجوز التعطيل من الأسماء الحسنى، ولا يجوز التشبيه بالصفات التي يشترك فيها الخلق، فيطلق على الباري سبحانه الوجود وندعوه بأحسن الأسماء وهو العليم القدير السميع البصير، إلى غير ذلك مما ورد في التنزيل، لكن لا بمعنى الوصف والصفة لأن عليا عليه السلام) كان يقول في توحيده لا يوصف بوصف، ولا يحد بحد، ولا يقدر بمقدار الذي كيف الكيف لا يقال له كيف، والذي أين الأين لا يقال له أين لكنا نطلق الأسماء بمعنى الإعطاء فهو موجود بمعنى أنه يعطي الوجود، وقادر وعالم بمعنى أنه واهب العلم للعالمين والقدرة للقادرين، حي بمعنى أنه يحيي الموتى قيوم بمعنى يحيي الموتى، قيوم بمعنى أنه يقيم العالم، سميع بصير؛ أي خالق السمع والبصر ولا نقول : إنه عالم لذاته أو يعلم، بل هو إله العالمين بالذات والعالمين بالعلم، وعلى هذا المنهاج يسلكون في إطلاق الأسامي وكذلك الواحد والعليم والقدير.
And we were commanded to call Him, mighty and majestic, by the best of the two opposing names, as He said: 'And to Allah belong the best names, so invoke Him by them and leave those who deviate concerning His names. They will be recompensed for what they used to do.' [al-A'raf 180]. And this cold precaution did not avoid, nor this strange affectation. And a group of the Shia said: Negation of the best names is not permissible, nor is anthropomorphism by attributes in which creation shares. So they apply to the Creator, exalted, existence and we call Him by the best names—He is the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful, the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing, and other such things that came in the revelation—but not in the sense of description and attribute, because Ali (peace be upon him) used to say in his declaration of God's unity: He is not described by any description, nor bounded by any limit, nor measured by any measure; He to whom the 'how' is said 'no how', and He to whom the 'where' is said 'no where'. But we use the names in the sense of giving: so He is existent meaning He gives existence; Powerful and Knowing meaning He is the bestower of knowledge to the knowers and power to the powerful; Living meaning He gives life to the dead; Self-Sustaining meaning He gives life to the dead, Self-Sustaining meaning He sustains the world; Hearing and Seeing meaning the Creator of hearing and sight. And we do not say: He is knowing by Himself or knows, rather He is the God of the worlds by essence and the worlds by knowledge. And on this path they proceed in applying the names, and likewise the One, the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents two elements: first, the author's initial statement that we are commanded to use the best names for God, citing the Quranic verse, and criticizes a certain precaution or affectation (perhaps referring to those who avoid using names due to fear of anthropomorphism). Second, he introduces a group of Shia who articulate a position on divine names and attributes: they reject both ta'til (negation of names) and tashbih (anthropomorphism in attributes). They apply names like alim, qadir, etc., but not in the sense of describing God with inherent attributes. Instead, they interpret them as indicating God's action of giving: e.g., 'existent' means He gives existence, 'knowing' means He gives knowledge, etc. They support this by quoting Ali (peace be upon him) who said that God is not described by any description, not bounded, not measured, and deny any 'how' or 'where'. They assert that names are used in a sense of bestowal (i'ta'). They explicitly deny that God is 'knowing by Himself' or 'knows', but rather He is the God of the worlds by essence and by knowledge—this seems to mean He is the origin and giver of knowledge. This position rejects inherent attributes to avoid tashbih, but they still affirm the names by interpreting them relationally.

Key Terms
التعطيل
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'this cold precaution'?
A) A precaution that is warm and welcoming.
B) A precaution that is ineffective and overly cautious.
C) A precaution that is recommended by the Shia.
D) A precaution that avoids all names.

Key Takeaway
We are commanded to use Allah's best names without excessive caution or affectation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah using His names, do you ever hold back because you feel your sins make you unworthy to say 'Ar-Rahman' or 'Al-Ghafur'? What would it take for you to trust His command and use His names with boldness?
?
The author calls 'cold precaution' a failure. Where in your own life do you let excessive caution—rather than obedience—guide your actions? Identify one area where you have been overthinking instead of simply doing what Allah commanded.
?
Do you find any of Allah's names uncomfortable to use because they seem too intimate or too powerful? Write down one name that you have avoided, and commit to using it in your next supplication, as He commands.

The Shia View: Attributes as Bestowal (I'ta')
Shi'a group explains divine names as indicating bestowal (i'ta') to avoid anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia position that divine names refer to God's acts of giving rather than inherent attributes, as a way to avoid both ta'til and tashbih.

Core Idea
Shi'a theologians interpret names like 'Knowing' as meaning 'Bestower of knowledge' to avoid anthropomorphism.

In response to the dilemma of affirming divine names without falling into anthropomorphism, a group of Shia theologians proposed a distinctive solution. They maintain that we should use all the names mentioned in the Quran, but not in the sense of describing God with inherent attributes. Instead, they interpret each name as indicating an act of bestowal (i'ta'). For example, 'alim' means He gives knowledge, not that He has knowledge. They support this by quoting Imam Ali's statements about God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia position
Read the second part of the passage carefully. Note how they affirm using names like alim, qadir, sami', basir, but with a different meaning.
2
Understand their reasoning
They quote Ali: 'He is not described by any description, nor bounded by any limit.' This is to avoid tashbih. Then they explain the meaning of each name as giving.
Notice the examples: 'existent' means 'gives existence', etc.
3
Identify what they deny
They explicitly deny that God is 'knowing by Himself' or that He 'knows'. Instead, He is the God of the worlds by essence and by knowledge. This is subtle—they separate essence from attributes.
This is a key difference from Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمرنا أن ندعوه عز وجل بأحسن الاسمين المتقابلين، كما قال تعالى : ( وَلِلَّهِ الْأَسْمَاءُ الحُسْنَى فَادْعُوهُ بِهَا وَذَرُوا الَّذِينَ يُلْحِدُونَ فِي أَسْمَائِهِ سَيُجْزَوْنَ مَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ) [الأعراف: 180]، ولم يحترز هذا الاحتراز البارد، ولا تكلف هذا التكلف الشارد. وقالت طائفة من الشيعة لا يجوز التعطيل من الأسماء الحسنى، ولا يجوز التشبيه بالصفات التي يشترك فيها الخلق، فيطلق على الباري سبحانه الوجود وندعوه بأحسن الأسماء وهو العليم القدير السميع البصير، إلى غير ذلك مما ورد في التنزيل، لكن لا بمعنى الوصف والصفة لأن عليا عليه السلام) كان يقول في توحيده لا يوصف بوصف، ولا يحد بحد، ولا يقدر بمقدار الذي كيف الكيف لا يقال له كيف، والذي أين الأين لا يقال له أين لكنا نطلق الأسماء بمعنى الإعطاء فهو موجود بمعنى أنه يعطي الوجود، وقادر وعالم بمعنى أنه واهب العلم للعالمين والقدرة للقادرين، حي بمعنى أنه يحيي الموتى قيوم بمعنى يحيي الموتى، قيوم بمعنى أنه يقيم العالم، سميع بصير؛ أي خالق السمع والبصر ولا نقول : إنه عالم لذاته أو يعلم، بل هو إله العالمين بالذات والعالمين بالعلم، وعلى هذا المنهاج يسلكون في إطلاق الأسامي وكذلك الواحد والعليم والقدير.
And we were commanded to call Him, mighty and majestic, by the best of the two opposing names, as He said: 'And to Allah belong the best names, so invoke Him by them and leave those who deviate concerning His names. They will be recompensed for what they used to do.' [al-A'raf 180]. And this cold precaution did not avoid, nor this strange affectation. And a group of the Shia said: Negation of the best names is not permissible, nor is anthropomorphism by attributes in which creation shares. So they apply to the Creator, exalted, existence and we call Him by the best names—He is the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful, the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing, and other such things that came in the revelation—but not in the sense of description and attribute, because Ali (peace be upon him) used to say in his declaration of God's unity: He is not described by any description, nor bounded by any limit, nor measured by any measure; He to whom the 'how' is said 'no how', and He to whom the 'where' is said 'no where'. But we use the names in the sense of giving: so He is existent meaning He gives existence; Powerful and Knowing meaning He is the bestower of knowledge to the knowers and power to the powerful; Living meaning He gives life to the dead; Self-Sustaining meaning He gives life to the dead, Self-Sustaining meaning He sustains the world; Hearing and Seeing meaning the Creator of hearing and sight. And we do not say: He is knowing by Himself or knows, rather He is the God of the worlds by essence and the worlds by knowledge. And on this path they proceed in applying the names, and likewise the One, the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents two elements: first, the author's initial statement that we are commanded to use the best names for God, citing the Quranic verse, and criticizes a certain precaution or affectation (perhaps referring to those who avoid using names due to fear of anthropomorphism). Second, he introduces a group of Shia who articulate a position on divine names and attributes: they reject both ta'til (negation of names) and tashbih (anthropomorphism in attributes). They apply names like alim, qadir, etc., but not in the sense of describing God with inherent attributes. Instead, they interpret them as indicating God's action of giving: e.g., 'existent' means He gives existence, 'knowing' means He gives knowledge, etc. They support this by quoting Ali (peace be upon him) who said that God is not described by any description, not bounded, not measured, and deny any 'how' or 'where'. They assert that names are used in a sense of bestowal (i'ta'). They explicitly deny that God is 'knowing by Himself' or 'knows', but rather He is the God of the worlds by essence and by knowledge—this seems to mean He is the origin and giver of knowledge. This position rejects inherent attributes to avoid tashbih, but they still affirm the names by interpreting them relationally.

Key Terms
التعطيل
التشبيه
الإعطاء
واهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that this Shia view completely denies all names and attributes.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm the names and even reject ta'til, but they reinterpret the names as acts of giving rather than inherent attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the Shia group mean when they say God is 'existent'?
A) He has existence as an attribute.
B) He gives existence to others.
C) He is the only one who exists.
D) He exists in all places.

Key Takeaway
Some Shia theologians interpret divine names as referring to God's bestowal of qualities, not inherent attributes, to preserve transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you primarily think of Him as knowing all things, or as the source of all knowledge? How does your default interpretation affect your relationship with Him? Before today, were you even aware that there was a difference?
?
Ali's statement that God is 'not described by any description, nor bounded by any limit' confronts our tendency to conceptualize God. In your worship, do you ever feel uncomfortable with the fact that God is beyond all your descriptions? What is one specific thing you can do this week to remind yourself of His transcendence when you make supplication?
?
The Shia group risked a radical reinterpretation of divine names to protect God from being compared to creation. Are you willing to let go of any comfortable, familiar image of God that might inadvertently limit Him? Name one mental image or attribute you might be clinging to that could verge on tashbih, and commit to replacing it with the language of the Qur'an and Sunnah without explaining the 'how.'

Imam al-Baqir's Quote and the Salaf's Principle on Divine Attributes
Author quotes al-Baqir and defends an inference-based approach to attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqir's statement about divine attributes avoids ta'til and aligns with the Salaf's principle of refraining from speculation.

Core Idea
We know God's attributes through His creation, not by examining His essence.

In this passage, the author presents a quote from Imam al-Baqir that offers a middle path between outright denial of attributes (ta'til) and speculative inquiry. He argues that affirming God's attributes by inferring from their effects in creation is a valid and safe approach, and he shows that this interpretation is supported by the consensus of the Salaf and the words of Ali ibn Abi Talib. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical style of al-Baqir's questions.
2
Identify the three rhetorical questions
Al-Baqir asks: Is He called knowing except because He bestows knowledge? Powerful except because He bestows power? This establishes a pattern of inference from creation to Creator.
These questions are the core of the argument.
3
Understand the distinction from pure negation
The author explicitly says this is not ta'til. Compare with the extreme view that denies all names and attributes. This view affirms names but interprets them differently.
Ta'til means depriving God of attributes.
4
Learn the principle of avoiding speculation
The passage warns against delving into whether attributes are identical to essence or distinct meanings. Such inquiry is seen as overstepping human limits.
This reflects a key Salaf principle.
5
Connect to Salaf consensus and Ali's saying
The author cites the Salaf's agreement that whatever comes to mind is created by Allah. He then shows how Ali's saying 'He is not described by any description' fits this interpretation.
This grounds the view in authoritative sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينسب إلى محمد بن علي الباقر أنه قال : هل سمي عالما إلا لأنه واهب العلم للعالمين، وهل سمي قادرًا إلا لأنه واهب القدرة للقادرين، وليس هذا قولا بالتعطيل، فإنه لم يمنع من إطلاق الأسامي والصفات كمن امتنع وقال : ليس بمعدوم ولا موجود ولا عالم ولا جاهل ولا قادر ولا عاجز، ولكنه استدل بالقدرة في القادرين على أنه قادر وبالعلم في العالمين على أنه عالم، وبالحياة في الأحياء على أنه حي قيوم، واقتصر على ذلك من غير خوض في الصفات بأنها لذاته أو لمعاني قائمة بذاته كيلا يكون متصرفا في جلاله بفكره العقلي وخياله الوهمي، وقد اجتمع السلف بل الأمة على أن ما دار في الوهم أو خطر في الخيال والفهم فالله سبحانه خالقه ومبدعه، ولا محمل لقول علي - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه - : لا يوصف بوصف، إلا هذا الذي ذكرناه.
And it is attributed to Muhammad ibn Ali al-Baqir that he said: Is He called knowing only because He is the bestower of knowledge to the knowers? And is He called powerful only because He is the bestower of power to the powerful? And this is not a statement of negation (ta'til), for he did not prevent the application of names and attributes like one who refrained and said: He is neither non-existent nor existent, neither knowing nor ignorant, neither powerful nor powerless. But he inferred from power in the powerful that He is powerful, and from knowledge in the knowers that He is knowing, and from life in the living that He is living and self-sustaining. And he confined himself to that without delving into attributes—whether they belong to His essence or are meanings subsisting in His essence—lest he be acting in His majesty through his rational thought and his imaginative fancy. And the Salaf, indeed the whole Umma, have agreed that whatever occurs in the mind or comes to imagination and understanding, Allah is its Creator and Originator. And there is no interpretation for the saying of Ali (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him): 'He is not described by any description' except this which we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by quoting Imam al-Baqir (Muhammad ibn Ali) who asks rhetorically: Is God called 'knowing' only because He bestows knowledge? This supports the earlier Shia view. The author then defends this position against the accusation of ta'til (negation of attributes) by distinguishing it from outright denial: this view does not reject names and attributes entirely, but it interprets them in a specific way—inferring God's attributes from their manifestations in creation (e.g., from power in the powerful). The author emphasizes that this approach avoids speculation about whether attributes are identical to God's essence or distinct meanings subsisting in it, because such inquiry would amount to applying human rational and imaginative faculties to God, which is inappropriate. He then cites a consensus of the Salaf (and the whole Umma) that whatever occurs in the mind or imagination, Allah is its Creator and Originator, implying that human concepts of attributes do not refer to anything in God's essence. Finally, he asserts that the only way to understand Ali's saying 'He is not described by any description' is this interpretation: affirming names but not as descriptions of an inherent essence.

Key Terms
التعطيل
الاستدلال
السلف
الصفات
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This position completely negates all attributes of God.
Correction: It affirms names and attributes like 'knowing' and 'powerful' but interprets them as acts of bestowal, not as inherent qualities of an essence that we can describe.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the position attributed to al-Baqir differ from pure negation (ta'til)?
A) It denies all attributes entirely.
B) It affirms names and attributes but interprets them as acts of bestowal.
C) It only negates attributes that are shared with creation.
D) It says God is not existent nor non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqir's approach affirms divine attributes by inferring them from their manifestations in creation, avoiding both negation and speculative inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe someone's knowledge or power, do you immediately turn your heart to the One who bestowed it, or do you stop at the creation? What would change in your daily life if you consciously practiced this inference?
?
The Salaf agreed that whatever occurs in your mind about Allah is created by Him. If your mental image of God is not God Himself, how does that affect your reliance on your own understanding? Are there aspects of God you have tried to 'figure out' that you need to entrust to Him?
?
If you stopped speculating about the 'how' of God's attributes today, which questions about God could you finally let go? Can you trust that the Living, Knowing, Powerful One revealed through creation is sufficient for you to worship and rely upon?

Affirming Divine Names through Effects without Ta'til
Refutation of ta'til and method of affirming names via effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the middle path of affirming divine names by inferring from effects in creation, avoiding both ta'til (complete negation) and tamthil (anthropomorphism).

Core Idea
God is known by His effects, not by His essence — a middle way between denial and likeness.

This passage from Imam al-Baqir presents a balanced approach to affirming God's names and attributes. It refutes those who negate all descriptions while also avoiding any claim to know how God's attributes relate to His essence. The method is to infer from the manifest effects in creation—knowledge, power, life—that God is Knowing, Powerful, Living, without venturing into speculative theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: a quote from al-Baqir, a refutation, and a concluding principle.
The passage is dense with theological nuance; take your time.
2
Identify the two extreme positions rejected
The passage rejects both complete negation (ta'til) and any attempt to describe God's essence through human imagination. Find the specific language that marks these rejections.
Ta'til is defined as preventing the application of names altogether.
3
Understand the method of inference from effects
Notice how al-Baqir argues that God is called Knowing and Powerful because He bestows knowledge and power. This is an inference from the effect to the attribute, not from direct perception.
This method avoids both denial and anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the Salaf's principle
The passage states that whatever occurs in the human mind is created by God. Understand how this principle guards against forming mental images of God.
Connect this to the final statement by Imam Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينسب إلى محمد بن علي الباقر أنه قال : هل سمي عالما إلا لأنه واهب العلم للعالمين، وهل سمي قادرًا إلا لأنه واهب القدرة للقادرين، وليس هذا قولا بالتعطيل، فإنه لم يمنع من إطلاق الأسامي والصفات كمن امتنع وقال : ليس بمعدوم ولا موجود ولا عالم ولا جاهل ولا قادر ولا عاجز، ولكنه استدل بالقدرة في القادرين على أنه قادر وبالعلم في العالمين على أنه عالم، وبالحياة في الأحياء على أنه حي قيوم، واقتصر على ذلك من غير خوض في الصفات بأنها لذاته أو لمعاني قائمة بذاته كيلا يكون متصرفا في جلاله بفكره العقلي وخياله الوهمي، وقد اجتمع السلف بل الأمة على أن ما دار في الوهم أو خطر في الخيال والفهم فالله سبحانه خالقه ومبدعه، ولا محمل لقول علي - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه - : لا يوصف بوصف، إلا هذا الذي ذكرناه.
It is attributed to Muhammad ibn Ali al-Baqir that he said: Is He called Knowing except because He is the bestower of knowledge to the knowers? And is He called Powerful except because He is the bestower of power to the powerful? And this is not a statement of ta'til (negation), for he did not prevent the application of names and attributes like one who refrained and said: He is not non-existent nor existent, nor knowing nor ignorant, nor powerful nor powerless. Rather, he inferred from power in the powerful that He is Powerful, and from knowledge in the knowers that He is Knowing, and from life in the living that He is Living and Self-Subsisting. And he confined himself to that without delving into the attributes as to whether they are His essence or meanings subsisting in His essence, so that he would not be meddling in His majesty with his rational thought and imaginative fancy. And the Salaf, indeed the entire community, have agreed that whatever occurs in the imagination or crosses the mind and understanding, God—glorified is He—is its Creator and Originator. And there is no interpretation of the saying of Ali—peace and blessings be upon him—'He is not described by any description' except this which we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending a middle way regarding divine attributes. It begins by quoting Muhammad ibn Ali al-Baqir, who explains that God is called 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' because He is the bestower of knowledge and power to created beings. This does not amount to ta'til (complete negation of attributes), because the author does not deny the names altogether, unlike those who say God is neither existent nor non-existent, etc. Rather, the method is to infer from the effects (knowledge, power, life in creatures) that God possesses these attributes in a manner befitting Him. The author then emphasizes that the Salaf and the whole community agree that whatever occurs in the human mind is created by God and is not like Him. Finally, Ali's statement 'He is not described by any description' is interpreted as meaning that God is not described by any description that would liken Him to creation or that would be a product of human imagination, but the descriptions derived from effects (like Knowing, Powerful) are permissible.

Key Terms
تعطيل (ta'til)
أسامي (asma')
صفات (sifat)
سلف (salaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author denies that God has knowledge and power as attributes; actually he affirms them through effects but avoids discussing modality.
Correction: The passage clearly states that God is Knowing and Powerful because He is the bestower of knowledge and power. This is an affirmation, not a denial. The author only warns against speculating about how these attributes relate to God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do we affirm that God is Knowing and Powerful?
A) By directly perceiving His essence
B) By inferring from knowledge and power in creatures
C) By denying all attributes
D) By comparing Him to creatures

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are affirmed by inference from their effects in creation, without claiming to know their modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the knowledge, power, and life around you, do you spontaneously see them as gifts from God, or do you take them for granted? Will you today pause and say, 'Allah is al-'Alim, al-Qadir, al-Hayy' with every instance you witness?
?
The Salaf refrained from speculating about how God's attributes are. Are you ever tempted to imagine God's nature or to ask 'how'? Will you repent from such curiosity and confine yourself to the safe path of inference from effects?
?
Ali said, 'He is not described by any description.' Do you ever unknowingly describe God with your own imagination, creating a mental image? Will you commit to rejecting every mental image and affirming only the descriptions given by revelation and inference from effects?
