The Reality of Mental Aspects: Refuting Linguistic Reductionism
The author argues that mental aspects are real and not merely linguistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mental aspects (al-wujuh al-aqliyya) are considered real and not merely linguistic, and how the author refutes the view that generality and particularity are reducible to words.

Core Idea
Mental aspects are real because they remain constant while languages change.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in Islamic theology: are universal concepts (like 'human' or 'existence') just words, or do they have a real mental existence? The author argues that mental aspects are real and that reducing them to mere words leads to contradictions. He uses the fact that expressions change across languages while the underlying concepts remain stable as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Focus on the overall argument.
Notice the repeated emphasis on change vs. stability.
2
Identify the two distinct sciences
Find the opening statement about two sciences – necessary and acquired. Understand its role in setting up the discussion.
This frames the debate as a matter of knowledge, not mere words.
3
Analyze the refutation of 'one thing has no participation'
Examine how the author distinguishes between the specific thing (which has no share) and the attributes (which are not specific but allow generality).
This is key to showing that attributes like 'generality' are real.
4
Understand the proof from language variation
The author argues that expressions change with language and time, but mental aspects do not. This proves that mental aspects are independent of words.
Ask yourself: if mental aspects were mere words, they would change too.
5
Reflect on the final point about properties of essences
The author concludes that things are distinguished by essential properties (khawass) that are unique, so there is no sharing in those properties.
This reinforces that general attributes must refer to real mental aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهما علمان متمايزان ؛ أحدهما ضروري، والثاني مكتسب، وليس ذلك كالنسب والإضافات فإنها ترجع إلى ألفاظ مجردة ليس فيها علم محقق متعلق بمعلوم محقق. وقولهم : الشيء الواحد لا شركة فيه ، والمشترك لا وجود له باطل، فإن الشيء المعين من حيث هو معين لا شركة فيه وهذه الصفات التي أثبتناها ليست معينة مخصصة، بل هي صفات يقع بها التخصيص والتعيين، ويقع فيها الشمول والعموم، وهو من القضايا الضرورية كالوجوه عندكم ، ومن رد التعميم والتخصيص إلى مجرد الألفاظ فقد أبطل الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية، أليست العبارات تتبدل لغة فلغة وحالة فحالة ؟ وهذه الوجوه العقلية لا تتبدل بل الذوات ثابتة عليها قبل التعبير عنها بلفظ عام وخاص على السواء، ومن ردها إلى الاعتبارات أفقد ناقض وردها إلى العبارات فإن الاعتبارات لا تتبدل ولا تتغير بعقل وعقل والعبارات تتبدل بلسان ولسان وزمان وزمان. وقولهم : حد الشيء وحقيقته وذاته عبارات عن معبر واحد، والأشياء إنما تتميز بخواص ذواتها ولا شركة في الخواص.
There are two distinct sciences: one necessary, and the other acquired. And that is not like relations and additions, for they refer to mere words in which there is no certain knowledge connected to a certain known object. And their statement: 'The one thing has no participation in it, and the common has no existence' is false. For the specific thing insofar as it is specific has no participation in it. And these attributes which we have affirmed are not specific and restricted, but rather they are attributes by which specification and restriction occur, and in which inclusiveness and generality occur. And that is from necessary propositions, like the aspects (wujuh) according to you. And whoever reduces generality and particularity to mere words has nullified the mental aspects and considerations. Are not expressions altered language by language and state by state? And these mental aspects do not change; rather, the essences are established upon them before expression by a general or particular word alike. And whoever reduces them to considerations has contradicted, and whoever reduces them to expressions [is mistaken], for considerations do not change or alter with one intellect and another, while expressions change with one tongue and another, and one time and another. And their statement: 'The definition, reality, and essence of a thing are expressions about one referent' — things are only distinguished by the properties of their essences, and there is no participation in properties.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the nominalist position that reduces all generality and particularity to mere words. The author begins by stating that there are two distinct sciences: one necessary (acquired by necessity) and one acquired. He denies that relations and additions are mere words without certain knowledge. Then he addresses the claim that 'the one thing has no participation' and 'the common does not exist.' He argues that the specific thing as specific has no sharing, but the attributes he affirms (like generality and particularity) are not specific themselves; rather, they are attributes by which things become specific or general. These attributes are real and are known by necessary reason, like the 'aspects' (wujuh) that the opponents accept. He then argues that if someone reduces generality and particularity to words, he destroys the mental aspects and considerations (i'tibarat aqliyya). His proof: expressions change with languages and situations, but mental aspects remain constant. Essences have these aspects even before any particular word is used. So if one reduces them to words, he contradicts the stability of mental aspects. If one reduces them to mere considerations, that is also contradictory because considerations do not change while expressions do. The final part addresses the claim that definition, reality, and essence are just expressions for one referent; the author argues that things are distinguished by essential properties (khawass) which are unique, so there is no sharing in those properties. Thus, general attributes must refer to real mental aspects.

Key Terms
وجوه عقلية
اعتبارات
تعميم
تخصيص
خواص الذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universal concepts like 'human' are just words with no real mental reality.
Correction: The author argues that mental aspects (wujuh) are real because they remain constant across languages, unlike words which change. Also, things are distinguished by essential properties that cannot be shared, implying real universals.

Checkpoint

A) They change with each language.
B) They are destroyed.
C) They remain unchanged.
D) They become particular.

Key Takeaway
The reality of mental aspects is proven by the constancy of concepts across changing languages; thus, universals are not mere words.

The Objection from Common Terms in Definitions and the Denier's Response
Objection and response on general terms in definitions proving states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the affirmer's argument that general terms in definitions prove the reality of states, and the denier's counter that this very argument is self-defeating.

Core Idea
General terms in definitions seem to prove states, but the word 'state' itself defeats the argument.

This passage presents a crucial exchange between the affirmer of real attributes (the muthbit) and the denier (the nafi). The affirmer argues that definitions of universals like 'substance' require general terms, which must refer to real 'states' (ahwal). The denier counters by turning the argument on its head: the very word 'state' is a general term, so the same reasoning that was supposed to prove states either dissolves into wordplay or leads to an infinite regress. Let's follow this dense argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmer's argument
Read from 'قال المثبت' to 'فقد أثبتم معنى عاما' and note how he argues that definitions of universals require a general term like 'tahayyuz' (occupying space).
Focus on the logical necessity: without a general term, each individual substance would need its own separate definition.
2
Identify the affirmer's conclusion
From 'فإذا لم نجد بدا' to 'وتلك أحوال له', the affirmer concludes that definitions require general terms, which refer to attributes that are not essences—these are 'states', aspects, or mental considerations.
The affirmer leaves the terminology open but insists on the reality.
3
Read the denier's response
From 'قال النافون' to 'محال', see how the denier attacks the concept of 'state' itself: the term 'state' is a general term, so it either reduces to mere words or requires another state to particularize it (regress).
This is a classic self-referential argument.
4
Understand the final rebuttal
The denier ends by noting that the affirmer's principle 'the attribute is not described' fails because the affirmer already makes existence an attribute of states (attribute of attribute).
The passage cuts off at 'الوجود' but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبت : هب أن الأمر كذلك، لكن خاصية كل شيء معين غير، وخاصية كل نوع محقق غير وأنتم لا تحدون جوهرًا بعينه على الخصوص، بل تحدون الجوهر من حيث هو جوهر على الإطلاق، فقد أثبتم معنى عاما يعم الجواهر وهو التحيز مثلا، وإلا لكان كل جوهر على حالة محتاجًا إلى حد على حياله، ولا يجوز أن يجري حكم جوهر في جوهر من التحيز وقبول الأعراض والقيام بالنفس، فإذا لم نجد بدا من إدراج أمر عام في الحد ، وذلك يبطل قولكم : إن الأشياء إنما تتمايز بذواتها، ويصح قولنا: إن الحدود لا تستغني عن عمومات ألفاظ تدل على صفات عموم الذوات وصفات خصوص، وتلك أحوال لها ووجوه واعتبارات عقلية، أو ما شئت فسمها بعد الاتفاق على المعاني والحقايق. قال النافون : غاية تقريركم في إثبات الحال هو التمسك بعمومات وخصوصات ووجوه عقلية واعتبارات، أما العموم والخصوص فمنتقض عليكم بنفس الحال، فإن لفظ الحال يشمل جنس الأحوال، وحال هي صفة لشيء تخص ذلك الشيء لا محالة، فلا يخلو إما أن يرجع معناه إلى عبارة تعم وعبارة تخص، فخذوا منا في ساير العبارات العامة والخاصة كذلك، وإما أن ترجع إلى معنى آخر وراء العبارة فيؤدي إلى إثبات الحال للحال وذلك محال، ولا يغني عن هذا الإلزام قولكم الصفة لا توصف، فإنكم أول من أثبت للصفة صفة حيث جعلتم الوجود
The affirmer said: Grant that the matter is so, but the property of each specific thing is different, and the property of each realized type is different. And you do not define a particular substance specifically, but you define substance insofar as it is substance absolutely. So you have affirmed a general meaning that encompasses substances, such as being in space (tahayyuz) for example. Otherwise, every substance would be in a state needing a definition specific to it, and it would not be permissible for the judgment of one substance to apply to another with respect to occupancy (tahayyuz), accepting accidents, and self-subsistence. Therefore, since we cannot avoid including a general matter in the definition, that invalidates your statement that things are only distinguished by their essences. And our statement becomes correct: definitions cannot dispense with general terms that indicate attributes of the generality of essences and attributes of particularity. And those are states (ahwal) for them, and aspects, and mental considerations, or whatever you wish to call them after agreement on meanings and realities. The deniers said: The extent of your argument in affirming the state (al-hal) is adherence to generalities and particularities and mental aspects and considerations. As for generality and particularity, it is refuted against you by the state itself. For the word 'state' (hal) encompasses the genus of states, and a state is an attribute of a thing that particularizes that thing necessarily. So either its meaning goes back to a general expression and a particular expression—then take from us concerning all general and particular expressions likewise—or it goes back to a meaning other than the expression, which leads to affirming a state for a state, and that is impossible. And your statement 'the attribute is not described' does not avail against this objection, for you are the first to affirm an attribute for an attribute when you made existence
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the affirmer of attributes (muthbit) and the response from the denier (nafi). The affirmer argues: even if you say each thing is distinguished by its own special properties, when you define a universal like 'substance' you use a general term like 'occupying space' (tahayyuz). Otherwise, you would have to define every individual substance separately, and no universal judgment would be possible. Therefore, definitions require general terms, which refer to general attributes (ahwal) that are common. So your claim that things are only distinguished by their essences is false; there must be real general attributes. These can be called states, aspects, or mental considerations. The denier responds: your entire argument for affirming states (al-hal) relies on concepts of generality and particularity, which are themselves problematic. Specifically, the term 'state' (hal) itself is a general term that covers many states. But a state is supposed to be a specific attribute of a particular thing. So if you say the meaning of 'state' is a general expression and a particular expression, then you are just reducing it to words—which is what you originally argued against. If you say it refers to a meaning beyond the expression, then you have to affirm a state for the state (since the word 'state' is general and needs a state to particularize it?), which leads to an infinite regress. This is impossible. And the claim that 'the attribute is not described' does not save you, because you are the ones who first made existence an attribute of the attribute (i.e., you attribute existence to states), so you have already made an attribute have an attribute.

Key Terms
الحال
أحوال
التحيز
الحد
العموم
الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The use of general terms in definitions necessarily proves the reality of universal attributes (states) beyond mere words.
Correction: The denier shows that the same argument can be turned against itself: the term 'state' is a general term, and if it proves anything, it either reduces to linguistic convention or leads to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is the denier's main argument against the affirmer's use of general terms to prove the reality of states?
A) The affirmer's definitions are inconsistent.
B) The term 'state' itself is general, so it either reduces to words or leads to a state for a state.
C) The affirmer has not proven the existence of anything.
D) The denier accepts the argument.

Key Takeaway
The argument from general terms in definitions fails because the very term used to denote universals ('state') is itself a general term, leading to either triviality or regress.

?
The denier shows that the term 'state' itself can lead to an infinite regress if taken as a real thing. What would it mean for you to ensure that your claimed states are not just empty words but actual, particular parts of your character? ?
The affirmer insists that definitions need general terms, yet the denier exposes a flaw. Have you ever accepted a religious or philosophical idea simply because it sounds universal and self-evident, without questioning its logical foundations? The Error of Affirming Distinct States (Aḥwāl)
The author refutes those who posit distinct states (aḥwāl) for existing things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming distinct states (ahwal) for existing things is logically absurd, and how the author demonstrates that attributes like accidentality and color-ness are identical to the thing itself.

Core Idea
Attributes are not separate realities; they are identical to the thing itself.

This passage confronts a subtle metaphysical error: treating abstract properties like 'accidentality' or 'color-ness' as distinct entities. The author argues that such properties are nothing but expressions for the same particular thing. Let's read how he dismantles the notion of distinct states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own argument unfold without interruption.
The passage is dense; read slowly to grasp the logical flow.
2
Identify the central claim
The author claims that what is specific to a thing and what is shared with others are one relative to that thing. Find where he states this and note how he uses examples to illustrate.
The key phrase: 'والذي يشاركه فيه غيره واحد بالنسبة إلى ذلك المعين'.
3
Analyze the specification chain
Notice the chain: existence → accidentality → color-ness → blackness. The author shows each is identical to the previous when specified. This demonstrates the absurdity of regarding them as separate states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجه خطأ المثبتين للحال فهو أنهم أثبتوا لموجود معين مشار إليه صفات مختصة به وصفات يشاركه فيها غيره من الموجودات، وهو من أمحل المحال، فإن المختص بالشيء المعين والذي يشاركه فيه غيره واحد بالنسبة إلى ذلك المعين، فوجود عرض معين وعرضيته ولونيته وسواديته عبارات عن ذلك المعين المشار إليه، فإن الوجود إذا تخصص بالعرضية فهو بعينه عرض، والعرضية إذا تخصصت باللونية فهي بعينها لون، وكذلك اللونية بالسوادية، والسوادية بهذا السواد المشار إليه، فليس من المعقول أن توجد صفة لشيء واحد معين وهي بعينها توجد لشيء آخر، فتكون صفة معينة في شيئين كسواد واحد في محلين، وجوهر واحد
As for the aspect of the error of those who affirm the state (al-ḥāl), it is that they affirm for a specific designated existent attributes specific to it and attributes in which other existents share with it. And this is among the most absurd absurdities. For that which is specific to the particular thing and that which others share with it are one relative to that particular. Thus, the existence of a specific accident, and its accidentality, its color-ness, and its blackness are expressions for that designated particular. For existence, when it is specified by accidentality, is exactly accident; and accidentality, when specified by color-ness, is exactly color; and likewise color-ness (specified) by blackness, and blackness (specified) by this black. So it is not reasonable that an attribute exist for one specific thing and that same attribute exist for another thing, such that a specific attribute would be in two things like one black in two locations, and one substance...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage refutes the view of those who posit distinct states (aḥwāl) in existing things, such as the view that an accident has distinct properties like accidentality, color-ness, blackness, etc. The author argues that these are not distinct realities; rather, they are all expressions for the same particular thing. For example, the existence of a specific accident, when specified by accidentality, is exactly the accident itself. Similarly, accidentality when specified by color-ness is exactly color. Thus, there is no real distinction between the thing and its attributes—they are identical. It would be absurd to think that the same attribute could exist in two different things, like one black color in two locations. This guards against the error of reifying abstract attributes as separate entities.

Key Terms
حال (ḥāl)
صفة (ṣifa)
وجود (wujūd)
عرضية (ʿaraḍiyya)
لونية (lawniyya)
سوادية (sawādiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes like accidentality and color-ness are distinct realities that 'attach' to a thing.
Correction: The author shows they are not separate; they are identical to the thing itself. Specification does not create new entities.

Checkpoint

A) They are distinct states
B) They are identical
C) Accidentality is a separate entity
D) Accidentality is more general

Key Takeaway
Attributes like accidentality and color-ness are not separate states; they are identical to the particular thing itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Positions on the Thingness of the Non-Existent
Lists different positions on the thingness of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spectrum of early kalām positions on whether the non-existent (al-ma'dūm) can be called a 'thing' (shay') or have 'thingness' (shay'iyya).

Core Idea
There was no single early kalām view on whether the non-existent is a thing.

The question of whether the non-existent (al-ma'dūm) can be described as a 'thing' was a major debate in early kalām. It touches on the nature of divine attributes and the objects of God's knowledge. In this passage, the author reports several positions ranging from full affirmation to complete denial of thingness for the non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of positions
Read the Arabic passage listing the different views. Notice the spectrum: some allow 'thingness' as a name, others forbid even that, some restrict 'thing' to the eternal, and Jahm ibn Safwan restricts it to the originated.
The positions are given in a compressed list.
2
Identify the key scholars
Pay attention to the names: Abu al-Hudhayl, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and Jahm ibn Safwan. These are major figures in the debate.
Their views represent opposite extremes.
3
Note the underlying issue
The debate is not about whether non-existents have any reality, but whether they have a stable, determinate nature (thubut) that can be known by God.
This will be important for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : للعرض، وخالفه جماعة؛ فمنهم من لم يطلق إلا اسم الشيئية، ومنهم من امتنع هذا الإطلاق أيضًا مثل أبي الهذيل وأبي الحسين البصري، ومنهم من من الشيء هو القديم، وأما الحادث فيسمى شيئًا بالمجاز والتوسع، وصار جهم بن صفوان إلى أن الشيء هو المحدث، والباري سبحانه [مشى الأشياء]. قال نفاة الشيئية عن العدم : قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن النفي والإثبات يتقابلان، والمنفي والمثبت يتقابلان تقابل التناقض، حتى إذا نفيت شيئًا معينا في حال مخصوص بجهة مخصوصة لم يمكنك إثباته على تلك الحال وتلك الجهة المخصوصة، ومن أنكر هذه القضية فقد أنكر هذه الحقايق كلها، وإذا كان المنفي ثابتا على أصل من قال : إن المعدوم شيء فقد رفع هذه القضية أصلا، وكان كمن قال : لا معقول ولا معلوم إلا الثبوت فحسب، وذلك خروج عن القضايا الأولية، وتحديد ذلك أن كل معدوم منفي وكل منفي ليس بثابت، فكل معدوم ليس بثابت.
He said: For the accident, and a group disagreed with him; among them are those who only applied the name of thingness, and among them are those who also forbade this application, such as Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and among them are those who [said] that the thing is the eternal, and as for the originated, it is called a thing metaphorically and by extension. Jahm ibn Safwan held that the thing is the originated, and the Creator, exalted is He, [؟ is the creator of things]. The deniers of thingness for non-existence said: It is established in the first principles of intellects that negation and affirmation are opposite, and the negated and the affirmed are opposite by opposition of contradiction, such that if you negate a specific thing in a specific state with a specific respect, you cannot affirm it in that same state and that same specific respect. Whoever denies this proposition has denied all these realities. If the negated is affirmed according to the principle of him who says that the non-existent is a thing, then he has uprooted this proposition entirely, and is like one who says: Nothing is intelligible or known except subsistence only. That is a departure from primary propositions. The specification of that is: Every non-existent is negated, and every negated is not subsistent, so every non-existent is not subsistent.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage first presents several positions regarding whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) can be called a 'thing' (shay') or have 'thingness' (shay'iyya). There is a spectrum: some restrict the term 'thingness' but not the word 'thing'; others completely forbid even that, like Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri; others hold that 'thing' properly applies only to the eternal, and to the contingent only metaphorically; Jahm ibn Safwan held the opposite, that 'thing' applies only to the originated. The author then reports the argument of those who deny thingness to non-existence (nufat al-shay'iyya 'an al-'adam). Their argument is based on the fundamental opposition between negation (nafy) and affirmation (ithbat). They assert that negation and affirmation are opposites in a contradictory sense: if you negate a specific thing under specific conditions, you cannot simultaneously affirm it under those same conditions. This is a primary axiom. If someone claims that the non-existent is a thing, then that which is negated (the non-existent) would be affirmed (i.e., subsistent), which violates this axiom. Such a person would be denying this primary rational principle, effectively saying that nothing is intelligible except subsistence (thubut). The conclusion is that every non-existent is negated, and every negated is not subsistent, so the non-existent is not subsistent.

Key Terms
شيئية (shay'iyya)
عدم (al-'adam)
شيء (shay')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether non-existent things are real in the sense of having existence.
Correction: The debate is about whether they have a stable nature (thubut) that can be known, not about existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who held that 'thing' applies only to the originated?
A) Abu al-Hudhayl
B) Jahm ibn Safwan
C) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri
D) The unnamed group who restricted it to the eternal

Key Takeaway
Early kalām scholars disagreed sharply on whether the non-existent has thingness, with positions ranging from full acceptance to total denial.

The Negators' Proof from Contradiction
Presents the negators' argument from the contradiction of negation and affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that denies thingness to the non-existent based on the contradictory opposition between negation and affirmation.

Core Idea
If the non-existent were a thing, negation and affirmation would not be opposites.

Having presented different positions, the author now reports the argument of those who deny thingness to the non-existent. Their reasoning is based on a fundamental rational principle: negation and affirmation are contradictory opposites. If the non-existent (which is negated) were also affirmed (as a thing), this principle would be violated.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قال نفاة الشيئية عن العدم'. Notice that it invokes 'first principles of intellects' and the concept of contradictory opposition.
The argument is concise and relies on basic logical axioms.
2
Understand the key premise
The negators assert that negation and affirmation are opposites in a contradictory sense – you cannot both negate and affirm the same thing under the same conditions.
This is presented as a primary, self-evident truth.
3
Trace the conclusion
If the non-existent were a thing, then the negated (non-existent) would be affirmed, contradicting the premise. Therefore, the non-existent cannot be a thing.
The final syllogism: every non-existent is negated, every negated is not subsistent, so every non-existent is not subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : للعرض، وخالفه جماعة؛ فمنهم من لم يطلق إلا اسم الشيئية، ومنهم من امتنع هذا الإطلاق أيضًا مثل أبي الهذيل وأبي الحسين البصري، ومنهم من من الشيء هو القديم، وأما الحادث فيسمى شيئًا بالمجاز والتوسع، وصار جهم بن صفوان إلى أن الشيء هو المحدث، والباري سبحانه [مشى الأشياء]. قال نفاة الشيئية عن العدم : قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن النفي والإثبات يتقابلان، والمنفي والمثبت يتقابلان تقابل التناقض، حتى إذا نفيت شيئًا معينا في حال مخصوص بجهة مخصوصة لم يمكنك إثباته على تلك الحال وتلك الجهة المخصوصة، ومن أنكر هذه القضية فقد أنكر هذه الحقايق كلها، وإذا كان المنفي ثابتا على أصل من قال : إن المعدوم شيء فقد رفع هذه القضية أصلا، وكان كمن قال : لا معقول ولا معلوم إلا الثبوت فحسب، وذلك خروج عن القضايا الأولية، وتحديد ذلك أن كل معدوم منفي وكل منفي ليس بثابت، فكل معدوم ليس بثابت.
He said: For the accident, and a group disagreed with him; among them are those who only applied the name of thingness, and among them are those who also forbade this application, such as Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and among them are those who [said] that the thing is the eternal, and as for the originated, it is called a thing metaphorically and by extension. Jahm ibn Safwan held that the thing is the originated, and the Creator, exalted is He, [؟ is the creator of things]. The deniers of thingness for non-existence said: It is established in the first principles of intellects that negation and affirmation are opposite, and the negated and the affirmed are opposite by opposition of contradiction, such that if you negate a specific thing in a specific state with a specific respect, you cannot affirm it in that same state and that same specific respect. Whoever denies this proposition has denied all these realities. If the negated is affirmed according to the principle of him who says that the non-existent is a thing, then he has uprooted this proposition entirely, and is like one who says: Nothing is intelligible or known except subsistence only. That is a departure from primary propositions. The specification of that is: Every non-existent is negated, and every negated is not subsistent, so every non-existent is not subsistent.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage first presents several positions regarding whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) can be called a 'thing' (shay') or have 'thingness' (shay'iyya). There is a spectrum: some restrict the term 'thingness' but not the word 'thing'; others completely forbid even that, like Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri; others hold that 'thing' properly applies only to the eternal, and to the contingent only metaphorically; Jahm ibn Safwan held the opposite, that 'thing' applies only to the originated. The author then reports the argument of those who deny thingness to non-existence (nufat al-shay'iyya 'an al-'adam). Their argument is based on the fundamental opposition between negation (nafy) and affirmation (ithbat). They assert that negation and affirmation are opposites in a contradictory sense: if you negate a specific thing under specific conditions, you cannot simultaneously affirm it under those same conditions. This is a primary axiom. If someone claims that the non-existent is a thing, then that which is negated (the non-existent) would be affirmed (i.e., subsistent), which violates this axiom. Such a person would be denying this primary rational principle, effectively saying that nothing is intelligible except subsistence (thubut). The conclusion is that every non-existent is negated, and every negated is not subsistent, so the non-existent is not subsistent.

Key Terms
نفي (nafy)
إثبات (ithbat)
ثبوت (thubut)
عدم (al-'adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether non-existent things can be known by God.
Correction: The argument is purely logical: if the non-existent were a thing, it would violate the law of non-contradiction between negation and affirmation.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the negators' argument?
A) The non-existent is not a thing because it would violate the opposition of negation and affirmation.
B) The non-existent is a thing because it can be known.
C) The non-existent is not subsistent because it is eternal.
D) The non-existent is both negated and affirmed.

Key Takeaway
The negators prove that the non-existent cannot be a thing because that would contradict the fundamental opposition between negation and affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that logically contradict each other, like affirming something while also denying it under the same conditions? How did you resolve that contradiction?
?
If the negators' argument proves that the non-existent cannot be a thing, what does that imply about how you should think about things that do not yet exist but are possible? Are they 'things' in your mind?
?
The argument says denying this primary proposition is a 'departure from primary propositions.' Are there any self-evident truths about your own existence or your relationship with Allah that you have denied or ignored? What would it cost you to accept them fully?

The Negators' Counter-Objection on Generality
Negators present a symmetrical argument about generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' argument that if subsistence is general, then the negated must also be general, and why this creates a problem for the affirmers.

Core Idea
If subsistence covers existent and non-existent, consistency demands the same for negation.

The negators quickly seize on the affirmers' concession that subsistence is broader than existence. They argue that this principle should apply symmetrically to negation, forcing a contradiction. Let's follow their reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the negators' argument carefully. Notice how they build on the affirmers' distinction.
2
Identify the symmetry demand
The negators argue that if subsistence is more general than existence, then negation should be more general than non-existence. Find that line.
3
See the forced contradiction
The negators then claim that if no difference is admitted, the affirmers' own position forces a contradiction. Understand why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة : الآن صرحتم بالحق حيث ميزتم بين قسم وقسم، فالثبوت عندكم أعم من الوجود، فإن الثبوت يشمل الموجود والمعدوم، فهلا قلتم في المقابل كذلك : وإن المنفي أعم من المعدوم حتى يكون صفة عمومة حالا أو وجها للمنفي ثابتا، كما كان صفة خصوص المعدوم حالا للمعدوم أو وجها ثابتا، فيتحقق أمر ثابت، ويرتفع المقابل العقلي، وإن لم يثبتوا فرقاً بين المنفي والمعدوم، ثم قالوا : كل معدوم شيء ثابت لزمهم أن يقولوا : كل منفي شيء ثابت، فرجع الإلزام عليهم رجوعاً ظاهرًا، وهو رفع التقابل بين النفي والإثبات. قال المثبتون : إذا حققتم الكلام في النفي والإثبات والموجود والمعدوم، وميزتم بين الخصوص والعموم فيه عاد الإلزام عليكم متوجها من وجهين؛ أحدهما : أنكم أثبتم في المعدوم خصوصاً وعموما أيضا حتى قلتم : منه ما هو واجب كالمستحيل، ومنه ما هو جائز كالممكن ومنه ما يستحيل لذاته كالجمع بين المتضادين، ومنه ما يستحيل لغيره كخلاف المعلوم، فهذه التقسيمات قد وردت على المعدوم، فأخذتم المعدومية عامة، وخصصتم بهذه الخصايص، فلولا أن المعدوم شيء ثابت وإلا لما تحقق فيه العموم والخصوص ولما تحقق التميز بين قسم وقسم. والوجه الثاني : أنكم اعترفتم بأن المنفي والمعدوم معلوم، وقد أخبرتم عنه، وتصرفتم بأفكاركم فيه، فما متعلق العلم؟ وما معنى التعلق إذا لم يكن شيئًا ثابتا أصلا؟
The deniers said: Now you have declared the truth by distinguishing between division and division, for subsistence according to you is more general than existence, since subsistence encompasses the existent and the non-existent. Why then did you not say likewise concerning the opposite: that the negated is more general than the non-existent, such that it becomes a general attribute, a state or respect for the negated that is subsistent, just as the specific attribute of non-existence is a state or respect for the non-existent that is subsistent? Then a subsistent thing would be realized, and the intellectual opposite would be removed. And if they do not establish a difference between the negated and the non-existent, then they say: Every non-existent is a subsistent thing, so they must say: Every negated is a subsistent thing. Thus the objection returns against them clearly, and it is the removal of the opposition between negation and affirmation. The affirmers said: When you examine the speech concerning negation and affirmation and the existent and non-existent, and you distinguish between specificity and generality therein, the objection returns against you directed in two respects: One of them: You have affirmed in the non-existent specificity and generality as well, until you said: Among it is what is necessary like the impossible, among it is what is contingent like the possible, among it is what is inherently impossible like the conjunction of opposites, and among it is what is impossible due to another like the contrary of what is known. These divisions have been applied to the non-existent, so you took non-existence as general and specified it with these properties. If the non-existent were not a subsistent thing, then generality and specificity would not be realized in it, nor would distinction between division and division be realized. The second respect: You have admitted that the negated and the non-existent are known, and you have informed about them, and you have occupied yourselves with them in your thoughts. So what is the object of knowledge? And what is the meaning of relation (ta'alluq) if it is not a subsistent thing at all?
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The negators first counter the affirmers' distinction between existence and subsistence. They point out that if subsistence is broader than existence (encompassing both existent and non-existent), then consistency demands that 'negated' (al-manfi) be broader than 'non-existent' (al-ma'dum). This would make the negated as a general category have some subsistent reality, and the original intellectual opposition would be restored. Furthermore, if no difference is admitted between 'negated' and 'non-existent', then the affirmers' claim that every non-existent is subsistent forces them to say every negated is subsistent, which clearly violates the opposition of negation and affirmation. The affirmers then give a twofold rebuttal. First, they argue that the negators themselves classify the non-existent into types: impossible (mustahil), possible (mumkin), etc. Such classification presupposes that the non-existent has some stable character (thubut) to be classified; otherwise, it could not be divided into general and specific categories. Second, they note that the negators admit that the non-existent is known (ma'lum) and they speak about it and think about it. The relation (ta'alluq) of knowledge to something requires an object that is somehow stable; if the non-existent were absolutely nothing, then knowledge would have no object. Thus, the non-existent must have some mode of subsistence (thubut) to be an object of knowledge.

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
ثبوت
منفي
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the negators' objection?
A) They deny subsistence.
B) They argue for symmetry between subsistence and negation.
C) They affirm that existence is more general.
D) They reject the possibility of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The negators argue that consistency forces the affirmers to extend the generality from subsistence to negation, leading to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you demand logical consistency from others—online, at work, or with family—are you equally willing to apply that standard to your own beliefs and actions? What would it cost you to be as rigorous with yourself as you are with them? Commit to one area today where you will examine your own contradictions.
?
The negators used the affirmers’ own principles to argue against them. Is there an argument you frequently use against others that could just as easily be turned against you? What does that reveal about your motives? Write down that argument and ask God for sincerity.
?
This debate centers on what is truly real and permanent. If you were to strip away everything fleeting in your life—status, comfort, plans—what remains that is truly subsistent? Are you building your life on what is real or on shadows? Name one concrete change you will make today to anchor yourself in what endures.

The Affirmers' Twofold Rebuttal
Affirmers rebut with two arguments: classification and object of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the affirmers' two arguments: classification of the non-existent implies its subsistence, and knowledge requires an object.

Core Idea
Classifying the non-existent and knowing it both demand its subsistence.

The affirmers counter the negators' objection by pointing out that the negators themselves treat the non-existent as something that can be classified and known. These very acts presuppose that the non-existent has some stable reality. Let's examine their two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmers' response
Read the affirmers' reply. They present two distinct arguments. Notice the structure: 'one of them' and 'the second respect'.
2
First argument: classification
The affirmers point out that the negators classify the non-existent. How does this imply subsistence?
3
Second argument: knowledge
The affirmers argue that the negators know and speak of the non-existent. What does that require?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة : الآن صرحتم بالحق حيث ميزتم بين قسم وقسم، فالثبوت عندكم أعم من الوجود، فإن الثبوت يشمل الموجود والمعدوم، فهلا قلتم في المقابل كذلك : وإن المنفي أعم من المعدوم حتى يكون صفة عمومة حالا أو وجها للمنفي ثابتا، كما كان صفة خصوص المعدوم حالا للمعدوم أو وجها ثابتا، فيتحقق أمر ثابت، ويرتفع المقابل العقلي، وإن لم يثبتوا فرقاً بين المنفي والمعدوم، ثم قالوا : كل معدوم شيء ثابت لزمهم أن يقولوا : كل منفي شيء ثابت، فرجع الإلزام عليهم رجوعاً ظاهرًا، وهو رفع التقابل بين النفي والإثبات. قال المثبتون : إذا حققتم الكلام في النفي والإثبات والموجود والمعدوم، وميزتم بين الخصوص والعموم فيه عاد الإلزام عليكم متوجها من وجهين؛ أحدهما : أنكم أثبتم في المعدوم خصوصاً وعموما أيضا حتى قلتم : منه ما هو واجب كالمستحيل، ومنه ما هو جائز كالممكن ومنه ما يستحيل لذاته كالجمع بين المتضادين، ومنه ما يستحيل لغيره كخلاف المعلوم، فهذه التقسيمات قد وردت على المعدوم، فأخذتم المعدومية عامة، وخصصتم بهذه الخصايص، فلولا أن المعدوم شيء ثابت وإلا لما تحقق فيه العموم والخصوص ولما تحقق التميز بين قسم وقسم. والوجه الثاني : أنكم اعترفتم بأن المنفي والمعدوم معلوم، وقد أخبرتم عنه، وتصرفتم بأفكاركم فيه، فما متعلق العلم؟ وما معنى التعلق إذا لم يكن شيئًا ثابتا أصلا؟

?

?

Are you careful not to assume that non-existence implies insignificance in God's sight?

God's Knowledge of Non-Existence in Pre-Eternity
The affirmers refute the claim that knowledge requires existence by invoking God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the affirmers use God's eternal knowledge of the world's non-existence to establish that non-existence is a real object of knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of the world before it existed proves non-existence has a reality.

The negators argued that knowledge must attach to what exists or to an estimation of existence. The affirmers respond by pointing to God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity (azal). This challenges the assumption because in pre-eternity the world did not exist, yet God knew it would exist. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the negator's claim
The negators claim that knowledge attaches only to existence or to an estimation of existence. Note that this would make non-existence unknowable.
3
See how the affirmers counter
The affirmers use the example of God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity. Even though the world did not exist, God knew it. So knowledge does not require existence. Furthermore, estimation of its existence in pre-eternity is impossible for two reasons: (1) estimating something impossible is impossible, and (2) estimation on God's part implies vacillation, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The affirmers conclude that non-existence must be an established thing (shay' thabit) to be an object of knowledge. This establishes that non-existence has a form of reality (thubut) that knowledge can attach to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون: أما قولكم في العلم يتعلق بالوجود أو بتقدير الوجود، فإنه ينتقض بعلم الباري سبحانه بعدم العالم في الأزل، فإنه لا وجود للعالم في الأزل ولا تقدير الوجود من وجهين؛ أحدهما أن تقدير العالم في الأزل محال، والثاني أن التقدير من الباري سبحانه محال فإنه ترديد الفكر بوجود شيء وعدمه، فإن قدر في ذاته فهو محال، وإن فعل فعلاً سماه تقديراً فهو محال في الأزل، ولا بد للعلم من متعلق وهو معلوم محقق، فوجب أن يكون شيئاً ثابتاً. وقولكم: العلم يتعلق بوجود الشيء في وقت وجوده ثم يلزم من ضرورته العلم بعدمه قبل وجوده يلزمكم حصر العلم في معلومات هي موجودات، والموجودات متناهية، فيجب تناهي المعلومات، وأنتم لا تقولون به، ثم تلك اللوازم إن كانت معلومة فهي كالموجودات على السواء في تعلق العلم بها، وإن لم تكن معلومة فقد تناهى العلم والمعلومات.
The affirmers said: As for your statement that knowledge attaches to existence or to an estimation of existence, it is refuted by God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity (azal). For in pre-eternity there is no existence of the world and no estimation of existence from two aspects: one is that estimation of the world in pre-eternity is impossible, and the second is that estimation on the part of God is impossible, for it is a vacillation of thought over the existence of something and its non-existence. If He estimated in His essence, that is impossible; and if He performed an act called estimation, that is impossible in pre-eternity. Yet knowledge must have an object (muta'alliq) which is a known reality (ma'lum muhqaqq), so it is necessary that it be an established thing (shay' thabit). And your statement: knowledge attaches to the existence of a thing at the time of its existence, then necessarily its non-existence becomes known before its existence – this would restrict knowledge to objects that are existents, but existents are finite, so the objects of knowledge would necessarily be finite, which you do not affirm. Then those necessary concomitants (lawazim): if they are known, they are like existents equally in the attachment of knowledge to them; and if they are not known, then knowledge and its objects have come to an end.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the affirmers' (those who affirm the reality of non-existence) refutation of the negators. They make two main points: First, they counter the claim that knowledge requires existence or estimation of existence by citing God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity (azal). In pre-eternity, the world did not exist, and estimation of its existence is impossible because estimation is a mental act that would be impossible for God (since it implies ignorance or deliberation). Yet God had knowledge of the world's future existence, so that knowledge must have attached to something established (thabit) in pre-eternity, namely the non-existence of the world. Second, they point out a consequence of the negators' view: if knowledge only attaches to existents, then the objects of knowledge would be limited to finite existents, making knowledge finite, which is absurd for God. Moreover, the necessary concomitants (like impossibility preceding impossibility etc.) would either be known (and thus treated as existents in that context) or unknown, leading to a halt in knowledge. Thus the negators' position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
علم
وجود
عدم
تعلق
معلوم
ثابت
أزل
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the affirmers claim non-existence is an actual external entity or thing.
Correction: The affirmers hold that non-existence (al-'adam) has a state of reality (thubut) as an object of knowledge, not that it exists externally. It is a conceptual reality, not a physical one.

Checkpoint
What is the main example the affirmers use to refute the claim that knowledge requires existence or its estimation?
A) The knowledge of a future event after its occurrence
B) God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity
C) The knowledge of a creation after its creation
D) The knowledge of an accident after its existence

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity demonstrates that non-existence is a real object of knowledge, establishing a state of reality (thubut) for it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses even non-existence—that which has not yet happened—then He knows every thought you will ever have before you think it. Does this make you more vigilant over your inner life, knowing that nothing escapes His awareness? How will you act on that today?
?
The affirmers argue that God's knowledge does not depend on existence; He knows without needing the object to be present. How does this truth challenge your tendency to feel unknown or unloved when your actions or circumstances seem hidden? Will you let His eternal knowledge of you be enough?
?
If non-existence itself is an established reality in God's knowledge, then there is no gap between when He knows you and when you exist. Before you were born, you were a known reality to Him. Does that not demand a response of gratitude and trust in His plan for your life, even when you feel confused or lost?
